## Page 8

SECTION I BACKGROUND TO THE INVESTIGATIONS by C. Reid Ferring Introduction This report describes the results of a multidisciplinary investigation of cultural resources and past environments in the Upper Trinity River Basin. The study has been conducted by the Institute of Applied Sciences, North Texas State University, and as part of a contract between NTSU and Alan Plummer and Associates. Alan Plummer in return is the prime contractor to the Ft. Worth District U.S. Army Corps of Engineers and has been charged with preparation of portions of the regional resource environmental impact statement for the Upper Trinity Basin. Purpose As defined by the scope of work for this project, the preparation of the regional EIS for the Trinity River will be accomplished within the present study area through completion of several specific work items. These include preparation of an overview of prehistoric and historic culture resources and lastly, a geomorphological study of the study area. These three tasks and their integration into a unified piece of research represent only part of the overall objective of preparing an environmental impact statement. They provide the necessary database for interpreting the age, character, and paleo-environmental context of the cultural resources within the study area. other components of the environmental impact statement were addressed in different parts of this work. For example, another objective of this study was to evaluate the impact of past, present, and anticipated landuse activities on cultural resources sites in the study area. These objectives were addressed through aerial photograph analyses and onsite investigations of landuse activities. Second, a regional environmental impact statement must contain a summary of the previous investigations in the region, summarizing knowledge of prehistory and history and the level to which previous researchers have brought this region into concert with contemporary methods and theories in archaeology. The significance of specific cultural resources sites may be evaluated relative to the status of archaeological research and the state of knowledge concerning archaeological periods within the project area. Thus, previous researct in the project area and in the region surrounding the project area has been explicitly defined as an objective for this research. 1


---

## Page 9

Therefore, the principal purpose of the project is to assist in the formulation of a regional EIS. Specific objectives within that overall goal include the review of previous work, the nature of archaeological resources and historical resources within the study area, the relationships of past depositional environments as contexts for the archaeological resources, and an evaluation of landuse activities, both previous and anticipated. Project Area The area of investigation for this study has been defined on the basis of the 500-year flood elevation (Standard Project Flood) of the Trinity River Basin in Dallas, Tarrant, and Denton counties (Figure 1). This land area covers the majority of the floodplain and some of the low terrace exposures of the Elm Fork, West Fork, and main Trinity River. The geographic bounds within this area are best defined by the portions of the Trinity floodplain on the Elm Fork south from Lewisville Dam and on the West Fork east of the Fourth Street Bridge in Ft. Worth, Texas. The lower end of the project area is defined by the crossing of Interstate Highway 635 over the Trinity Valley in southeast Dallas county. The project area includes areas flanking major tributaries to the Trinity. On the Elm Fork and main channel of the Trinity, tributaries include Indian Creek, Denton Creek below Grapevine Dam, Five Mile Creek, and portions of White Rock Creek in the eastern portion of the project area. On the West Fork, principal tributaries considered include Fossil Creek, Village Creek below Lake Arlington Dam, Lower Bear Creek, and Mountain Creek below the Mountain Creek Lake Dam. The project area covers some 327 square kilometers (80,555 acres) and is situated in the midst of one of the most rapidly growing metropolitan areas in the southwest. Later in this report, the native condition of the area will be described as part of the environmental review. In addition, the nature of the impacts of growth on the development of the Trinity floodplain will be considered under landuse analyses (Appendix A). For the majority of this report, however, the project will be considered from a strictly geological and archaeological perspective: that is, as a major portion of the principal drainage of northcentral Texas, and a place where a complex and hitherto poorly studied record of past human activities and past environments is -contained in sediments deposited by the Trinity and its tributaries. 2

## Page 11

Nature of Previous Investigations Previous investigations in the project area have included archaeological, historical, geologic, and paleontologic studies of the natural history and human history of the upper Trinity basin. Each section in this report will illustrate that both avocational and professional researchers have over the years built a record of archaeology and past environments in the Upper Trinity. Yet, the summed character of these investigations has been shaped by a number of important factors, which will be briefly considered here. Foremost is the relatively incomplete literature concerning past environments and archaeology for this study area. In contrast to intensively investigated regions within the state or in other parts of the U.S., this portion of the Trinity Basin is not well described in the archaeological literature. The specific reasons for this are quite diverse. First, most of the reservoirs that exist in the project area were built before federal laws and/or federal funds were available to launch large-scale archaeological salvage projects. Examples such as Grapevine and Garza-Little Elm are testimony to this pattern. Certainly, federal reservoirs under construction today, for example, Joe Pool or Ray Roberts, have received adequate funding to study and mitigate impacts to archaeological and historical resources under Section 106 of the National Historic Preservation Act. But, by and large, most of the reservoir construction and highway construction in the area was conducted without adequate attention given to the study and/or preservation of cultural resources. In the absence of large-scale federal or state funded projects, the burden for archaeological studies has fallen largely on the shoulders of avocational archaeologists. As will be developed in detail later, the literature of the Trinity Basin shows a continuous contribution from local avocational archaeologists. Their efforts in finding, reporting, and studying archaeological sites is the principal source of information concerning the prehistory of this area. Only limited professional archaeological investigations have been undertaken. These include excavations at the only site that has been intensively studied (41DL234). This site was part of a field school conducted by University of Texas at Arlington. Numerous smaller surveys have been ~onducted by professional archaeologists, either through their universities or as consultants to fulfill small-scale environmental impact statement preparations. Overall, however, there has been no single project nor a combination of smaller projects, which were unified in method a~d theme, that have built a foundation of archaeological 4


---

## Page 12

knowledge concerning the age, character, and diversity of archaeological sites within the project area. Historical investigations in the area are, perhaps, equally as diffuse as those in the prehistoric arena. Local historians, both avocational and professonal, have slowly built a scattered literature concerning the nature of early settlements, the history of growth and development within the region, and the identificaton of specific historically important sites or buildings within the project area. Finding material evidence of historical sites has proven to be a challenging task. And again, the absence of large federally. or state funded projects and the absence of a continuous, concerted effort by historians or groups of historians is a causal factor. Therefore, the historical investigations in this region have been uneven if not eclectic. Geologic and paleoenvironmental investigations differ in character to a certain degree from those of the archaeological and historical ones. For the most part, these have been conducted by professional geologists, paleontologists and/or their students. Yet as will be shown later, these investigations also have been episodic and often quite limited. For example, there has been no doctoral dissertation completed on any portion of the project area which pertains to the Quaternary geology and environments of the Trinity. A number of master's theses have been completed that addressed either specific problems relating to geomorphology or paleontology. A number of maters degrees were granted at southern Methodist University based on preparation of quadrangle geologic maps that cover portions of the project area. Considering that within the Dallas/Ft. Worth area there are several major state schools and private universities, each of which contain departments of geology, the amount of work conducted on the Trinity is surprisingly small. From a historical perspective, most of the work was finished before 1955, and only certain paleontological investigations have been launched from a university base since that time. While the focus of geologists' research since the mid-50s turned increasingly to economic- and petroleum-related aspects of geology, the lack of interest in the Quaternary history of the T~inity and its tributaries is perhaps not so curious. Later in the report, the results of these investigations will be summarized and areas for future investigations will be outlined. A significant record of past environments and diverse geologic contexts for prehistoric archaeological sites is evident in the project area. Yet, this research base is disappearing rapidly in the face of continuing development of the Trinity floodplain. 5


---

## Page 13

Another dimension of p~evious work in the region is the level to which agencies at the federal, state, and local level have acted to fulfill legal obligations to define cultural resources jeopardized by landuse activities. This arena, referred to as cultural Resources Management (CRM), is one of the major considerations in this report in terms of previous investigations. Later, the specific agencies responsible for conducting CRM work in the project area will be identified, and the cumulative result of their surveys and site evaluations will be considered. Unlike a well-defined federal project such as a reservoir or highway construction area, the Trinity is dominated by private lands. Federal law requires environmental impact statements when certain federal monies or federal permits are used for certain activities under the provisions of the National Environmental Policy Act (40 CFR Parts 1500-1508). Therefore, it is evident that a diversity of agencies and a diversity of particular kinds of impacts, ranging from pipelines to housing developments, have been the focus for CRM work in the Trinity Basin. These projects, usually small surveys, have been completed by many local archaeologists. What is lacking in these separate efforts is a matrix of common objectives and common methods. Finding out who worked where has been a major obstacle to the completion of this report. In this regard, the present report perhaps represents an opportunity to establish some regional perspectives of the cultural resources in the project area and some regionally relevant questions and methods to guide future investigations. 6


---

## Page 14

SECTION II PROJECT OVERVIEW by C. Reid Ferring Objectives This project has focussed on the archaeological resources within a major part of The Upper Trinity Basin in Texas. Unlike many cultural resources management (CRM) investigations, this is not the final stage of an intensive archaeological study of a well-defined project area such as a reservoir. Rather, as the scope of work for the project has clearly indicated, this is a review of an area that has received sporadic and uneven archaeological attention over the last four decades. This project differs from other CRM projects in that the potential impacts and the nature of landuse activities in the project area are not guided by a single well-funded federal landuse activity, but rather by the diverse projects launched from the private sector, which continue to emerge along the Trinity River and its major tributaries. This last "central frontier" for commercial development within the Dallas/Ft. Worth metroplex has long been the subject of gravel and sand excavations and limited commercial development. Today, real estate prices in this region have escalated to the point that potential risks from flooding and the necessary costs to reclaim land formerly quarried for construction materials have overtaken the situation, and development now proceeds at a staggering pace. An intensive pedestrian survey of the entire project area would be exorbitantly expensive. The government's strategy in order to identify and potentially mitigate adverse impacts to cultural resources within the project area is to develop a comprehensive environmental impact statement that allows project-by-project evaluation of known and potential cultural resources sites. This report contains the most up-to-date summary of the known archaeological resources within the project area and the information which at present can best be used to predict sites in unsurveyed portions of the project area. Both theoretical and regional perspectives are necessary in order to define the status of archaeological and historical knowledge in the region. They are also necessary in order to evaluate the potential for past and ongoing investigations to make substantial contributions to the current theoretical 7


---

## Page 15

development of the discipline of archaeology. While theoretical interests, as well as research methods and techniques, evolve according to prevailing interests and even "fads," they do provide some baseline from which to launch research questions in an area such as the Upper Trinity Basin. Many of these questions are fundamental to the discipline of anthropological archaeology. They consider broad issues of human history such as adaptations to changing environments or culture change as a result of diffusion or migration. These kinds of broad issues must be suited to local culture histories and to records of local archaeological research. Theoretical issues at the discipline level, regional issues concerning culture history and the record of regional investigations are of fundamental significance to cultural resources management. The nexus between these archaeological issues on the one hand and the somewhat legal dimensions of cultural resources management on the other is the concept of significance. Difficult to cope with in many specific situations, the issue of significance in cultural resources management revolves around a decision made by both archaeologists and government officials concerning the relative merit of a cultural resources site. Potential impacts to cultural resources as a result of landuse activities subject to federal environmental laws can be quite easy to define'in physical terms; that is, the complete physical destruction of an archaeological site and its contained artifacts and features may be easily defined by reference to the engineers' plans for the project. what is to be done about such an impact is quite another question and hinges upon the consensus of opinion concerning the importance of the site and whether or not such federal regulations as 33 CFR 325 are applicable. Significance as defined by National Register criteria (36 CFR 900) includes the general notions of whether a site or building contains information that can be regarded as important to science or history. As mentioned earlier, what is important to science and what is also important to history are subject to change as an information base is accumulated within a region or as theoretical and methodological changes impact a discipline. It is incumbent on us as participants in the process of formulating an environmental impact statement for the known and predicted cultural resources within the project area to make our statement concerning which theoretical perspectives and what levels of regional archaeological research are pertinent to defining the potential significance of sites within the project area. This can only serve as a working tool for subsequent efforts by cultural resources managers. They will be charged with making site-specific 'and area-specific determinations with regards to specific measures taken to locate, evaluate, and 8


---

## Page 16

preserve/mitigate cultural resources that are threatened by development activities. A major part of our research strategy therefore is to develop a theoretical framework reflecting both individual research interests and a more objective assessment of comtemporary archaeological theory. This theoretical framework is the source for 'specific research questions that may be used to identify broad research methodologies in the project area as well as specific methodologies and techniques at specific cultural resources sites. Secondly, this report will contain the necessary regional perspectives mentioned above. Regional perspectives address both the apparent records of culture history in the region in terms of the nature of culture groups, their settlement patterns, their activities, and their apparent changes through time. This record of culture history provides a framework from which to evaluate specific sites. It facilitates identifying periods of time or cultural units that are poorly known or poorly documented by research in the project area. Additionally, the regional perspective identifies the kinds of methods and techniques that have been used in the region and evaluates these against contemporary standards for work in archaeology. In essence, the regional perspective establishes the status of archaeological knowledge in a region as a result of the cumulative archaeological efforts by professional and avocational researchers. The combination of a disciplinary perspective expressed in terms of archaeological theory and a regional perspective expressed in terms of archaeological knowledge provide a major portion of the information necessary to evaluate specific sites in terms of their significance. Methods Although each of the major sections of this report provides a more detailed description of the methods used for specific tasks, a general statement concerning our overall methods is warranted here. This project has the character of something between a literature review and an archaeological reconnaissance. No intensive on-the-ground surveys have been conducted. Yet, site-specific visits were formally included in the project. Therefore, our methods of data collection have been largely those of a literature review but were supplemented by some data collection methods that are not typical of literature reviews. These include the site visits just mentioned and more intensive geologic investigations. A geologic reconnaissance of the project area has been conducted, and a number of stratigraphic/soils profiles have been described to supplement available data concerning the geology of the Trinity Basin 9


---

## Page 17

pertinent to archaeological investigation. An additional difference between this study and a literature review has been a comprehensive analysis of landuse activities based on maps, aerial photographs, and on-the-ground inspections. These efforts have been made in order to identify the kinds of landuse activities in the project area and their potential impacts on known and predicted cultural resources sites. A number of informant interviews have been undertaken as part of this project. People in the region who have knowledge concerning the prehistory and geology of the project area have been contac.ted and interviewed. They have provided a wealth of information concerning locations of previously undocumented archaeological sites. They have provided background information on the kinds of research that have been conducted in this region in the last few decades, and they have provided valuable perspectives on the important issues surrounding the prehistory and history of this area-issues which are important in developing research strategies that may be implemented in the future. All of these tasks have resulted in "data" ranging from a collection of over 90 site survey forms to maps describing landuse activities based on air photo analysis. These forms of data provided the basis for our summary of archaeology and history in the project area and our recommendations concerning the nature of archaeological resources relative to present and future landuse activities. cultural Resources Management Issues As mentioned above, the Trinity River Project is significantly different in character than most cultural resources investigations. The basic difference is that in this case the federal government is charged with assessing the development of some floodplain and wetlands areas by private developers under Section 404 of the Clean Water Act, Section 10 of the River and Harbor Act, and other applicable federals laws. Immediately, this scenario invokes differences from projects that are planned and funded by the federal government. First, there is no single comprehensive development plan such as surrounds reservoir construction or highway construction. This normal "plan" involves detailed engineering objectives and relatively clear cut landuse activities. These immediately translate into quite clear impacts or potential impacts on archaeological resources. The Trinity floodplain today is a prime gravel mining area and the subject of urban development including residential, industrial, and commercial building. Given the present status of these developments, it is clear that they will range from small, individually funded projects to projects impacting hundreds of 10


---

## Page 18

acres. The latter may involve the construction of new lakes, levees, roads, and structures with deep foundations. Many of these potential projects must be accomplished under a permit from the u.s. Army Corps of Engineers when it is determined that one is required. The nature of these developments and the relationship between the developers and the U.S Government is expected to vary with each permit application. Each request for a construction permit on the floodplain will become a new case in which the developer must reckon with the potential impacts of his project on the natural and cultural environment. It is expected that each developer will view his project quite separately from all the others. It is expected, therefore, that it will be difficult to build a sense of cumulative impacts as the Trinity floodplain is developed. It is imperative that each case involving potential impacts or even quite well-defined probable impacts to cultural resources be handled in a way that is fair to the individual developer and at the same time addresses the social, scientific, and educational significance of a specific cultural resources site. Despite the fact that the project area is under intensive development with private monies and that this expansive growth is a vital part of the local economy, the spirit and intent of the laws that cover our environmental and cultural resources must be addressed seriously. In Texas a number of uevelopers have already initiated, at their own expense, exemplary progFams that evaluate and in many cases mitigate the potential impacts of their private developments on private lands to archaeological and geological resources. This "best case" scenario will hopefully be expressed in the Dallas/Ft. Worth area. Yet there are practical limits to such a system of voluntary compliance. We fully recognize that a small private developer may find mitigation costs to be incompatible with his planned development budget. How a demonstrably significant site threatened by a small-scale development is to be adequately studied and/or protected is a serious issue that challenges both the government and private sector. We offer no easy solutions to these potential problems in this report. Rather, we offer what we feel we can do best. That is, to provide a reasonable and considered opinion concerning the scientific, historical, educational, and social significance of the cultural resources sites ·that are known or that we can predict as being present in the project area. This in fact is an overview that can assist in formulating a balanced appraisal of significant archaeological sites. Only with such an information base can a program that combines preservation, avoidance, and when clearly necessary, mitigative efforts be formulated. 11


---

## Page 19

SECTION III THE UPPER TRINITY RIVER BASIN by Bonnie C. Yates and C. Reid Ferring Introduction Located in northcentral Texas, the upper portion of the Trinity River has its headwaters in Grayson, Montague, and Archer counties, forming three major tributaries that conjoin downstream from Dallas. Although other large creeks feed these tributaries (e.g., Clear Fork, Denton Creek, and White Rock Creek), it was the confluence of the Elm Fork and West Fork just above the East Fork that gave the river its name. The Spanish explorer Alonzo de Leon (ca. 1690) named the river La Santisima Trinidad (Most Holy Trinity) after its three major tributaries. From here, the river flows in a northwest to southeast direction, reaching the Gulf of Mexico at Anahuac and draining 17,969 square miles in its total length of 550 river miles. The Upper Trinity Basin is the northernmost portion of the river's watershed (Figure 3.1). The Trinity, between the Brazos and the Red, is a second-class river, developed on upland stretches of plain that separate the stream valleys of the two older rivers [Hill 1901]. It is identified as Zone 1 by the Texas Dept. of Water Resources for planning purposes [TDWR 1983]. In addition to the tributaries mentioned above, Mountain Creek, and Village Creek are included in TDWR's Zone 1, which constitutes more than half of the total area of the watershed and encompasses 23 counties. Elevation at the headwaters (Archer County, TX) is 1200 feet AMSL down to around 420 AMSL in the Dallas/Ft. Worth area. Perennial flow is limited to the main channel and parts of the larger tributaries; otherwise, most of the creeks are considered intermittent to ephemeral [USDA-SCS 1979J. Narrow stream channels and rolling topography promote flash flooding during storm events in areas with rapid surface runoff. These flash floods occur frequently during the rainy seasons in the small secondary tributaries and upper reaches of larger tributaries but are generally of short duration. Extensive urbanization has aggravated flooding potential in the metropolitan areas as a result of alteration of drainage patterns [TDWR 1985J. 12

## Page 21

· As part of cultural resources investigation~ in the area, the natural environment is included in reports such as those by Sciscenti et al. [1971], Coster et al. [1972], Lynott [1977], Richner and Bagot [1978], and Bennett et al. [1981]. These comprehensive investigations of archaeological resources in all or large areas of the Trinity watershed likewise contain valuable information on the environmental setting. For example, contributions of J.E. Ubelaker and W.F. Mahler [Sciscenti et al. 1971] cover the zoological and botanical resources, respectively, in great detail for the entire stretch of the Trinity. The survey of environmental and cultural resources directed by J. E. Coster of Stephen F. Austin State University [1972] offers in depth reports on archaeology, geology, biology, ichthyology, ornithology, mammalogy, and forest hydrology for most of the watershed. A section in Mark Lynott's dissertation (SMU) [1977] summarizes the natural environment of most of the river basins in northcentral Texas·. Shaw's environmental overview of the Trinity Basin [in Richner and Bagot 1978] deals with channel variability and lithic resources as well as the major vegetation zones of the entire basin, even though the archaeological reconnaissance concerns primarily sites in the Middle Trinity Basin. The report by Environment Consultants, Inc. [Bennett et al. 1981] is the most recent and provides an abbreviated but adequate description of the natural setting in the Dallas Floodway Extension, which comprises the lower third of the present study area. without replicating their findings in total, the following descriptions have been extracted primarily from these volumes in addition to other sources. Environmental Setting of the Study Area The land between the 96th and 99th meridians in North Texas has been the subject of various environmental assessment. Hill [1887, 1901] discussed the differences of the timbered belts and interspersed prairies of the region as "...unique, without analogy or counterpart. In topographic, economic, and cultural aspects, it is a distinct geographic region" [1901:62]. From a continental view, Fenneman [1938] places the area in his West Gulf Coastal Plain physiographic province. Dyksterhuis [1946, 1948] considers the vegetation as part of the midcontinental grasslands. Some researchers consider the entire Trinity Basin as part of the physiographic province known as the Coastal Plain [Stephenson 1949a; Mertes et al. 1972; Bennett et al. 1981]; others, however, place the northern reaches in the lowlands of the Great Plains province [Hays et al. 1972; USACE 1978]. In agreement with the latter, we consider the Upper Trinity as part of the southern Plains. 15


---

## Page 22

In short, the region is an immense ecozone in which the flora, and hence the fauna, are controlled by various edaphic and climatic factors that come together there. As a transitional area, therefore, the Upper Trinity River Basin is difficult to succinctly categorize. The interdigitation of forest belts and grasslands, along with their geologic underpinnings, has been the object of research, if only descriptive, since the mid-1800s [See Hill 1887; Dyksterhuis 1946]. The geology and paleoenvironment and cultural development of the study area are discussed in detail elsewhere in this report; modern climate, vegetation, and fauna are treated in this section. Climate. The project area has hot, humid summers; rainy fall seasons; cool, sometimes icy winters; and windy, rainy springtimes. Officially, temperature records for the area indicate an average mean temperature of 65 F, average maximum daily temperature of 77 F, average minimum daily temperature of 56 F, with maximum record of 114 F (June 1980) and a minimum record of -1 F (January 1985): 1938-77 1931-69 D!FW Denton Annual Avg. Daily Temp. Max. 76.1 F 77.5 F Min. 55.2 F 52.9 F Annual Avg. precip. 32.10 inches 31.99 inches Monthly Avg. 2.60 2.67 Rainy Months Apr. 3.97 Apr. 3.81 May 4.57 May 4.86 Avg. Total Snowfall 2.90 1.90 [Dyksterhuis 1946; Ford and Pauls 1980; Ressel 1981] Precipitation patterns are irregular, trending to a cycle of flood and drought [Brown 1979]. For example, the drought of the early 1950s was broken by 3 months of sporadic heavy rain totalling 28.8 inches; more recently, the record heat wave of 1980 was followed by record flooding (30 inches in 1 month in Pilot Point) the next spring. These incidents of extremes are accompanied by climatological side effects: dry summers are usually the hottest, exceeding 100 F for weeks on end; early spring torrents stimulate budding only to be thwarted by late freezes. 16


---

## Page 23

This moisture pattern is a general characteristic of the plains area. wedel [1941:6-7; 1961:32] has shown that precipitation on the plains is quite complex, in terms of amounts annually, amounts on a single occasion, occasions annually, and areas of receipt. Moreover, the random, unpredictable periods of debilitating dryness have considerable antiquity. [Marmaduke 1975] Vegetation. The study area consists of three vegetation zones from east to west: the Blackland Prairie, which dictates the soils and plants characteristic of most of Dallas County; the Eastern Cross Timbers, which includes portions of Denton and Tarrant counties in the project area; and the Grand Prairie, upon which some sites in eastern Tarrant County are found. The slightly tilted plain of the Blackland Prairie slopes imperceptibly toward the Gulf Coast and is dotted with low rounded hills. Early European settlers were dependent on cisterns and ponds for water because there are few running waterways in this fertile region [Hill 1901]. The decomposition of grasses produces humic acid that acts upon the underlying marls and chalks and forms the black calcareous clay soils from which the prairie gets its name [Hill 1901]. High fertility of these normally water absorptive soils has attracted increased cultivation and hence erosion has become more pronounced. Urbanovsky [1972:27] states, "... the Blackland Prairie exhibits the highest average rate of erosion in the Trinity River watershed. Severe erosion in cultivated areas is the major source of sediment. In many of the smaller watersheds, sediment deposition on densely vegetated streambanks has increased flood frequencies as much as 50% through reduction of channel capacities." According to Nixon and Willett [1974], the natural condition of the prairie had been dominated by grasses such as little blue stem, big bluestem, sideoats grama, switchgrass, and Indiangrass. Other grasses include hairy grama, Texas grama, buffalograss, switchgrass, dallisgrass, dropseed, smutgrass, and wintergrass. Along the stream courses, overstory species include hackberry, oaks, elms, cottonwood, ash, and willow; underbrush consists of grapes, berries, peppervine, honeysuckle, hawthorne, and trumpetvine; sedges, wildrye, and paspalum are also present in wet areas, whereas, the prairie grasses occupy drier areas [Shaw 1978J. 17


---

## Page 24

Along the western border of the Blackland Prairie, the soil becomes increasingly sandy as the substructure grades into the Eastern Cross Timbers. The relief is that of a greatly dissected dip plain, which is almost level in the east and broken into low hills in the west [Hill 1901]. Some authorities lump the Eastern and Western Cross Timbers and Grand Prairie into a single vegetative zone called Cross Timbers and Prairies, not wishing to distinguish either type of vegetation as the ultimate climax vegetation [Urbanovsky 1972; Gould 1975]. It is quite possible that the climax is a mixture, with rolling to hilly, deeply dissected terrain having rapid surface drainage and a wide variety of soil types and vegetation cover [Urbanovsky 1972]. Dyksterhuis [1948] argues that the Cross Timbers specifically is a true woodland representing an extension of the East Texas Austroriparian and dependent on the aquiferous Woodbine Sands, which permit greater penetration of moisture than the clayey soils of the surrounding prairies. Early travelers through the area remarked on the striking contrast of the grasslands and almost impenetrable forests that skirt them: Occupying east and west a belt of one hundred miles in width, with about equal quantities of prairie and timber, intersected by numerous clear, fresh streams and countless springs, with a gently undulating surface of prairie and oak openings, the most charming views, as of a country in the highest state of cultivation, and you are startled at the summit of each swell of the prairie with a prospect of groves, parks, and forests, with intervening plains of luxuriant grass. [Pope 1854] Where brush merges into woodlands, the oaks dominate (mainly postoak, blackjack oak, live oak, shinnery oak), and the understory consists of big and little bluestem, lovegrass, switchgrass, Indiangrass, and many kinds of forbs and legumes. In the bottoms, floodplain trees in the Cross Timbers consist of elms, ash, pecan, cottonwood, sycamore, black willow, and soapberry; understory vegetation includes coral berry, greenbriar, and various frutescents such as haws, hog plum, and dewberries. The Grand Prairie is located south and west of the Eastern Cross Timbers. It gently slopes into steeply rolling hills farther south of the project area and consists of shallow, 18


---

## Page 25

stone-laden soils that support medium to tall grass vegetation. Almost half of the original prairie still exists and is scattered as tame pasture or meadow throughout the area [Urbanovsky 1972]. The soil is more shallow, and the decreased rainfall makes the land less suitable for agriculture than the Blackland Prairies. According to Hill [1901:72], The chief difference between the two regions is that the Grand Prairie is established upon firm, persistent bands of limestones, which are harder than the underlying clay substructure of the Black Prairie region, and which, under erosion, result in more extensive stratum plains and more angular cliffs and slopes. The virgin tall and mid grass pra1r1e between the timber belts has been described as luxuriant, lush, and indescribably beautiful, especially in its abundance of wildflowers [Greer 1935]. After 1860, the dominant influence on the vegetation was an increased intensity of grazing cattle, and with the peak stocking rates of 1890, the vegetal composition and productive capacities deteriorated immensely [Dyksterhuis 1946]. In relict areas of the Ft. Worth Prairie, a subsection of the Grand Prairie in our study area, Dyksterhuis [1946] reports that little bluestem (Andropogon scoparius) was the principal climax dominant. other species include sideoats and hairy grama, wintergrass, Indiangrass, dropseed, and perennial three awns. A recapitulation of the data on relict perennial grasses, based upon stature, indicates coverage by mid grasses of 77.37 percent while coverage by tall, and short grasses is 7.52 and 2.50 percent, respectively. [Dyksterhuis 1946:14] To summarize, Tables 3.1 and 3.2 contain data from by the Dallas Museum of Natural History and Texas A&M University, respectively, compiled by Mertes et al. [1972] and reproduced here for reference. Nixon and Willett [1974J intensively sampled various plant communities along the West Fork and main stem below Dallas as well as the rest of the Basin. Shaw's [1978] chapter on the biota of the Trinity River Basin provides and excellent overview of biological research conducted in the area. The final EIS for floodwater retarding structures in the watershed [USDA-SCS 1979J adds information regarding critical habitats and threatened plants, stating that there are no threatened nor 19

## Page 28

endangered plant species or critical habitats in the watershed; however, five plants have been identified as proposed for listing, and at least eight species on the Rare Plant study Center list may have distributions into the project area (Table 3.3). Olin McCormick et al. [1975; Gilmore and McCormick 1982] has compiled a compendium of aboriginal uses for 56 plants collected from the project area; he also reports approximately 30 different types of mushrooms or fungi found in his summer collection. The USDA report also notes the presence of Maximilian sunflower, engelmanndaisy, gayfeather, sundrop, and prairieclover as sources of color and variety to the diets of foraging animals and birds. Fauna. The metroplex lies within Blair's [1950] Texan biotic province (Figure 3.2). Blair's provinces generally consist of discrete geographic areas within which distinct plant and animal communities exist; however, the Texan province is more like a giant ecotone where species reaching their eastern and western extremes overlap. It is a rich and diverse area with 49 species of mammals, 39 species of reptiles, 20 species of amphibians, and 239 species of birds have been listed from the Tennessee Colony study area alone [Coster et al. 1972]. Species diversity is relatively low in the Dallas/Ft. Worth area as a result of habitat reduction and modification. Cosmopolitan bird species, such as sparrows, grackles, blackbirds, starlings, robins, cardinals, and bluejays are fairly abundant. In the floodplains, coyotes, raccoons, squirrels, rabbits, skunks, opossums, and armadillos may still be found. Aquatic faunas are greatly affected by water pollution, and studies reported in the General Design Memo for the Trinity River Project [USACE 1978] cite the presence of pollution-tolerant species only: carp, gar, shad, mosquitofish, red shiner, and bullhead catfish; no game fish were recorded. Some additional fishes currently known in the study area are listed in Table 3.4; a list of fishes from the Clear Fork tributary west of Ft. Worth is included as representative of Trinity fishes from less polluted waters. Invertebrates and zooplankters in the Upper Trinity are likewise pollution-tolerant species: pulmonate gastropods, chironomidae, and tubificidae among others. Terrestrial invertebrates include representatives of most major groups, including the almost ubiquitous presence of the introduced Argentine fire ant (Iridomyrex humilis), a health factor to consider in any hand-excavation activity, and an agent of bioturbation in sediment profiles. Historically, the region' was inhabited by bison, black bear, mountain lion, gray wolf, pronghorn antelope, passenger pigeon, wild turkey, and Carolina parakeet [USACE 1978; Schmidly 1983]. The conversion of native woodlands and prairies to areas of 22

## Page 33

cultivation and unrestricted hunting pressures have pushed these species completely out of northcentral Texas, if not to the verge of extinction. "Like that of the passenger pigeon, the bison's demise was relatively sudden. commercial hunters took such a heavy toll in North Texas that after 1880, except for stragglers, only captive animals remained" [Doughty 1983:64]. Nevertheless, habitat for some species has actually been upgraded by improved pastures and range practices: quail, dove, and rabbits are common small animals still hunted in the area at times. Tables 3.5, 3.6, and 3.7 list additional mammals, birds, and herptiles found in the Upper Trinity River Basin. Lynott [1977:37] reports that plant and animal resources were not particularly numerous in the uplands of the native prairie and Cross Timbers; the bottomlands supported a greater variety of resources, including the all-important deer, raccoon, opossum, rabbit, squirrel, and wild turkey. Shaw [1978:30] remarks that in the Blackland Prairie "...most larger food-yielding game species would have concentrated in greater numbers within the bottomland forest zone. This would have been especially true during fall and winter when acorns began to be dispersed and when a number of fruits first ripen." Seasonal resources include migratory birds, especially waterfowl which feed along the margins of ponds, spawning fishes, most amphibians and reptiles, which are more active in warmer seasons, vegetable materials (berries, fruits, and nuts), and insects [Marmaduke 1975; McCormick et al. 1975]. The tall and mid grass prairies probably did not support large herds of bison or pronghorn which prefer short grass prairies found farther west. Hornaday [1887:426] lists several grasses consumed by bison, but the only one cited by Dyksterhuis [1946] as remaining in a sampled relict prairie (Grand Prairie) was Andropogon scoparius, which would have at least supported straggling herds. Neither bison nor pronghorn have been recovered from archaeological sites in the project area in appreciable numbers; deer is by far the staple meat protein source. Because of such sparse and poorly dated evidence for bison here, it is impractical to assess the temporal presence / absence scheme of Dillehay [1974] with regard to the study area. Many of the sites described in the present study report faunal remains (Table 5.3); with the exception of isolated deer, bison, and turtle remains, the vast majority are remains of shellfish. Read [1954] records 30 species of unionids in Dallas County and credits the wide variety of stream bottoms to this diversity. "Shellfish could be collected at low water periods from pools in the creekbeds or streams themselves" [Lynott 1977:36]. The identity of the shellfish remains from archaeological sites is unknown at this time, but mussel 27

SECTION IV LATE QUATERNARY GEOLOGY AND ENVIRONMENTS OF THE UPPER TRINITY BASIN by C. Reid Ferring Introduction The scope of work for this project outlined a program of geologic study including literature reviews, informant interviews, as well as field work that would address the last 20,000 years of geologic history for the Upper Trinity Basin. This time frame was established because it is within this range that all probable archaeological occurrences would fit. Sediments containing archaeological sites by their very nature offer the potential to reconstruct the depositional environments that prevailed at the time of occupations. Post-depositional and post-occupational histories of weathering and disturbance can selectively alter archaeological records within and between sites. Additionally, sediments contain other forms of primary evidence concerning past environments including vertebrate faunas, invertebrate faunas, plant macrofossils, and pollen. The purely archaeological reasons for establishing the geologic context of archaeological sites in the Trinity Basin are therefore quite clear. Another compelling reason to conduct this study, however, involves site prediction. In an area that has not been the subject of intensive archaeological surveys, it is important to establish, when possible, the age of sediments and land forms within a project area as part of the site prediction process [Gardner and Donahue 1985]. For the above reasons, a review of previous work concerning the geologic and environmental history of the Upper Trinity Basin has been conducted. In addition, a field reconnaissance of terraces and exposed stratigraphic cuts was implemented during the early stages of the project. Interviews were conducted with several local researchers knowledgeable about the Trinity's geologic history, and subsequently, detailed section descriptions from several terrace exposures in the project area were completed. By any means, this entire effort must be viewed as a reconnaissance study. The Trinity and its terraces clearly contain a rich and important record of past environments and at 32


---

## Page 38

the same time, constitute the context for archaeological sites in the basin. However, the review of previous work, as well as the results of our own studies, suggests what was perhaps obvious from the onset: this geologic record is complex. It must be studied from an interdisciplinary point of view and with a team of researchers qualified to address each of the several dimensions of this complex geological record. Therefore, while certain contributions to the geologic study of the Trinity's Late Quaternary history have been made by this project, perhaps our most important conclusion has been reached in terms of defining the kinds of work that need to be done in the future in order to resolve the details of stratigraphy, geochronology, and past environments. The contexts for archaeological sites extend beyond the perimeters of artifact distributions themselves. The terraces, sediments, soils, and paleontologic materials contained within these constitute an important resource in themselves, a resource which may be used to reconstruct past environments and past landscapes. From such a vantage point, prehistoric occupations may be best interpreted. This geologic resource, including sediments containing artifacts and those which do not, is disappearing rapidly in the face of floodplain and terrace development. Gravel and sand quarrying activities spanning most of this century have taken their toll on archaeological sites and geologic records. Concurrently, they have provided important exposures and allowed geologists and paleontologists the opportunity to see stratigraphic cuts that would be otherwise unavailable for study. As gravel and sand resources have been depleted, however, we now see that those areas, as well as unquarried portions of the floodplain, are now being used for commercial and residential development. It is almost possible to say that the time to conduct detailed mapping of terrace surfaces is past. It is probably equally safe to predict that within the next decade serious geologic studies within the project area will be impossible. There simply will not be opportunities to investigate surficial or buried deposits. It is therefore important to recognize the sediments and their contained evidence for past environments and past occupations as resources in themselves, resources which need to be preserved and/or studied prior to their perhaps inevitable obliteration. This section of the Trinity Basin report is divided into several sections reflecting the structure of our inquiry. First, the history of investigations is reviewed in order to establish the nature of geologic and paleontologic studies of the Trinity Basin sediments. Secondly, the results of all previous studies will be summarized in terms of fundamental geologic problem areas 33


---

## Page 39

including geomorphology, stratigraphy, and geochronology. Next, the results of our own study will be described and related to the summed evidence of previous investigations for a geologic history of the last ca. 20,000 years in the Trinity Basin. Lastly, conclusions will be presented that describe the present status of knowledge concerning Trinity's Late Quaternary history and the lines of inquiry that we feel should be followed with respect to future investigations. As stated above, the ultimate objective of these investigations is not purely geologic or environmental in focus, but rather is intertwined with the archaeological objectives of this entire project. The Late Quaternary geology of the Trinity is viewed here as a part of the archaeological record. It is in fact the context for evidence of past occupations and also the context for independent evidence concerning past environments. Within these contexts, archaeological sites may be evaluated and compared in the process of reconstructing the culture history and cultural ecology of the Upper Trinity Basin. History of Previous Investigations In a land with little water and with as intriguing a natural history as the state of Texas presents, it is truly remarkable how little attention has been paid to the geologic investigation of her rivers. The Trinity is no exception in this respect. It has been studied more than some and less than others, but in the final analysis, the geologic history of the Trinity is very poorly known. Perhaps the reason lies in the oil wealth of the state which has attracted geologists away from historical geology and Quaternary geology to more lucrative fields. Regardless, the last 85 years have yielded a scant page full of names indicating research interest in the Upper Trinity's geologic history. The vast majority of these investigations have been conducted by faculty or students from Southern Methodist University. others include the venerable Robert T. Hill, who touched practically every dimension of Texas geology, some nonlocal researchers such as Stovall and McAnulty [1950J, and W.W. Crook and R.K. Harris, local avocationals, who conducted notable investigations. Together this truly small group of individuals over a period of some eight decades have accumulated a literature that is accommodated easily by a small briefcase. This is not a derogatory appraisal, however, for single quadrangle maps [which were produced in abundance by SMU masters students during one period] represent true geologic sweat. These maps are worth numbers of articles or monographs. Nonetheless, this literature betrays a comple~, fascinating and potentially enlightening ~eologic history that can and should serve as a focus for research lnterests in the future. 34


---

## Page 40

Practically all geologic stories in Texas begin with Robert Hill. One of the products of his efforts in the late nineteenth century was the tome entitled "Geography and Geology of the Black and Grand Prairies and Cross Timbers, Texas" [Hill 1901], with detailed description of the Cretaceous formations and special reference to artesian waters. This work represented the culmination of some 18 years' investigation of the prairies of central and northcentral Texas and Oklahoma by Hill and his students. It is a remarkable document, fully readable and rich with geologic, geographic, and environmental information. The geologic map Hill provides is still one of the most intriguing in the history of Texas geology, particularly his interpretation of structural elements along the Red River Basin. It provides some of the earliest descriptions of the rivers draining Northcentral Texas, relating them to bedrock morphology, climate, soils, and vegetation. Towards the end of World War I, county geologies for this region were being published by the Bureau of Economic Geology at the University of Texas at Austin. Most of these make scant reference to Quaternary deposits, and most of those references in turn relate primarily to the economic potential of sands and gravel contained in river terraces. The inkling of a more than passing interest in these young sediments is found in Shuler's [1918] geology of Dallas County. After defining the economic significance of gravel and sand deposits in the county, Shuler goes on to indicate, by photographs and descriptions, the interesting sedimentary records of these rivers, commenting on faunas within the sediments and alluding to the potential to define the Cenozoic history of these streams. Shuler was later to continue this interest and to seed his own interests in students. Very shortly after Shuler published his geology of Dallas County, he urged Lull [1921] to publish faunas that had been found in sand pits on the south side of Dallas. Lull's paleontologic description of large vertebrate faunas from that locality is one of the earliest in the state and the first for the Trinity River. This article was to become the first of a continuing series of investigations into the well-preserved vertebrate and invertebrate faunas contained within the terraces overlooking the Trinity Floodplain. Not long after in 1923, Shuler again published an important discovery, the Lagow Sand Pit. This sand quarry, which had been described in his 1918 geology, is located near modern Fair Park in southern Dallas. The discovery of a human skeleton by excavation crews at that locality exceeded great interest in the possibility of finding human remains associated with extinct 35


---

## Page 41

Pleistocene faunas. Eleven years later shuler [1934] published again on fossil elephants in the Trinity alluvial deposits. This time his audience was archaeological, and Shuler aptly noted the possibility of discovering artifacts in association with probiscidean remains in Dallas County. In this respect, Shuler is one of the first archaeological geologists in the history of Texas archaeology, advising archaeologists on what contexts the finds might occur in and what to do when such finds might arise. In the following year, shuler [1935] provided the first earnest publication concerning the Quaternary geology of the Trinity. Shuler describes the intent of his paper in its abstract as one that "presents the larger facts concerning the terraces of the Trinity River in Dallas County and points out some of the more important problems to be solved in future studies" [Shuler 1935:44]. Shuler indeed began the study of the Trinity with this paper. He describes basic geomorphic processes in terms of terrace deposition and its relationship to headwards cutting by streams. He describes the geomorphology of the principle terraces evident in the vicinity of Dallas, compares them to terraces of other Texas rivers, and summarizes the work of Carter [1924] on the terrace soils. The latter study, incidently, is the last major investigation other than county soil survey preparation of terrace soils along the Trinity. In the same issue of Field and Laboratory [the now out-of-print publication by Southern Methodist University that contains a large portion of the published information on the Trinity], one of Shuler's students, Kelsey, provided a detailed description of the Marsalis terrace in the vicinity of Dallas. The ~ast pre-war effort devoted to the study of Trinity terraces was an important one by Patillo [1940] who described the terraces in the Carrollton area. The next decade and a half of research on the Trinity follows a pattern perhaps typical of most geology programs in the U.S. The war years were dominated by interests focusing on petroleum and mineral exploration to serve the war effort. The post-war years were devoted to offering basic undergraduate educations to returning veterans, and in the succeeding few years, the products of graduate studies in geology are evident. Graduate students in geology, needed in abundance to serve the post-war needs for oil exploration, were apparently graduated in substantial numbers by Southern Methodist University. Between 1948 and 1953, many of the quadrangle maps (Figures 4.1, 4.2) in Dallas County were prepared by graduate students (Table 4.1). Because of the interest in Quaternary geology by some of the faculty, those students assigned to map areas containing Trinity terraces dealt with these in terms of their geomorphology and potential correlations. They were assisted by independent researchers in the region. For instance, Dodge [1952], whose primary objective 36

## Page 45

was to describe Woodbine stratigraphy near Arlington, provided some mapping information concerning West Fork terrace deposits. An important study downstream by Stovall and McAnulty [1950] described three terraces and their Pleistocene faunas. Lastly, Taggart's [1953] synthesis, which resulted in a geologic map of Dallas County, explicitly addressed the terrace system flanking the Trinity and summarized previous work by SMU students and others (Figure 4.3). Taggart's work built upon the cumulative efforts of many others and remains the most comprehensive geomorphological summary of the Trinity River terraces. It has not been succeeded by any detailed geomorphic investigations, the main reason probably being that Taggart concluded that the major problems yet to be resolved concerning the Trinity River's alluvial history required detailed stratigraphic, soils, and geochronological studies. His work was in effect a terminus for geomorphic attempts to resolve the Trinity's depositional history. The next major turn in Trinity studies was the publication in 1957 of the discovery of a Pleistocene fauna at the Lewisville Site [Crook and Harris 1957]. As a result of some 5 years of both avocational and professional collection of vertebrate and invertebrate fossils from the borrow pit located near the juncture of Hickory Creek and the Elm Fork of the Trinity, this investigation·culminated in the discovery of several artifacts including a Clovis spear point and several hearth-like features that were contained in Pleistocene deposits at that locality. This site made contributions not only to the paleontologic study of the Trinity, but also resulted in new stratigraphic descriptions. Not the least of the results of this work was the beginning of a continuing controversy concerning the age and context of the clovis spear point. It should be added, however, that publications by Crook and Harris on Lewisville are part of many contributions this team made to Trinity River archaeology and geology. One subsequent publication by Crook [1961J reviewed again the terrace chronology for the Trinity and described a model concerning the context of archaeological materials in the terrace sequence. Beginning in 1959, the impact of Bob Slaughter's paleontologic investigations dominated the literature on the Trinity for nearly 10 years. Slaughter, his colleagues, and students actively pursued vertebrate and invertebrate fossil localities in the Trinity terraces and published descriptive monographs and interpretive summaries of the Late Pleistocene history of those deposits. These include identification of new taxa [Slaughter 1959, 1960] or first occurrences in Texas. They included relatively exhaustive summaries of faunas recovered from the "Hill-Shuler deposits" in the "T2 terrace" [Slaughter et al. 40


---

## Page 46

## Page 47

1962; Slaughter and Ritchie 1963; Slaughter 1966]. Interspersed during these "Slaughter" years were contributions from others involving Pleistocene fishes [Uyeno and Miller 1962] and molluscs [Cheatum and Allen 1963]. Slaughter also contributed to studies on the East Fork [Slaughter and Thurmond 1965]. An important work on the East Fork of the Trinity [Thurmond 1967] was one of the first to give balanced attention to the sedimentary and paleontologic data contained in East Fork terrace deposits. Closing the period of relatively active study of Trinity terraces and paleontologic data was a thesis by Willimon [1970; 1972]. This study, like that of Thurmond involved stratigraphic descriptions as well as systematic descriptions of recovered Pleistocene faunas. Willimon's thesis differed from earlier studies in that it provided a number of radiocarbon ages for the sediments containing the Late Pleistocene faunas. The last publication dealing with the Quaternary geology of the Trinity is one of few dealing with the West Fork, and one not produced by an SMU student. Yu [1974] studied the West Fork sediments from a point of view of granulometry, mineralogy, and organic chemistry. In the last 10 years, active research on the Trinity's geologic history has waned considerably. Some studies of terrace sediments and soils have been conducted in conjunction with archaeological projects to the south of the Dallas area [Raab 1982b] and in association with the Lakeview (Joe Pool) project on Mountain Creek [Pheasant 1982]. A renewed investigation of the Lewisville site was conducted in the late 1970s by the Corps of Engineers and the Smithsonian Institution. This team, taking advantage of the lowest levels of that lake since its construction, were charged with defining the age and context of the archaeological features and artifacts at that site. By way of summary, there are several important dimensions of the history of investigations of the Late Quaternary geology of the Trinity River. First, almost all of the work conducted on the Upper Trinity has been restricted to the Elm Fork. Only Dallas County portions of the West Fork have been seriously investigated, and these were done so mainly because they occurred in Dallas County. Therefore, no detailed correlations between terraces or sediments between the Elm Fork and the West Fork have been attempted. Parenthetically, the same condition prevails for archaeological research on the Elm Fork and West Fork. Secondly, the work conducted on Trinity sediments and terraces has been dominated by two major thrusts, one concerning the geomorphology and correlation of the terraces, the second being the paleontologic study of faunas contained primarily within the "T2" terrace. As will be described below, these two major tasks have dominated researchers' interests in the Trinity. 42


---

## Page 48

Very little attention has been paid to reconstructing sedimentary environments through detailed analysis of sedimentary facies or the geometry of sediment bodies within and between terraces. Little attention has been given to systematic subsurface coring to define bedrock benches and potential bedrock channels. Very scant radiometric evidence is available concerning the ages of the terrace deposits. Virtually no soils work of any serious character has been conducted, save for the recent work in association with archaeological projects mentioned earlier. While these do constitute shortcomings to our abilities to interpret the Trinity's geologic history, it is perhaps somewhat consoling to compare the available literature on the Trinity with that of other rivers in the region. The number of studies published on the Trinity for example vastly outnumbers those for the entire Red River, one which even in Hill's [1901J scheme was a river of much greater antiquity and therefore of perhaps greater historical interest than one of Hill's "class-two" rivers such as the Trinity. With this perspective in mind, the discussion will now turn to a summary of the previous investigations with respect to the major geologic and paleoenvironmental dimensions of the Trinity's history. These discussions will build towards a review of the present study's findings and an overall summary of available information concerning the last 20,000 years of past environments in the Upper Trinity Basin and the relationship these environments have to the preservation, character, and research potential of archaeological sites contained within Trinity sediments. Synthesis of Previous Geologic Investigations The subject of this section of the report is a synthesis of the investigations described above with the aim of identifying the major geologic and paleoenvironmental evidence for conditions that prevailed in the Upper Trinity Basin over the last 20,000 years and at the same time to define the geomorphic, stratigraphic/soils deposits that pertain to that time period. As mentioned above, the time range of this consideration (i.e., the last 20,000 years) is that within which any probable archaeological occupations of the valley are expected. Ideally, this discussion should concentrate on well-dated sediments that encompass this 20,000-year age. The reason why this relatively straightforward approach could not be taken will become clear as the following discussion unfolds. 43


---

## Page 49

The review of Late Quaternary Trinity geologic history will begin with a brief overview of the studies concerning the entire Quaternary in the study area. Then, the identification of a tentative 20,000 year old "time line" will be undertaken. Following this discussion, younger deposits presumably within the time period of interest will be considered in terms of their geomorphology, stratigraphy, depositional environments, post-depositional weathering, and geochronology. The Trinity Terraces: A Resume Many of the published works cited above have dealt with geologic mapping and/or identification and correlation of Trinity terraces. These investigations, conducted mainly in Dallas County, have spanned a number of decades and have resulted in a significant portion of the literature concerning the Trinity River's history. Any review of these separate publications, including the debates concerning correlations, is bound to leave the reader somewhat confused. This situation has been created by several conditions. First, it is entirely likely that the Trinity's terraces are not perfectly matched. Second, it seems likely that terrace surfaces in many cases grade gently one to the other, making their identification tentative if not somewhat arbitrary. Third, some of the correlations and terrace identifications proposed by various workers have been strictly geomorphic (i.e., based on elevations above present stream grade or present floodplain), whereas others have combined geomorphic and stratigraphic observations in order to establish a terrace nomenclature. No systematic coring programs have been undertaken in order to define the elevations of terrace surfaces, their internal lithology, and the elevation of bedrock benches that underlie the terrace deposits. And last, there are few radiometric dates upon which to base a Late Pleistocene and Holocene geochronology. An additional factor is the near absence of any soil studies that would help give a relative age to land surfaces along the Trinity. Together, these shortcomings have created a problematic situation for the student or independent reader. The names and elevations of terraces along the Trinity provided by a number of workers over the past few decades are provided in Table 4.2. While certain names obviously appear in the schemes of several researchers, comparisons of the elevations and in consideration of their stated correlations, one to the other, make any unification of these schemes difficult if not impossible. Many of these workers appreciated the difficulties in correlation as they describe their terrace sequences. other workers have been confident enough to revise and synthesize. 44
