## Page 51

Foremost among these have been Taggart [1953] and Crook and Harris [1957]. The former is significant in that Taggart's study involved the most comprehensive review of all previous mapping exercises within Dallas County. His map is essentially a summary of his own work as well as the combined efforts of those cited above (Table 4.1). Crook and Harris's scheme [1957] is important because it has dominated the archaeological literature for the Trinity over the last 20 years. It is also been adopted in the course of certain paleontologic investigations [Slaughter et al. 1962; Slaughter 1966J. The scheme of Crook and Harris is also important because it contains what is the apparently earliest evidence for human occupation in the Upper Trinity Basin. The Pemberton-Hill Lewisville (T2) terrace remnant situated on the right bank of the Elm Fork at its confluence with Hickory Creek contained a Pleistocene vertebrate and invertebrate fauna and a Clovis spearpoint. Since this discovery, the age and correlation of this terrace with others along the Trinity has been actively pursued [Slaughter et al, 1962; Slaughter and Ritchie 1963; Slaughter 1966]. Because this terrace has been described as that which contains the earliest artifact-bearing alluvial sediments in the Trinity Valley, it is a convenient if not necessary starting point for the subsequent discussions. Therefore, the "Pemberton Hill-Lewisville" terrace, using the terminology of Crook and Harris [1957], will be briefly discussed in terms of its age and potential as an artifact-bearing deposit. The Pemberton Hill-Lewisville Terrace ("T2") The Pemberton Hill-Lewisville Terrace (hereinafter refered to as "T2") received national attention when the discovery a Clovis spear point in deposits contained under this terrace surface was announced. Since that time, there have been several publications describing the faunas from these terrace deposits and correlative faunal materials from other Quaternary deposits along the Trinity and its tributaries [Slaughter et al. 1962; Slaughter and Ritchie ~963; Crook 1961]. Using the Lewisville Site as a reference section, the described stratigraphy of the "T2" terrace as presented by Crook is evident. The several formations in the "T2" deposit were named after important people who had worked on the geology of this region. The basal Hill gravels and the overlying "shuler Formation" were named for the earliest workers on the Trinity. At the top, the "Richards Formation" was also named for a Trinity geologist. This stratigraphic section has served as a reference for all subsequent publications on the "T2" deposits. 46


---

## Page 52

At the Lewisville Site, the "T2" deposits form a thick section of alluvium that may be traced for a number of miles upstream along Hickory Creek. Today, at least two good exposures of deep deposits that are grossly similar to the inundated Lewisville Site section may be observed upstream from the Hickory Creek arm of Lewisville Lake. These deposits show the alternate input of silicious sediments as well as carbonate-rich sediments derived from Cretaceous rocks farther upstream. The inheritance of calcareous parent materials in the "T2" terrace therefore is one of the dominant aspects of its lithologic character. The possibility that deposits of similar age, but with sediments derived from different source rocks, has not been given serious attention. In fact, a uniform stratigraphic appearance for the "T2" deposits has been presumed for all exposures along the Trinity. Another factor that is important in considering alluvial stratigraphy is the depositional environments of these sediments. The "TZ" section at Lewisville, like most others that have been observed in terrace deposits along the Trinity, fines upwards. In other words, all other factors being equal, the deposits appear to reflect waning energies and sequential deposition of bedload, then coarse-grained traction and suspension deposits, and finally in the upper portion of the terrace, thick suspension deposits. This fining upwards sequence is not at all atypical for sediments deposited on a floodplain. The combination of parent materials and depositional histories must be considered when stratigraphic correlations are made. The faunas recovered from the Lewisville Site suggested a Sangamon age [Slaughter et al. 1962; Slaughter 1966]. Correlations with faunas up and down the Trinity and at at least one tributary (Clear Creek) confirm that the faunas found within the lower Shuler Formation sands were roughly contemporary and were most likely of Sangamon age. At the time, Slaughter and others felt confident that an age of approximately 25,000 to 50,000 years was reasonable for these deposits [Slaughter 1966]. Today, a discrepancy between the accepted age of Sangamon deposits and those age estimates is evident, or the Sangamon-Wisconsin transition would be placed much closer to 80,000 years B.P.. In the older scheme, the two radiocarbon dates obtained for Shuler sands and the overlying Shuler clays were acceptable. The first date of 37,000 years B.P. was obtained on charred materials (which possibly included some lignite) from the Lewisville Site [Crook and Harris 1959]. The second date was obtained from shells located within "upper Shuler clays" from the Clear Creek faunal locality [Slaughter and Ritchie 1963]. This date of 28,840 + 4,740 years B.P. (SM-534) fell within the general range of the Sangamon at that time, although Slaughter and Ritchie were required to propose that the date might reflect an interstadial within the Wisconsin. 47


---

## Page 53

Together these two radiocarbon dates do not agree well with general interpretations of the faunas from clear Creek, Lewisville, and other localities described farther downstream. Comparisons with other faunas of the plains [Slaughter 1966; Hibbard 1970] suggest that the Hill-Shuler and Moore pit faunas (all of which are correlated within the ""T2"" deposits) are in fact of Late Sangamon or earliest Wisconsin age. A clear problem with this age assignment is the evidence for human activities at the Lewisville site. The clovis spear point and other artifacts (the others of which appear to have been found in less firm contexts than the Clovis spear point) clearly pose problems when Sangamon or Early Wisconsin ages are invoked. The issues surrounding the age and potential archaeological contents of the "T2" deposits will require substantially more information before any clear resolve may be expected. Several issues need to be addressed in any such investigations however. First, a consideration of the "T2" sediments with respect to depositional environments and lateral variability in those depositional environments is imperative. Elevating what are essentially sedimentary facies (i.e., the Hill gravels, the Shuler sands and clays, and the Richards "alluvium") to formational status defies standard stratigraphic principles. Until these depositional facies may be shown to have mapable horizontal extent and well-defined upper and lower stratigraphic limits, they should in fact be looked at as local depositional units, not as lithostratigraphic formations. In conclusion, the "T2" deposits of Crook and Harris are tentatively and tenuously correlated with the Travis School "T3" deposits of Taggart [1953]. These sediments, with diverse lithologic and pedogenic characteristics, occur sporadically along the Elm Fork, West Fork, and some of its principal tributaries. Radiometric and faunal evidence for the age of these terrace deposits are not in good accord, but all of them suggest an age that is well in excess of established ages for Clovis occupations in North America [Stanford 1983]. While the potential for pre-Clovis occupations in the Americas is still hotly debated and new evidence from South America continues to suggest ever more strongly that pre-Clovis occupations are in fact demonstrable with archaeological evidence, it is important to note that the principal artifact recovered from the Lewisville Site is indeed typical of the Clovis spear points that are very well dated elsewhere in North America to approximately 11,000 to 12,000 years B.P. While it is not possible to conclude that some Wisconsin- aged occupations of North America took place, there is no evidence to suggest that the Clovis spear point tradition persisted for more than a few centuries about 11,500 years ago. Therefore, the "T2" deposits of Crook and Harris are best looked at first from a geologic and paleontologic point of view. From 48


---

## Page 54

this view, they comprise a mid-Wisconsin to Sangamon deposit. Resolving the provenience of the Clovis spear point within these deposits it is not easily accomplished given that geologic interpretation. Turning now to the lower terraces of the Trinity, we will consider younger sediments as potential contexts for archaeological sites. The Lower Terraces of the Trinity The "T2" terrace was abandoned as the Trinity began a phase of major downcutting. The timing of this is naturally related to the age of the "T2" sediments themselves as well as the age of the youngest inset sediments below this abandoned floodplain. The terraces below "T2" have been classified by Taggart [1953] as the Union Terminal or his "T4" at elevations between 20 and 35 feet above the floodplain and the Carrollton or "T5" at elevations from 5 to 20 feet above the floodplain (Fig. 4.3). Tentatively correlated with these terrace classifications are Crook and Harris's [1957] Union Terminal-Carrollton or "Tl" terrace situated approximately 30 feet above the floodplain. Crook and Harris also mention a "Tl rise" approximately 15 feet above the floodplain, but pay scant attention to it in terms of its age or potential archaeological contents. These lower terraces are of increasing interest to archaeologists in that they are clearly younger than the "T2" deposits of Wisconsin or Sangamon age, and also they are of significance because archaeological materials occur on these terraces. The extent of Taggart's Union Terminal terrace (his "T4") is notable. It occurs in somewhat alternating fashion along the Elm Fork and along the main Trinity River in Dallas County. Fairly extensive areas occur along the south side of Denton Creek near Carrollton. Relatively continuous exposures are mapped by Taggart in the Farmers Branch area on the east bank of the Trinity. An extensive exposure of deposits is mapped in the Irving area near the junction of the Elm Fork and West Fork of the Trinity. Deposits occur on the south side of Dallas and then, with some regularity, along the west bank of the river below the confluence of the main fork of the Trinity with White Rock Creek. These distributions may also be seen on quadrangle maps prepared by Winn in the Carrollton area [1953; Fig 4.1] and in the Pleasant Grove area [Overmyer 1953; Fig 4.2]. The recognition of a geomorphic unit lower than Taggart's Union Terminal terrace is evident in Winn's map. His lowest terrace is correlated with Taggart's T5 or Carrollton terrace, 49


---

## Page 55

though it is less extensive than the Union Terminal terrace. In some places, it is contiguous with the higher Union Terminal terrace deposits and is always situated between those higher deposits and the present channel of the Trinity. In other cases, it occurs as isolated rises in the floodplain recalling the citations of Crook and Harris. Near Farmers Branch, Taggart mapped (following Winn [1953]) small exposures of this lowest terrace that are isolated from Union Terminal terrace deposits by what appear to be abandoned meanders of the Trinity. A similar type of outcrop is evident just south of Farmers Branch near where state Highway 114 crosses the eastern portion of the Trinity terraces. It is perhaps significant that near the confluence of the Elm Fork and the West Fork where the floodplain is broad, only small isolated remnants of Taggart's Carrollton terrace are mapped. In the constricted area near downtown Dallas where the Austin chalk is crossed by the Trinity and where the valley is narrowest in the project area, there are no outcrops of this terrace whatsoever. Farther downstream, Taggart's maps terraces at this elevation only below the second constriction at Dowdy Ferry Road and in fact, according to his map, it is a remnant of this terrace that severely constricts the Trinity valley at Dowdy Ferry. This constriction has potential significance to the Late Quaternary and Holocene history of the Trinity and will be considered later. Taggart's Union Terminal terrace had been recognized earlier by Shuler [1935] and later called the Farmers Branch terrace by Patillo [1940]. Taggart suggests the terrace is relatively undissected, and he considers it to be a distinctive geomorphic feature. The Carrollton terrace, on the other hand, appears to be both geomorphically and lithologically distinct from the Union Terminal according to Taggart. Mapped earlier by Patillo [1940], who named it the Carrollton Terrace, and later by Turner [1951J and Winn [1953J, it was called the Low Terrace. Crook and Harris [1957J apparently group these two terraces together under the Union Terminal-Carrollton Terrace designation ("Tl" by their scheme). While brief mention is made of the "floodplain or Tl rises," no other attention is paid to them in their report. The "typical" stratigraphy of Crook and Harris's "Tl" is described as 5 feet of basal gravels, 5 feet of red sandy clay (the Albritton clay), and 5 feet of unstratified sand (the Patillo sand). A number of deep stratigraphic cuts found in South Dallas County quarries in this locality provided Willimon [1970; 1972] with the opportunity to describe multiple sections of the floodplain deposits (Figure 4.4). At two localities, radiocarbon dates on these deposits were obtained that challenge previous notions concerning the antiquity of the Trinity floodplain. These stratigraphic sections of the faunas and the dates, bear strongly ,on the interpretation of the lower terraces in the area. 50


---

## Page 57

Willimon describes six sections in the Trinity floodplain which he interprets as being truncated "Tl deposits" (Figure 4.4). Each of these consists of fining upwards sequences of alluvium, each with basal gravels and overlain by crossbedded sands and clays. In one of the sections, the crossbedded sands are not present. In the upper portion of several of the sections are described what appear to be well developed soils. This is probably the "buried paleosol" described by Willimon [1970;1972J that occurs on these truncated deposits and which is covered in some sections by dark gray recent alluvium. The latter is dated to ca. 300 years B.P. [Willimon 1972J although no specific radiocarbon ages are cited. In Willimon's study area, the Trinity floodplain is considerably broader than in our study area farther upstream. These sections, in the vicinity of Bois D'arc Island and the adjacent areas flanking the high "T2" terrace, pose problems in correlation with deposits farther upstream. of particular importance here is the pronounced constriction of the Trinity near Dowdy Ferry Road (Figure 4.3). This constriction, like the one farther upstream near downtown Dallas and the one on the West Fork near the county line, must have been important in terms of past hydrologic regimes. Below the Dowdy Ferry constriction, a marked expansion of the Trinity floodplain is evident, suggesting that given similar supplies of sediment, depositional features below the constriction might have attained an elevation that was somewhat lower relative to the active channel than those in narrower portions of the valley. At the same time, it is possible that broadening of the valley enhanced fall-out of suspended sediments, thereby maintaining relative equilibrium between upstream and downstream localities. Nonetheless, the radiocarbon dates obtained by Willimon on these truncated terrace deposits are challenging. The three dates cluster between 20,000 and 22,000 years B.P. (Figure 4.4). These occur below the soil zone suggested by Willimon's profiles and in association with a Wisconsin-aged fauna of snails and the remains of a Bison antiquus. Willimon's stratigraphic correlation of these deposits with others along the Trinity is encumbered by the fact that he only recognizes three terraces. In this case then, his "Tl" must subsume both the Carrollton and Union Terminal terraces of Taggart. At the same time, his "Tl" should correlate with Crook and Harris' "Tl." This correlation contradicts work that Crook and Harris [1955] conducted very near Taggart's study area. Their archaeological investigation of the Obshner Site revealed early Holocene Scottsbluff spear points in the upper portion of a 52


---

## Page 58

terrace some 40 feet above the Trinity floodplain. Crook and Harris identified this as the "Union Terminal Carrollton terrace" principally on the basis of the "Albritton and Patillo Formations" in the upper portion of the terrace. The fact that the sloping terrace surface at this point stands some 40 feet above the floodplain is not addressed in their report. Taggart maps the Carrollton and Union Terminal terraces inset below the high sloping remnant of the Travis school terrace, the latter of which correlates with Crook and Harris' "T2." Geomorphologically at least, the higher terrace designation of Taggart and Willimon for the setting of the Obshner site appears most reasonable. Willimon's data suggest that the sediments underlying the floodplain are considerably older than might have been suspected given previous interpretations. The bedrock bench upon which all of Willimon's sections were described must have been cut some time prior to 23,000 years B.P.. This may be taken as a minimum age for the abandonment of the next higher terrace, assuming that the next higher terrace is in fact the "T2" terrace of Crook and Harris [1957]. This indicates that the Trinity's floodplain was incised through at least 35 feet of alluvium and up to 30 feet of bedrock. Because of the magnitude of this down-cutting episode, an age for the uppermost "T2" sediments of Crook and Harris of mid- Wisconsin to perhaps Sangamon may seem reasonable. Trinity Floodplain For many years, it had been assumed that the Trinity's floodplain was an entirely recent feature and was comprised of sediments that were no more than a few hundred years old. As described by Crook and Harris [1957]; the floodplain consisted of approximately 20 feet of channel exposures: the lower ten of which were bedrock, and the upper ten were comprised of alluvium that was deposited "but yesterday." More recently, it has been observed that not only are the Trinity floodplain deposits thicker than had been earlier proposed, but also that they were somewhat older as well. Willimon [1970;1972] described floodplain alluvium obtaining depths of up to 38 feet. From one undesignated locality, a sample of mollusk shells yielded a radiocarbon age of 2,280 +/ 280 years B.P. (W-1689). Willimon [1972:467] further proposes that the Trinity floodplain contains channel fill dating to approximately 10,000 years B.P. Willimon assumes (perhaps correctly but on little geological evidence from the Trinity itself) that some of the sediments on the floodplain must fill the gap between the 2,000-year-old channel fill and his 20,000-year-old truncated "TI" deposits. His correlation with 53


---

## Page 59

Sulphur River alluvia dated to approximately 10,000 years B.P. [Slaughter and Hoover 1963J is convenient but not demonstrated by stratigraphic observations. Indeed, the geologic record of the Trinity following abandonment of the "Tl" terrace is one of the most pressing issues relevant to the archaeology of the upper Trinity Basin. This issue will be returned to later. Willimon also describes in very general terms a paleosol that occurs on the Trinity floodplain presumably below the Dowdy Ferry crossing in the area of his fieldwork. This paleosol is described as occurring beneath alluvium dated to approximately 300 B.P. (no radiocarbon ages cited). This younger alluvium, which buries the paleosol, varies between 8 inches and 3 feet [Willimon 1972:466-467J. Without clearer definition, it is difficult to assess the potential stratigraphic significance of the soil or to relate it to other areas of the Trinity. Nonetheless, Willimon's study clearly suggests that the Trinity floodplain and its buried alluvia probably represent much more time and accordingly much more potential significance to the Late Quaternary and Holocene history of the upper Trinity Basin. A Reconnaissance of Floodplain and Terrace Deposits Along the Trinity River in Dallas, Tarrant and Denton Counties Introduction As part of this project, a brief reconnaissance was undertaken of the floodplain and terrace sediments and soils of the Elm Fork, West Fork, and Trinity River. The extent of the study area and its obvious geologic complexity limited ours to a very preliminary investigation. Nevertheless, the study was focused on the investigation of deposits within the Trinity Basin that dated to the last 20,000 years. As the summary of previous work has indicated, this is by no means an easy task. This work was facilitated by some of the previously published investigations of the Trinity but was encumbered at the same time by extensive commercial development and alteration of the Trinity floodplain and its terraces in the last 20 years. Methodology The methods used in this study were designed to meet the scheduling constraints of the project as well as some of the other archaeological and cultural resources management objectives of the study. The latter included archaeological site visits that assisted both the geological investigations as well as the archaeological ones and also ground truthing of the remote sensing landuse analyses. The principal activities as undertaken 54


---

## Page 60

in the course of this study were the following: Reconnaissance. Trips were taken to numerous portions of the study area in order to observe geomorphic features. We examined naturally occurring or previously excavated stratigraphic profiles as possible sites for detailed description and to accomplish above-mentioned archaeological and landuse observations. stratigraphic-soils descriptions. Following reconnaissance work, a number of stratigraphic localities were defined over the study area (Figure 4.5). Most of these were revisited and detailed soils-stratigraphic sections were described. The included sections were measured, described, and sampled for subsequent laboratory analyses. Some sections were simply measured and described without sampling. These sections occurred at localities containing archaeological materials, as well as localities devoid of such materials. Laboratory analyses. Sediment and soil samples collected in the field were analyzed at the Institute of Applied Sciences, NTSU. Analyses included particle size (sieve-hydrometer), organic carbon, and pH. In addition charcoal or soil samples were processed for radiocarbon dating. Map and core analysis. As part of the literature review, all available geologic maps for the study area were acquired. These included the several quadrangle maps sited earlier in this report from Dallas County as well as geologic maps prepared by Taggart [1953] and several geologic maps provided by the Corps of Engineers, Fort Worth District. In addition, the geotechnical branch of the Corps of Engineers-Ft. worth generously provided numerous borehole logs. These came from three areas: the Ray Roberts Dam access in northern Denton County, the Lewisville Dam access in southern Denton County, and an extensive series of borehole logs from a previously planned levee project on the Trinity floodplain in the area between downtown Dallas and Dowdy Ferry Road. These borehole logs and the accompanying maps provided the opportunity to construct geologic cross sections at these three portions of the river. Interviews. One objective of this project was to interview persons who had previously worked on the geology of the Trinity River. Several of these people were contacted. Mr. W.W. Crook of Dallas generously provided his ideas information and spent one day taking the writer and Dan Prikryl to a number of localities along the Trinity River. Mr. Larry Banks also joined us for one day in the field examining localities along Hickory Creek and providing us with his knowledge and recommendations for further studies in this area. Mr. Bob Slaughter of Dallas Texas was 55

contacted concerning this ·project. He informed us that he is writing a summary of the Quaternary geology of the Trinity River for the Dallas Geologic Society at this time. We look forward to gaining his impressions concerning past and present ideas on the Trinity when that report is published. Investigations on the Elm Fork of the Trinity The lack of detailed geologic cross sections supplemented by drilled cores has hampered resolution of the stratigraphy of the Trinity River alluvia. A number of cores taken along the Elm Fork of the Trinity have been made by the US Corps of Engineers, Fort Worth District as well as private concerns that are developing portions of the floodplain. The Fort Worth District generously provided borehole logs from three areas on the Elm and Main forks on the Trinity. Two of the sections are on the Elm Fork, one at Ray Roberts Dam in northern Denton County, and the other at the site of the Lewisville Dam. The Lewisville Dam Cross Section As part of the construction of Lewisville Dam in the 1950s, the Corps of Engineers drilled a large number of boreholes in the terraces and floodplain of the Elm Fork of the Trinity along the dam axis and in areas of potential borrow pit excavation. Logs of these boreholes and the geomorphic cross sections that may be drawn with these data, provide useful insights into the character of the Trinity River terraces and alluvia at this point. It is important to bear in mind the setting of this dam. It is at one of the narrower portions of the Trinity Valley upstream from Dallas (Figure 4.6). This setting, typical of most dams, creates some problems in terms of correlating data from this locality with those collected from areas where the floodplain is much broader. The hydrologic controls on deposition in narrow or constricted portions of valleys may result in significantly different erosional and depositional regimes when contrasted with broader reaches of the valley [Leopold et al. 1964] Additionally, the dam at Lewisville was constructed just downstream from the confluence of Hickory Creek with the Elm Fork. Following suggestions from Dr. Claude Albritton, many of the students at SMU in the 1950s [e.g., Taggart 1953; Winn 1953; Overmyer 1953] pointed out the potential effects of tributary streams with regard to terrace formation in the trunk valleys, in this case the effects of dumping of sediments from the Hickory 57


---

## Page 63

N ~ o 2000 4000 feet I I I I I CONTOUR INTERVAL 20 FEET r-t-, ~ co Lf) o Borehole Location Cross Section 0-0 Figure 4.6a Contour map of the Le~isville 0am area


---

## Page 64

Creek drainage on the Elm Fork floodplain. One possible effect noted by those researchers was that "alluvial fans" might possibly form just downstream from such confluences. Such features would compound the lateral and downstream variability of geomorphic features and strqtigraphic sections. In any event, the location of the Lewisville archaeological site must be regarded cautiously with respect to correlations both up and downstream. Geomorphically, the cross section provided by the Lewisville Dam access illustrates several very distinct features. On the west is the well developed "T2" terrace of Crook and Harris. In fact, it is just a few hundred yards north of Borehole #1 that the Lewisville Site is situated (Figure 4.6). This terrace drops off sharply towards the Elm Fork where the floodplain and low terrace remnants describe an irregular surface at about 470 feet elevation. To the east, several small benches rise above the Trinity's floodplain until a terrace surface, approximately the same as that of the "T2" terrace on the opposite bank, is evident. The dam access continues across the incised Stuart's Creek valley and then rises to a still higher surface at the eastern end of the dam access. Fortunately, enough of the boreholes penetrated the bedrock, thereby enabling a fairly accurate reconstruction of the bedrock benches that underlie the alluvia (Figure 4.6). The highest bench is at the eastern end of the dam axis at an elevation of approximately 525 feet. This bench apparently slopes towards the west and then is deeply incised at Stuart's Creek. The next highest bench underlies the "T2" deposits at the western end of the dam axis. This bench, at an elevation of approximately 490 feet, is overlain by approximately 35 feet of alluvium as described by Crook and Harris [1957J. It is significant that the bedrock bench underlying the terrace opposite that containing the Lewisville Site is some 30 feet lower than the bench on the western side of the valley, yet the alluvia overlying the bench in Borehole #5 (Figure 4.6) attains an elevation almost exactly the same as that of the "T2" terrace on the opposite (west) bank of the river. A distinct pair of bedrock benches is exposed under the floodplain and possible low terrace remnants. This bench occurs at a depth of approximately 15 to 30 feet below the level of the floodplain. The channel of the Elm Fork at this point was approximately 25 feet deep. Of great interest is the 60 feet of alluvium that overlie the lowest bedrock exposure in the Elm Fork Valley at this point. This clearly shows an incision of bedrock some 35 to 40 feet below the principal bedrock bench under the floodplain. A 60


---

## Page 65

similar deeply incised cut to bedrock is illustrated along stuart's Creek, although this cut does not reach the same absolute depth as that of the Trinity. Nonetheless, it suggests a response to the incision of the Elm Fork by its laterals. Overall, the geomorphology of the terrace surfaces and the buried bedrock benches are quite revealing in terms of alluvial sequences of erosion and aggradation. First, while matched terraces appear to be evident on the west and east banks of the Elm Fork at the elevation of the "T2" terrace, it is clear that alluvia within these terraces differ in thickness and lithology and rest on bedrock benches of quite different absolute elevations. "T2" alluvia on the west bank are quite compatible with the general description of Crook and Harris [1957]. It consists of an overall fining upward sequence with basal gravels overlain by sands and finally by fine-grained silts and clays. The "T2" alluvia on the east bank, however, includes a thick section of loamy deposits overlain by alternating gravel, clays, and sands. Differences in lithology and thickness of the alluvial deposits suggest that multiple cycles of erosion and deposition may have culminated in floodplain abandonment and terrace formation at approximately the same elevations. These borehole logs do not permit dating of these events. Detailed studies of the sections, including the recovery of diagnostic faunal materials or samples for radiocarbon dating, would be necessary to fully define the cut and fill sequences here. The alluvium underlying the floodplain and low terrace remnants is of more archaeological concern than the higher terraces. There appear to be two principal units on the basis of the Corps of Engineers' well logs. First are gravels overlain by tan sands. These appear mostly on the west bank overlying the bedrock bench as exposed in Boreholes #2, #16, and #3 (Figure 4.6). They also occur, however, in Borehole #9 on the east bank of the floodplain. These coarse, gravelly, and sandy deposits are perhaps comparable to those found downstream underlying the "Carrollton" and/or "Union Terminal" terraces of Taggart. Unfortunately, there are no soil profiles included in the borehole descriptions that would permit estimation of paleosurface stability and also the possible extent of truncation of alluvia that may have overlain these gravels and sands. In marked contrast to these gravelly and sandy deposits at the flanks of the floodplain are the deep alluvia that infill the incised bedrock channel. In Borehole #17, the alluvia consist of alternating layers of sands and clays. The basal 8 feet of this deposit includes clayey and silty, blue to dark gray sand. This is overlain in turn by layers approximately 2 feet thick of gray and tan sands and clays. The middle portion of this core is dominated by silty brown sands, and the upper 16 feet consist of 61


---

## Page 66

silty brown to gray clay." Boreholes #14 and #15 have basal deposits of sand overlain by alternating loamy, sandy, or clayey deposits. While more detailed profile descriptions would be necessary to fully define the depositional environments for the sediments underlying the floodplain, some general observations are possible. The sediments on the margins of the floodplain clearly represent higher energy deposits. The gravel and sands therefore may represent the basal portions of now truncated fining upward sequences of channel fills, or they may possibly represent lag deposits overlain by braided stream sands. By contrast, the deeper alluvia filling the bedrock channel incised below the floodplain seem, by their heterogeneous textures and vertical changes in lithology, to represent the lateral and vertical changes in alluvial textures that might characterize an aggrading meandering stream. Overall, the cross section at Lewisville Dam provides important insights into the possible complexity of the alluvial history of the Trinity's floodplain and its terraces. These data clearly indicate that detailed descriptions of sedimentary faces and post-depositional weathering features, including soil profiles, are essential in the task of reconstructing the stratigraphic history of the terrace deposits and reconstructing the sedimentary environments. Except for major changes in the geomorphic relationships of the alluvial deposits (for example, that represented between the "T2" terrace and the sediments overlying the floodplain bedrock bench), it is clear that age controls will be necessary in order to accurately correlate some of these alluvial units. The Ray Roberts Cross section At the time of this writing, the Lake Ray Roberts Dam is under construction and nearly completed. This dam, located approximately 20 miles (35 km) upstream from the Lewisville Dam on the Elm Fork of the Trinity, was studied by Corps of Engineers geologists in preparation for construction activities. As at the Lewisville Dam axis, a series of boreholes were drilled across the valley. The logs of these boreholes and the topographic information provide new insights into variability in the geomorphology and alluvial stratigraphy of the Elm Fork. The major portion of the cross section is shown in Figure 4.7. Clearly evident are several bedrock benches and/or bedrock channels overlain by alluvia which are best described as comprised of one terrace and an irregular series of floodplain deposits forming an irregular surface. The higher alluvial deposits described in the valley occur on the western portion of the cross section. These deposits form an irregular surface approximately 50 feet above the modern 62

floodplain. Boreholes in these higher alluvial deposits indicate massive yellowish or grayish-brown clayey sands and clays with darker (soil??) horizons near the surface in several columns. In the apparent channels in the westernmost part of this high terrace are basal gravels and sands. These massive deposits of light-colored alluvium extend north into the area of the future reservoir. Borrowing activities in this alluvia resulted in the recovery about 3 years ago of mastodon and other Pleistocene megafaunas. The floodplain alluvia are comprised of basal gravels overlain by light-colored clays and sandy clays, with possible soil horizons and/or darker clays in the upper portions of several columns. These attain thicknesses of approximately 40 feet across the floodplain. The possibility of eroded portions of terraces intermediate to the floodplain and the high bench to the west cannot be confirmed from the available information. The eastern portion of the valley at this point consists of a steep bluff exposing Cretaceous bedrock. This is covered by a veneer of colluvium that interfingers with the alluvial deposits near the base of the slope. The striking difference between the Ray Roberts cross section and the Lewisville Dam cross section is the absence of a trenched bedrock bench beneath the floodplain deposits. This difference may be explained by the proximity of a steep bedrock valley wall and the fact that the width of the floodplain at Ray Roberts is only about 3,500 feet compared to approximately 8,000 feet along the Lewisville Dam axis. Quite different sediment loads and erosive potentials are certainly probable between these two areas on the Elm Fork. may in fact be filled alluvium that was deposited roughly at the same time as the alluvium in the shallow portions of that terrace exposure. Sections at Ray Roberts and Lewisville document deep incision of the Elm fork channel into bedrock after abandonment of the "T2" terrace, which stands approximately 50 feet above the floodplain. Subsequently, a bedrock bench was cut that underlies the modern floodplain and, at Lewisville, terrace remnants between the floodplain and "T2." Still later, the Elm Fork incised bedrock to a depth of 60 feet at Lewisville. The White Rock Creek Cross Section The third cross section studied as part of this project is located just south of downtown Dallas. This section was reconstructed -from some of the scores of boreholes drilled in this portion of the Trinity floodplain in anticipation of a levee 64


---

## Page 69

project that has never gone to construction. The logs of 24 boreholes, defining a transect from the west to the eastern side of the Trinity Valley were plotted. Each of these corings had been described in the field and lab, and their elevations were mapped providing an accurate cross section of the valley at this point. In Figure 4.8, the valley profile on the eastern end of the transect rising to elevations of over 400 feet was inferred from the USGS base map for the borehole locations. The cross section near White Rock Creek is particularly revealing when compared to the Lewisville cross section. First, this cross section does not include the several higher terraces that have been mapped in this area by Taggart [1953]. Rather, it is confined mainly to the floodplain. Focusing on these deposits, it is clear that there are lithologic variations in the vicinities of Boreholes #10, #11, #12, and #13 that suggest possible terrace remnants overlain by younger alluvium. All the rest of the boreholes are dominated by yellowish-brown or light yellowish-brown clays and silts that tend to be much darker gray or dark grayish brown near the surface. Near the Trinity channel, these alluvia are dark gray or dark grayish-brown for depths up to 40 feet below the present floodplain. A number of these boreholes penetrated Austin Chalk bedrock. These define irregular benches at approximate elevation of 375 feet on both sides of the Trinity River and also on the eastern side of the White Rock Creek confluence. Of importance is Borehole #23 which shows that alluvium near white Rock Creek attains depths of at least 60 feet, and as shown in Figure 4.8, bedrock was not penetrated at this depth. While the boreholes along the Trinity on this transect are considerably shallower, the assumption that the grade of white Rock Creek was controlled by the Trinity indicates clearly that the Trinity too must be incised deeply into bedrock at this point. The fact that these deep incisions occur under floodplain deposits strengthens the observation made on the Lewisville Dam data that the floodplain sediments are deeper and therefore probably considerably older than had hitherto been expected. They further provoke consideration of the timing of this deep incision. Willimon's [1970;1972] dates on truncated "Tl" deposits of 23,000 to 20,000 B.P. were obtained on materials in settings similar to the shallow bedrock benches beneath the floodplain described both at Lewisville and in the white Rock Creek area. Although these are described as truncated "Tl" deposits, the upper surfaces of these profiles are nearly at the elevation of the floodplain, and in fact it is implied by Willimon that they are capped by thin veneers of recent alluvium. It is proposed that the most reasonable timing for the incision .of the deep trenches into bedrock below the bedrock 65

## Page 71

--- ------------ benches underlying the floodplain postdates the aggradation phase dated by Willimon: the incision probably is younger than ca. 20,000 years B.P. As proposed by Willimon [1972], abandonment of the "Tl" deposits was followed by erosion of those deposits and subsequently incision of the floodplain. Although Willimon described exposures of deep alluvium along the floodplain in Highway Department cores at the present axis of u.s. 625, he does not emphasize the extent or depth of the deposits beneath the floodplain. The cross sections from Lewisville and white Rock Creek indicate this downcutting episode was one of considerable magnitude. The timing and environmental conditions surrounding this episode, as well as the implications of the floodplain geomorphology and stratigraphy will be discussed at the end of this section. Now we will turn to stratigraphic evidence concerning terrace sediments and soils along the Elm Fork and West Fork of the Trinity. Investigations in the Carrollton-Farmers Branch Area A number of reconnaissance visits to the Carrollton/Farmers Branch area were made, and several sections were described at localities that occur on the Carrollton USGS quandrangle map. This is an area that had been mapped in some detail in the past, which contains good exposures of the principal stratigraphic units of archaeological interest and also contains a high density of recorded archaeological sites. Not incidentally, this is also an area that is slated for intensive development in the future and is the site of active development today. Therefore, studies in this area concerning stratigraphy, soil formation, and the context of archaeological sites were well suited to the objectives of this project. Unfortunately, this area has already been subjected to intensive development of the terraces, as well as major portions of the floodplain. This, to a great degree, will encumber any future geologic investigation in the area. In particular, it will be extremely difficult to construct accurate topographic maps and measured cross sections in the valley, owing to extensive commercial and residential development and filling activities along the floodplain. Taggart's [1953] map indicates the location of the principal geomorphic, units that may be tentatively defined according to his scheme. The units Taggart defined, many of which are based on previous mapping efforts in this area [Patillo 1940; Winn 1953], must be viewed as informal geomorphic-stratigraphic units. This does not deny the efforts of previous investigators, but rather follows their own admissions that detailed stratigraphic 67


---

## Page 72

studies of these units, as well as soil studies and dating efforts, must be undertaken in order to refine the alluvial stratigraphy and to clarify stratigraphic relationships of geomorphic units. This project represents a reconnaissance effort directed towards these objectives. Regrettably, only a few sections could be described in the course of this study (Figure 4.5). These will be described and discussed below using Taggart's [1953] scheme for convenience. Investigations of the Union Terminal Terrace Taggart's Union Terminal terrace (QT4) is that which probably correlates with the "Union Terminal-Carrollton" terrace ("Tl") of Crook and Harris [1957]. In the Carrollton/Farmers Branch area, Taggart mapped extensive areas as Union Terminal terrace, and a number of archaeological sites have been found in association with this geomorphic unit. It is therefore of archaeological interest as a site setting. We investigated deposits mapped as the Union Terminal terrace in three areas. First, a section was described just west of Denton Tap Road along Denton Creek. A second section was described just north of the now destroyed Wheeler archaeological site [Crook and Harris 1954a], and a third section was described about 2.5 miles west of Farmers Branch and just north of Valley View Lane. These three exposures comprise an admittedly small sample of Taggart's Union Terminal terrace; however, all three of them may be related to nearby archaeological deposits. These represent the first investigations of these terrace deposits since they were originally described over 30 years ago. Geomorphically, the Union Terminal terrace exhibits variations over the areas mapped by Taggart. Elevations range from approximately 10 to over 30 feet above the Trinity floodplain in the vicinity of the confluence of Denton Creek with the Elm Fork. Similarly, in the vicinity of Farmers Branch, the Union Terminal terrace deposit lies from approximately 15 feet above the floodplain near the present Elm Fork channel to perhaps 30 or 35 feet above the floodplain approximately 1.5 to 2 miles to the east near Farmers Branch. Each of the sections we described were located relatively close to either the Elm Fork or Denton Creek and therefore represent the exposed lower elevations of this geomorphic unit. However, in the vicinity of the Wheeler Site (north of sandy Lake Road and west of Denton Creek), the area mapped as the Union Terminal terrace exhibits considerable local relief, and much of this area has been extensively quarried and is presently under construction. Small remnant exposures are present near Denton Creek, about a mile north of Sandy Lake Road. 68


---

## Page 73

At an elevation of approximately 470 feet along Denton Creek, a sloping cut on the north end of an old quarry area provide exposures of the well-developed soil near the surface. A red argillic horizon is present at the top of yellowish-brown, calcareous clays, which in turn overlie gravels and bedrock. A disturbed, but approximately 1 to 2 foot thick, massive A-horizon occurs over the red argillic horizon of the soil. This is strikingly similar to the "Albritton and Patillo formations" described by Crook and Harris [1954a,b; 1957]. This relict soil appears to follow the surface of the Union Terminal terrace as it slopes off towards the east. Without measured sections in several areas, it is not possible to say whether the soil has formed on a surface that has been progressively or perhaps episodically lowered by erosion, or whether in fact multiple inset alluvial units are present. The section we described just north of the old Wheeler archaeological site is at one of the lower elevations of this terrace exposure. Yet, the soils described in our section may be traced to the west for some 100 to 125 meters, and covering perhaps as much as 10 feet in increasing elevation. The Wheeler Profile. The profile described near the Wheeler site is referred to informally as the Wheeler Pit Profile (Figure 4.9; Table 4.3). This profile is located in a deeply incised gully that drains into Denton Creek some 40 meters to the east of the described section. The 3-meter profile has been divided into three main stratigraphic units, informally designated as units A, B, and C. These informal units actually represent changing depositional facies as much as they might possibly represent quite different depositional episodes. Therefore, these are quite strictly lithologic subdivisions and not necessarily mappable informal units. The basal unit, A, consists of interbedded gravels and sands. The gravels are well-rounded carbonate rock fragments, Creta~eous fossils, and rounded hematite concretions. The sands contain carbonate grains but are dominated by silicious sands. Unit B is comprised of medium crossbeds of quartz-rich sands. Plane laminations occur within each bed. These grade upwards to massive sandy loam deposits in the upper part of Unit B and the lower part of Unit C. These contain small calcareous nodules that appear to be modified carbonate clasts rather than pedogenic carbonates. unit C is a depositional unit upon which a soil has formed. The textural changes within this unit are therefore related both to the depositional environment and also to subsequent pedogenesis. As shown in Figure 4.9 this entire profile, like most we have investigated on the Trinity, fines upwards, a typical pattern for fluvial deposits. Therefore, the investigation of depositional/pedogenic records in alluvial deposits is 69

## Page 77

complicated by textural changes in the parent materials and subsequent downwards translocation of fine particles during soil formation. A principal objective of soil studies in fluvial environments is the recognition of the soil parent material. In this profile, as well as the others, one index or guide to parent material homogeneity is the breakdown of sand textures within the zones of soil formation. The assumption underlying this approach is that homogeneity of sand textures may be used to strengthen an interpretation of "homogeneous parent materials" for the soil. Downward translocation of clays then may be investigated within units that have homogeneous parent materials [Pheasant 1982]. Here at the Wheeler profile, the parent materials in Unit C for the soil all appear to be in the sandy loam textural range. Throughout all of the soil horizons in unit C, the size breakdowns within the sand fraction are not only homogeneous, but they are remarkably homogeneous (Table 4.4). There are probably no statistically significant changes in sand textures from the base of the C-horizon to the upper portion of the A-horizon in this profile. While mineralogic evidence may be used to firm conclusions on soil parent material homogeneity, it is assumed that the profiles we are investigating are not old enough to result in significant differences in weathering of stable versus unstable minerals [Birkeland 1984J. The soil developed on the alluvium at the Wheeler section includes a quite thick A-horizon. This has been divided into several subhorizons based on color and structural differences (Table 4.3). A transition zone, the AB horizon, shows both color and textural changes as a transition to the well developed argillic portion of the B-horizon. A significant increase in clay content is evident in this B-horizon as well as a significant change in colors with hues of 5YR. This B-horizon also exhibits fairly well developed subangular blocky structure and common clay films on ped surfaces. In the lower B-horizon and in the C-horizons of this soil profile, there are a number of faint yellowish-red mottles as well as iron/manganese concretions indicating the effects of groundwater in the profile. Although no artifacts were found in the immeditate vicinity of the described section, they were located eroding from the soil A-horizons approximately 40 meters to the west where the relict soil occurs at progressively higher elevations on the Union Terminal terrace. Denton Creek Profile 2. The section described on the Union Terminal terrace along Denton Creek is located approximately 200 meters south of Denton Creek and 600 meters west of Denton Tap Road. The profile was described on a vertical face of an abandoned gravel quarry. The terrace at this point forms a quite level surface at an approximate elevation of 465 feet. As at the 73

## Page 79

Wheeler profile, this elevation is only slightly above the floodplain at this point. The sediments in this exposure have been divided into two informal units, A and B, which as was the case for the Wheeler Profile, are of more depositional significance than any mappable geologic units. Unit A consists of the basal gravels of which only the upper portions were exposed in this profile. These gravels are similar to those at the Wheeler Profile consisting of moderately sorted subrounded to rounded limestone, fossil, and hematite clasts. Unit B overlies these gravels and attains a thickness of approximately 2.3 meters. These sediments are sandy loam in texture for the most part and exhibit a fining upwards character for about the lower two-thirds of the unit. A well-developed soil occurs on these sediments. A disturbed zone at the top, the result of gravelling activities, overlies a plow zone and relatively thin A-horizon. The red argillic B-horizon has hues in the 5YR range. There is a possibility that a change in the depositional character occurs between the B2t horizon and the B3-horizon (Figure 4.10;Table 4.5) . While there is an adequate increase in clay for the argillic designation, it is also clear that the parent materials below the B2t horizon appear to fine upwards. Therefore, the increase in clay and silt in the B3-horizon may be the result of depositional environments rather than subsequent soil formation. Analysis of the sand fractions from this profile indicates remarkably homogeneous particle size distributions from the A-horizon well down into the C-horizon, suggesting homogeneous parent materials (Table 4.4). Overall, the lithologic and pedogenic character of this profile is very similar to that described at the Wheeler Profile. Valley View Section The third section described in the Union Terminal terrace is located approximately 500 meters north of Valley View Lane and 400 meters east of the Elm Fork channel. This locality is situated about 2 miles west of downtown Farmers Branch. The profile described is situated on a long remnant of the original terrace surface, which is bounded to the north and south by large gravel pits. The profile described occurs at an elevation of approximately 437 feet. As at the Wheeler and Denton Creek profiles, this elevation is only slightly above the floodplain in this vicinity. An archaeological site (4IDL240) is present in the upper portion of the terrace deposits. Although no artifacts ~ere exposed in the section described, artifacts, features, and fairly dense accumulations of artifacts occur just to the west of the described profile over a fairly extensive area of the remaining portion of the terrace. The section . described at the Valley View locality is just 75

## Page 82

over 4 meters deep (Figure 4.11; Table 4.6). It consists of several informally designated units of alluvium that overlie the Eagle Ford shale. The basal gravels (unit A) consist of fairly well-sorted limestone and hematite pebbles and cobbles, with interbedded lenses of coarse sand. These are overlain unit B sands. The basal sands exhibit well preserved primary structures, while the upper portion of unit B is bioturbated and massive. The basal part of unit B consists of medium to thickly bedded, tabular cross-stratified sands with thin pebble lags at the bases of some of the beds. These in turn are overlain by trough cross-stratified sands and these in turn by another unit of more thinly bedded plane or very grossly trough cross-stratified sands. The zone of bioturbation in the upper part of Unit B is quite marked by burrows. The massive character of the sands, as well as the evident burrows, suggests that there may have been a period of surface stability at this point, and therefore, at the 2-meter depth of this profile, a break is designated between Units Band C. Unit C consists of sandy loam to sandy clay loam sediments. These are surmounted by a thin clay loam deposit, which is either a very recent flood deposit or perhaps the result of disturbance associated with the sand and gravel quarrying activities. A soil has formed on the unit C parent materials. This includes a relatively thin and possibly truncated sandy loam A-horizon with a well developed argillic horizon below. The colors in the B-horizon are in the 5YR hues; the pronounced increase in clay content between the AB and BIt horizons (approximately 10%) at first suggested a change in parent materials. Examination of the particle size distribution within the sand fraction (Table 4.4) indicates there is no change in the composition of sands between these horizons. However, farther down in the B-horizon, the fining of the sands is evident. The maximum amount of clay occurs in the lower portion of the B-horizon (B3t). This pattern is typical of B-horizons formed in Ultisols [Soil Taxonomy 1975]. Ultisols, which are common on Quaternary deposits in the southeastern United states, are also characterized by heavily leached A-horizons and argillic B-horizons that have abrupt upper surfaces and a tendency for maximum elluviated clay to occur in the lower portions of these horizons. It is entirely possible, therefore, that the A- and B-horizons for this soil have formed on the same parent material and that the textural variations in these horizons is in part the result of the upwards fining character of the alluvium, but is dominated by the post-depositional effects of pedogenesis. 78

## Page 86

Summary: The Union Terminal Terrace The reconnaissance investigations and profile descriptions of the Union Terminal terrace of Taggart has indicated three strikingly similar profiles. Each contains basal gravels and cross-stratified sands overlain by fining upwards alluvium. A well-developed soil occurs in each of the terrace deposits investigated. The red argillic horizons vary in character to a certain degree, yet these differences are certainly expected among soils that have formed on alluvium. Lateral variations in the depositional environments associated with rivers is inevitably going to result in different parent materials for soils that have formed under similar environmental conditions and over comparable periods of time. While considerably more work is clearly needed in investigating these terrace deposits, the homogeneity of the character of these three profiles is striking. The remaining questions revolve about the age of the alluvial deposits and the period of time during which the soil has formed after deposition on this terrace ceased. The occurrence of archaeological materials near the Wheeler profile and near the Valley View profile are restricted to the A-horizon. This position is probably identical to that described for the Wheeler site [Crook and Harris 1953] in which artifacts ascribed to the ·Carrollton Focus· were found in the "Patillo Formation" sands that occurred above the red "Albritton Formation" clays. The three terrace profiles investigated here strongly suggest that the "Albritton/Patillo" formations are one in the same with the A- and B-horizons of the soil described here. The fact that artifacts found in the "Patillo sands" range from late Paleoindian or Early Archaic in age to those of Late Archaic age at site 4lDL240 [Lorrain, personal communication] suggest that this surface has been stable for as much as 10,000 years, if not more. Over this period of time, weathering effects have resulted in the formation of a leached A-horizon typical of many Ultisols. The A-horizon then may be the result simply of pedogenic modification of the original parent material after this floodplain was abandoned. The possiblity that some deposition of younger materials on top of this terrace has taken place must clearly be entertained. It is indeed possible that some younger alluvium occurs on top of the "Union Terminal" terrace. Additionally, it is possible that eolian sands may have been deposited on truncated B-horizons. The data collected from the three profiles on this study, however, do .not clearly point towards separate depositional episodes for the A- and B-horizons respectively of these soils profiles. Admittedly, this is one of the most challenging areas of soils/stratigraphic investigations. 82


---

## Page 87

Fluvial environments are prone to generate laterally and vertically differentiated parent materials for soils that may have formed over the same intervals of time. An additional area that requires further investigation concerning these terrace deposits is the age and depositional environments of the basal gravels and sands. Whether these represent deposits that were laid down just prior to the overlying fine-grained alluvium or whether they are perhaps truncated basal portions of considerably older terraces is a matter of both stratigraphic and archaeological significance. The profiles just described can be interpreted as vertical changes in facies with subsequent pedogenic modification of the sediments. In other words, the breaks between the sands with primary structures and the overlying fine-grained alluvia may be looked at as minor erosional boundaries or simply gradations between depositional faces. On the other hand, the Valley View profile exhibited bioturbation of the upper sands, suggesting a period of some surface stability and/or possible truncation of older deposits prior to deposition of the fine-grained sediments comprising the upper part of this terrace. It has been noted earlier that with the exception of Willimon's study [1970; 1972] no datable faunal materials have been found in the ~Union Terminal" or "Carrollton" terraces. If a tentative and cautious correlation is made between the Union Terminal terrace of Taggart and the "Tl" of Willimon, then these basal sands and gravels may be approximately 20,000 years old. Independent evidence for the age of the terraces in the Carrollton/Farmers Branch area is clearly needed owing to downstream changes in the composition and geomorphology of terraces, as well as the important expansions and constrictions in the floodplain which were noted earlier. These latter effects may significantly alter the character of aggradation and downcutting phases within and between portions of the valley that vary markedly in width. At present, it must be concluded that the upper surface of the Union Terminal terrace is at least 10,000 years old and possibly 20,000 years old based on Willimon's dates. Investigation of the Carrollton Terrace Taggart [1953] stated that the Carrollton terrace in his scheme was both geomorphically and lithologically distinct from the higher Union Terminal terrace. Our brief investigations of this geomorphic/stratigraphic unit bears out his description. The Carrollton terrace in the Carrollton/Farmers Branch area is barely discernable from the floodplain in terms of elevation. At 83


---

## Page 88

most it rises 5 or 6 feet above the floodplain in the areas in which we investigated. However, on topographic maps a rise to somewhat higher elevations is indicated in areas we did not investigate. A section was described in the Carrollton terrace just below Sandy Lake Road on the west bank of the Elm Fork (Figure 4.12; Table 4.7). This is the locality of archaeological site 41DL12, which was recorded in the 1940s in the walls of the gravel pit that are still present [Kirkland et al. 1949] The section has been divided into three informal units that overlie the Eagle Ford shale. unit A consists of basal gravels. unit B consists of massive or cross-laminated sands, and Unit C consists of loamy sands and sandy loams. A very thick A-horizon has formed on the upper portion of this terrace. It is likely that some deposition took place concurrent with the formation of this soil making this a cumulic A-horizon. Artifacts occurring in this profile range in the age from Late Archaic through Late Prehistoric. These archaeological data, and a radiocarbon age of 820 +/- 80 B.P. (Beta-14907) from the upper portion of the soil A-horizon indicate deposition between ca. 2500 and 800 B.P. An increase in clay, a change in structure and color led to the designation of the upper part of Unit B as a buried argillic soil horizon (IIBltb). This designation implies a relatively stable surface for some time prior to the deposition of Unit C sands. The possibility that some change in parent material characterizes this IIBltb horizon must be entertained, however, for abrupt changes in clay content are noted below this horizon. The character of this profile is considerably different from that of the Union Terminal profiles just described. The examination of other sections of the Carrollton terrace confirm that this general lithologic and soils sequence appears to be more widespread in this area. Sand and gravel pits located along Interstate Highway 35 near the point where the Elm Fork crosses that highway are a number of sections exposed in the pits exhibit stratigraphic/soils profiles which are quite comparable to that at 4lDL12. Each of these exposures consists of 4 to 5 meters of alluvium above the eroded Eagle Ford shale. A section described at one pit just east of Interstate 35 exhibits approximately 3.5 meters of alluvium (Figure 4.13). Basal gravels are overlain by yellowish-red calcareous silts and clays, and these in turn are overlain by thick black clays. At this section, bovid bones and flakes are eroding from the lower portion of the A Holocene age for the clays, then, is strongly suggested. On the opposite side of the borrow pit, a relatively continuous horizon of firecracked rock, lithic debitage and several tools are exposed at a depth of approximately 2.5 to 3 meters below the surface. A thick buried 84

## Page 93

soil A-horizon is present in the upper part of these upper sediments, which are underlain by pale, massive sandy clays. This section appears to be the stratigraphically thickened equivalent of the dark clays exposed on the previously described portion of this borrow pit. These two exposures suggest, but certainly do not confirm that Late Holocene-aged alluvia are inset within and to some degree overlap older alluvium which comprises the major portion of the Carrollton terrace fill. As suggested by Taggart's map (Figure 4.3), the contact between the Carrollton terrace and the modern floodplain is very subtle topographically. The Carrollton terrace appears to be a unit that is lithologically distinct from the floodplain sediments. Considerably more work is required on these exposures, however, and every effort should be made to acquire datable materials from these alluvial deposits. Investigations of the Elm Fork Floodplain Our reconnaissance efforts were, in the main, unsuccessful in providing us with exposures of the floodplain sediments. A reconnaissance of the Elm Fork channel by boat certainly seems to be one of the most promising means to find such sections for description. Perhaps the most important evidence recovered concerning the character of the sediments underlying the Elm Fork floodplain was that described earlier in the context of the borehole logs from the Lewisville Darn and the White Rock Creek area. These clearly indicate that floodplain sediments attain thicknesses of 60 to 70 feet, and therefore even vertical cut banks in the moderate channel will only expose the upper half of these deposits. Indeed, determining the age of the sediments underlying the floodplain stands as perhaps one of the most compelling problems facing archaeological investigations along the Trinity. The great depth of these alluvial sediments alone suggests that they may be of considerable age and may contain very deeply stratified archaeological materials. At site 4lDL12, floodplain clays are inset against the Carrollton terrace deposits described above. These dark black massive clays contain an archaeological horizon buried approximately 3 meters below the surface. This contains sparse artifacts but also several hearths and very well-preserved mammal bones and clam shells. Charcoal from this horizon yielded a radiocarbon age of 1140 +/- 70 B.P. (Beta-14906). Somewhat farther up Denton Creek from this locality is an archaeological site (41DLll) situated on the floodplain just east of Denton Tap Road. This site has been the subject of avocational investigation for some time [Lorrain, personal communication] and 89


---

## Page 94

contains a deeply buried sequence of archaeological materials. The upper portion of the site exhibits thick organic rich clays at a depth of approximately 2.5 to 3 meters. These organic rich clays grade downwards into yellowish-gray clays. Bison bones and artifacts are eroding from the upper portion of the site, while at greater depths down to the upper portion of the yellowish-gray clays abundant hearths, mussel shells, and mammal bones along with artifacts dated to the Late Archaic have been recovered. The proximity of this locality relative to the Elm Fork indicates that aggradation of Denton Creek was controlled at this point by the Elm Fork master stream. Therefore, the accumulation of up to 3 or 4 meters alluvium in the last 2,000 to 4,000 years is indicated. A profile in this archaeological site will be described pending permission from' the landowners. However, a section was briefly described in the deposits closer to the Denton Creek channel (Figure 4.14). This section of just over 4 meters of alluvium contains no archaeological materials. The AIC soil profile at the top is perhaps indicative of the young age of these sediments, while relatively unweathered silts and sands beneath the modern soil suggest levee type depositional environments. These appear to grade laterally away from the modern channel into fine-textured silty clays, suggestive of a floodbasin environment. . The levee-like deposits may represent either a relatively fixed channel position over the last 2 or 3 millennia, or they may represent much younger inset fill within the floodplain. The former situation would suggest a stability of channel positioning, while the latter would indicate meandering of Denton Creek in recent times. Both scenarios are entirely feasible and radiometric dating would be needed to correlate these deposits with those in the nearby archaeological site. It is perhaps significant, however, that in this vicinity the Denton Creek channel is quite straight with no well developed meander loops. Thus, it is possible that depositional environments have not shifted laterally over the last several millenia during which time the floodplains of Denton Creek and Elm Fork were actively aggrading. Investigation of several archaeological sites in the Farmers Branch area along the Elm Fork also confirm that aggradation was actively proceeding during Late Archaic times. A number of Late Archaic sites occur at depths of 1 to 2 meters below the surface of the floodplain, and in some cases later prehistoric materials occur stratigraphically above these. A particular concentration of archaeological materials is tentatively suggested by survey results in the area of NB Houston Golf Course [Hays et al. 1972]. This may possibly signify some change in deposition rates near Late Archaic times, yet adequate dating of these deposits has not 90

## Page 96

been accomplished. The surface of the floodplain has been relatively stable, with the exception of meandering, for the last few hundred years as indicated by radiocarbon dated Late Prehistoric materials that are shallowly buried on the floodplain. Archaeologically, however, it is important to point out that this was only relative stability, and that overbank deposition of clays still is ongoing, and therefore many sites even of Late Prehistoric age have a high probability of being buried in these contexts. Investigations on the West Fork of the Trinity More limited reconnaissance investigations were undertaken on the West Fork of the Trinity. These revealed some apparent similarities and differences from the terrace and floodplain deposits on the Elm Fork. A schematic section drawn across the West Fork of the Trinity near Highway 157 appears to be representative of much of the West Fork. A high terrace, standing some 50 feet above the floodplain, contains deeply stratified, crossbedded sands and gravels overlain by fining upwards alluvial deposits. At the top of these deposits is a well-developed soil with a thick red argillic horizon overlain by a massive fine sandy A-horizon. This terrace has been mapped as "T2" by Taggart. At elevations of approximately 10 feet above the modern floodplain are exposures of a terrace mapped as either the Carrollton or the Union Terminal by Taggart [1953]. This lower terrace (or terraces) have not been adequately studied on the West Fork, and correlation with Taggart's geomorphic units is neither possible nor perhaps advisable at this time. An overall similarity with the Union Terminal or Carrollton terraces of Taggart is suggested however by the limited exposures seen on this reconnaissance. Near Hwy. 157, gravel pit exposures of this terrace indicate a thick red argillic horizon overlain by massive grayish sands remarkably similar to the soils developed on the Union Terminal terrace along the Elm Fork. Numerous exposures of the West Fork floodplain deposits are provided in gravel pits and channel cuts in the middle reaches of the reconnaissance area. These all indicate the presence of a buried soil between one half and as much as 2 meters below the present surface of the floodplain. This soil was not seen in all sections of the cutbank during a helicopter flyover, but it nonetheless appears more or less continuously from at least Ft. Worth to near Grand Prairie. The section described below provides a documentation of this soil. 92


---

## Page 97

The Gateway Park Profile A section of approximately 9 meters of alluvium is exposed in Trinity River channels near the proposed site of Gateway Park. The section described is located on a meander cutbank of the West Fork of the Trinity approximately 450 meters south of the First street bridge. The profile is located on the east bank of the river. The thick sequence of alluvium exposed in this cutbank has been divided into four stratigraphic units (Figure 4.15; Table 4.8). The basal unit, A, is comprised of medium to thickly bedded sands and loamy deposits, rich in mollusks, including gastropods and' unionids. A charcoal sample was collected from a depth of approximately 8 meters and has been submitted for accelerator radiocarbon dating. Overlying these coarser deposits of unit A is a 5-meter thick unit that varies little in texture and color. It is predominantly silty clay loams fining upwards slightly to clay loams. These fine-grained deposits are also rich in gastropods although no clams were seen in this section. The upper part of unit B is the C-horizon for the overlying soil. The buried soil here is informally named the West Fork paleosol. These silty clays and silty clay loams are very dark, organic rich and exhibit well-developed prismatic structure. While there is more clay in the lower portion of this soil A-horizon, this is interpreted at present as a change in parent material rather than illuviation of clays. The parent materials are calcareous silts and clays. Downward translocation of carbonates is evident and a Cca-horizon occurs below the A-horizon. A radiocarbon age of 1510 +/- 100 B.P. (Beta-14904) was obtained on humates from the upper 10 cm of the West Fork paleosol. The age confirms the late Holocene age for soil formation, and indicates the soil was buried by renewed alluviation in late Prehistoric times. The upper surface of the soil is truncated and is capped by the loamy deposits shown in Figure 4.15. These contain abundant mollusks, bovid bones, and one well-defined hearth that appears to be of cultural origin, although no artifacts have been found in this section. Formation of a Cca horizon associated with the modern soil has taken place; these deposits may well be of late Prehistoric age. The informally named West Fork paleosol described in this section is the same soil that occurs in many outcrops downstream from this locality. Its presence upstream has not been investigated. This buried soil is probably contemporaneous with the thick A-horizon soil described in the Carrollton area of the 93

Elm Fork; however, in the Carrollton area that soil is at the surface and therefore is not a paleosol, but rather a relict soil. Recent investigations along the upper Elm Fork of the Trinity have documented the widespread occurrence of a buried paleosol on the floodplain. At one section, a radiocarbon age of 1370 +/- 60 B.P. (Beta-14908) was obtained. This evidence confirms the regional significance of the West Fork paleosol a an environmental and stratigraphic marker horizon. That late Holocene alluvial deposits are buried by Late Prehistoric sediments has profound implications for site surveys and settlement pattern studies as well. Late Quaternary Geology of the Upper Trinity Basin: Synthesis Based on a review of previous investigations as well as the results of the current study, the following interpretive summary of the Late Quaternary history of the Upper Trinity Basin is offered (Figure 4.16). There are certainly numerous shortcomings to such a synthesis, most of which may only be resolved by additional detailed geologic investigations. Among the most outstanding problems facing such summaries are the lack of available radiometric dates on alluvial units, the lack of multiple stratigraphic sections and soil profile analyses within the basin, and the the lack of independent evidence concerning paleo-climates and paleoenvironments necessary in order to interpret the depositional history of the Trinity during Later Quaternary times. The following summary of events and conclusions is restricted to the deposits that occur below the "T2" terrace of Crook and Harris [1957J. This decision has been made following earlier discussions concerning the probable mid-Wisconsin to possibly Sangamon age for the "T2" alluvial deposits. Abandonment of the "T2" Terrace Sometime after Sangamon or some younger Wisconsin time, the "T2" terrace was abandoned. Incision of the floodplain to bedrock followed. Genesis of the "Union Terminal Terrace Complex" During and/or following incision to bedrock, the "Union Terminal" terrace complex formed. Given the apparent variations 97


---
