## Page 103

( in the elevation of surfaces above the modern floodplain, two possible scenarios (among other alternatives) are envisioned for the genesis of this terrace complex: A. The "Union Terminal Terrace Complex" may have accumulated as a result of alluviation contemporaneous with gradual incision of the valley. This is analogous to Brackenridge's [1984J ingrown meadering river process. By such a mechanism alluvium is laterally inset against older alluvium resulting in a sloping terrace profile with progressively younger deposits and a progressively younger surface with decreasing elevation. This mechanism may explain the sloped expression of the Union Terminal terrace and the observations made on this project of increasingly mature soil profiles at higher elevations on this terrace surface. B. The "Union Terminal Terrace Complex" may be the result of a single event of abandonment of a relatively level floodplain followed by sheet" erosion and a resulting sloped terrace profile. In either event, the maturity of soil profiles on the Union Terminal terrace deposits suggests considerable antiquity for these sediments. Other than Taggart's dates (discussed below), no reliable evidence concerning the age of the· Union Terminal terrace deposits has been acquired. Artifacts approximately 10,000 years old occur on this terrace, but at this time may not be considered in situ in these alluvial deposits. The Carrollton Terrace The Carrollton Terrace is interpreted as either (1) younger continuation of the "ingrown meandering" deposition of the Union Terminal terrace or (2) an alluvial unit inset against the older Union Terminal deposits. On the basis of lithology, it is not possible to distinguish between these two possiblities, nor are there known sections where the contact between these two units may be described. The dates acquired by Willimon [1970, 1972] from the "Tl" deposits are ambiguously related to the Union Terminal-Carrollton deposits of Taggart [1953]. In terms of elevation with respect to the modern floodplain, Willimon's stratigraphic descriptions imply a closer correlation with the lower (Carrollton) deposits. Accordingly, it is tentatively concluded that either the lowest and youngest of the Union Terminal deposits and/or some of the Carrollton deposits were aggrading approximately 23,000 to 20,000 years ago. 99


---

## Page 104

Abandonment of the Lower Trinity Terraces The lower portions of the Union Terminal terrace and the Carrollton terrace were abandoned, and deep incision of the Trinity into bedrock ensued. The best estimate for when this deep incision of the Trinity took place must be provided by Willimon's dates on alluvium that stands approximately 15 to 30 feet above the bedrock bench that was incised some 30 to 40 feet during this erosional episode. Cross sections at the Lewisville Dam and below Dallas indicate that the lower terraces of the Trinity appear to be restricted to the areas underlain by the shallow bedrock bench. All of the fill of the deeply incised trench in the Trinity valley appears to be younger alluvium. The young dates for this erosional event are only available in the form of quite deeply buried, Late Archaic materials on the floodplain of Denton Creek near its confluence with the Elm Fork (see discussion below). The most plausible explanation, given available evidence, is that this deep incision of the Trinity took place sometime after 20,000 years BP. Additionally, it may be concluded that the erosional event was complete and the deeply incised bedrock valley had been filled with alluvium, with Elm Fork floodplains within 3 to 4 meters of their present elevations by Late Holocene times. It is difficult to avoid the conclusion that abandonment of the lower Trinity terraces and incision of the Trinity into bedrock corresponds with the maximum depression of sea levels during late Wisconsin (full glacial) "times. Terminal Pleistocene and Early Holocene Aggradation Although no radiocarbon ages nor any full stratigraphic sections are available for this time period, it is suggested that alluviation within the deeply incised bedrock channel of the Trinity took place during terminal Pleistocene and Early Holocene times. If this scenario is later corroborated by borehole data and radiometric ages, then it is evident that the only sediments and surfaces amenable to easy field inspection, which date to the Late Pleistocene/Early Holocene periods, are those stable surfaces of the lower Union Terminal and/or Carrollton terraces that were exposed during the entire downcutting phase and during the earlier phases of subsequent aggradation. Middle and Late Holocene Alluvial Events other than stable surfaces on the lower terraces, continued aggradation during Middle and Late Holocene times implied by the available evidence. Archaic sites on the Elm Fork and nearby Denton Creek provide tentative evidence that active alluviation was in progress during Late Holocene times. Floodplain stability 100


---

## Page 105

was attained after these sites were formed as implied by the buried West Fork paleosol on the West Fork of the Trinity and by the seemingly common occurrence of Late Archaic materials at 1 to 1.5 meters depth on the Elm Fork floodplain (see later sections in this report). Additionally, Late Holocene alluvium containing Late Archaic through Late Prehistoric materials appears as a thin veneer over apparently truncated portions of the Carrollton (=truncated Union Terminal?) terrace. Discussion The preceding summary of the Late Quaternary history of the Trinity has been made with hopefully clear recognition of its limitations. These limitations define the need for further work on the Trinity. This will inevitably entail consideration of geologic and paleoenvironmental data in the Trinity Basin as a resource base pertinent to the archaeological history of this area. Both as contexts for archaeolgical sites and as primary evidence for past environments to which native populations adapted, these data are jeopardized along with the archaeological sites themselves. In particular, the following kinds of information are necessary in order to develop a chronology of events and past environments in the Upper Trinity Basin: (1) detailed borehole transects in areas of deep alluvium in order to define possible Late Pleistocene and Holocene erosional/depositional events; (2) description of multiple sections including sediments and soils on the lower terraces (lower Union Terminal and Carrollton) in order to assist in reconstructing paleosurface histories as well as providing age controls on' the aggradation of these alluvial units; (3) recovery and study of paleontologic data from both low terrace and floodplain alluvium. Geoarchaeological Considerations Late Quaternary sediments and soils of the Upper Trinity Basin are the context for archaeological materials deposited by prehistoric populations that inhabited this area over the past 12,000 years. It is necessary and appropriate to consider these geologic contexts with respect to archaeological research strategies focussed on the late Pleistocene and Holocene cultural ecology of this area. It is not the purpose of this section to elaborate on the efficacy pf geoarchaeological research strategies per se [see Butzer 1982]. Rather, brief consideration of the ways in which these contexts are significant is presented, followed by evaluation of the main geomorphic-stratigraphic units in the study area as known and potential site contexts. Additional discussions in section 7 101


---

## Page 106

consider site formation processes as they relate to the detection and evaluation of sites. The geologic contexts of prehistoric sites in the study area are critical dimensions of the archaeological record of this area. Explicit consideration of these contexts is required in formulating a broad research strategy concerned with variability and change in the adaptive strategies of the prehistoric populations that inhabited the Trinity Basin. Both primary data and also logistical-epistemological concerns are involved. Sediments and soils are the matrix not only for archaeological sites, but also for entrained fossils (bone, shell, plant macrofossils, pollen, etc.) . The package of sediments and other data comprise the primary evidence for past environments in this region; it is evident that a paleoenvironmental record is essential for studying the adaptive strategies and evolution of cultural systems in this area. At the same time our ability to generate a record of serial events and environmental contexts requires that a sound chronological framework emerge through dating of stratigraphic units, episodes of soils formation and erosional episodes. The prospects for building such a chronology lie in the continued geologic study of this area. Appraisals of past adaptive systems will be conditioned not only by primary data (relevant to both archaeological and paleoenvironmental dimensions) but also in terms of our ability to monitor differential modification of the archaeological record and differential exposure of archaeological sites within the study area. These logistical-epistemological issues are not insignificant with regard to archaeological research; quite the contrary, variability in both intersite and intrasite data may be more strongly conditioned by differential modification and exposure (detectability) than by the 'unmodified' archaeological evidence itself. The following discussions present geoarchaeological appraisals of each of the principal geomorphic-stratigraphic units defined for the Upper Trinity Basin. Of particular concern are site formation histories, site detectability and preservation potentials, and resultant implications in terms of research strategies that can improve site evaluations as well as contribute to regional research objectives. "T2" Terrace The great antiquity of these terrace deposits indicate that this terrace is a landform that had been in place long before settlement of this regions by Paleoindian populations. As a site forming context, the surface of this terrace offered potential locations .for occupations throughout the late Pleistocene and Holocene. During that interval, artifacts and features could have been deposited on the surface of the terrace, or along gullies cut into the "T2" sediments. The Lewisville cross-section (Figure 102


---

## Page 107

4.6 ) indicates perched water tables associated with this terrace. Seep springs along the terrace may have provided a focus for hunter-gatherer groups throughout prehistoric times. That these deposits have been stable or eroding over the last 12,000 years indicates generally poor site formation contexts. Sites should exhibit high potentials for erosion, mixing of artifacts of different ages and in situ deposits only along gullies or creek channels incised into the "T2" alluvium during late Pleistocene and Holocene times. Calcareous silts and clays in the upper portion of the "T2" terrace may provide locally favorable settings for bone preservation. Site visibility should be high yet strongly conditioned by local vegetation and colluvium depth. Union Terminal-Carrollton Terraces Numerous archaeological sites occur on these terraces along the Elm Fork and Trinity Rivers, and a few have been reported from these settings along the West Fork. Preceding discussions have concluded that these terraces are Pleistocene features, and therefore have little if any potential for archaeological materials buried in situ in alluvium. Nonetheless, buried materials have been found on these terraces, provoking the notion that alluviation of these terraces took place in the last 12,000 years. of concern therefore are the mechanisms that may account for burial of Holocene artifacts on Pleistocene surfaces. Two kinds of mechanisms will be discussed here: exogenetic deposition and bioturbation. In fluvial basins, exogenetic deposition refers to the introduction of sediment to the basin by non-fluvial mechanisms. These include eolian transport, slopewash (colluvium) and mudflows [Gladfelter 1977 Lewin 1978]. With respect to archaeological site construction, local redeposition of sediment may be significant. Sites on the Union Terminal-Carrollton terraces are subject to non-alluvial burial by these exogenetic mechanisms. On portions of these terraces adjacent to higher terrace or bedrock slopes, sites may be buried by colluvium. Land clearing and farming in the last 150 years may have hastened local slopewash, enhancing burial of sites. These activities have also promoted eolian redeposition on these terraces in historic times. Holocene eolian deposits have not been defined in the project area, but may be present. Such deposits would suggest considerable removal of vegetation from terrace surfaces, exposing sands and silts to deflation. Bioturbation agents also may account for buried archaeological materials on the Union Terminal-Carrollton Terraces. Rodents, other small animals, earthworms and especially insects are capable of bringing substantial amounts of subsoil to the surface each year [Wood and Johnson 1978; Stein 1983]. To the extent that small particles are transported selectively, larger 103


---

## Page 108

artifacts will gradually become buried through this vertical cycling of sediment. Features such as rock lined hearths can remain physically intact during sediment cycling. As a result, the site appears to be 'in situ' yet no allochthonous deposition has taken place. For relatively young, single component sites, this scenario has interesting connotations for geologic and archaeological studies. Well preserved assemblages, features and faunas may occur that exhibit little horizontal displacement of artifacts. Geologically, these settings only pose problems if the sediments covering the artifacts are interpreted as alluvium. This can result in erroneous alluvial chronologies to say the least. Given repeated occupations, these site formation scenarios are prone to generate 'in situ' yet badly mixed assemblages. Such sites probably account for common mistakes in projectile point 'chronologies' . ultisols on the Union Terminal-Carrollton terraces, described earlier in this section, have provided settings for bioturbated sites to form along the Trinity. These soil profiles, characterised by leached, sand-textured A-horizons over red argillic B-horizons, frequently exhibited insect burrows in the profiles. Since these soils formed over 10,000 years or more, the terrace surfaces have been potential occupation surfaces over the same interval. Bioturbation subsequent to site occupations could therefore account for the 'burial' of artifacts and features on these old terrace deposits. Overall, the Union Terminal-Carrollton terraces constitute stable surface settings for archaeological site formation. As proposed earlier, these surfaces have been the loci for occupations from Paleoindian through historic times. Individual site histories differ according to the number of occupation episodes as well as the local history of erosion, bioturbation and exogenetic deposition. Potentials for mixed archaeological deposits are high. The sand A-horizons of the soils are very porous and acid, promoting rapid bone deterioration and probably inhibiting preservation of charcoal as well. Mixing of artifacts into the underlying clay-rich B-horizons through bioturbation is likely to occur, especially for older sites. Marked declines in artifact densities at the A-B soil horizon contacts should betray this. Sites which have been occupied episodically over long intervals of time may exhibit .serial bioturbation', that is the progressive burial of artifacts over time, with apparent superpositioning of younger assemblages over older ones. While archaeological successions in such sites may be in correct relative order, the potential to establish absolute chronologies for these sites through radiometric dating is low. Overall, the archaeological research potential of sites on these terraces should be proportional to their age, when preservation of spatial patterning, faunas and datable materials are considered. However, late Pleistocene through middle Holocene alluvia are probably deeply buried under the Trinity floodplain, and the potential to 104


---

## Page 109

recover Paleoindian through middle Archaic assemblages in those contexts is minimal; accordingly, the surfaces of the Union Terminal-Carrollton terraces remain the most likely settings to recover materials from those time periods. Of necessity, these terraces must continue to be studied archaeologically. The Trinity Floodplain The floodplain of the Trinity River (encompassing the West Fork and Elm Fork as well) constitutes the most important geomorphic-stratigraphic unit with respect to archaeological contexts in the study area. Earlier discussions have stressed that the great depth of alluvium underlying the floodplain will encumber geoarchaeological investigation of sites within floodplain sediments. Nonetheless, available evidence suggests that floodplain deposits have been aggrading since the end of the Pleistocene and many if not most occupation sites in the Trinity Valley were probably located along the floodplain. At this time it is not possible to reconstruct the exact 'nature and tempo of floodplain aggradation over this interval of time. The depth of deposits will make such reconstruction difficult. Possible episodes of floodplain stability or incision will be difficult to document unless the stratigraphic-soils evidence of these events is exposed in the upper portions of the floodplain sediments (ie. in modern channel cuts that attain maximum depths of about 35 feet). While dating and paleoenvironmental study of the deeply buried sediments under the floodplain are pressing issues, both paleoenvironmental and archaeological studies are most feasible in the upper sections of the floodplain. Site formation settings on the Trinity floodplain contrast markedly with those of the terraces. Lower apparent stream energies supplied fine-grained calcareous clays and silts to the floodplain. Net rates of sedimentation may be grossly estimated for these deposits, that attain thicknesses of ca. 20 m near Dallas and Lewisville. Assuming that the floodplain aggraded from the maximum incision of the valley within the last 15,000 to 10,000 years, a net rate of sedimentation is estimated to be ca. 0.13 - 0.10 cm/yr. This moderate rate of sedimentation (cf. Ferring in prep) is ideal for the superpositioning of archaeological materials, but not too rapid to promote frequent erosion of the floodplain surface. While these estimates clearly do not consider periods of relative stability or periods of floodplain incisiQn, they suggest favorable site formation environments were common, particularly in late Holocene times. Coupled with calcareous sediments offering excellent preservation environments, the Trinity floodplain constitutes an ideal setting to study archaeological sites. The 'rub' here is that this setting constitutes a poor place to discover archaeological sites because of burial depth. That the Trinity floodplain has not aggraded with the same 105


---

## Page 110

tempo during the Holocene is evident for the upper (late Holocene) portion of this record. The West Fork paleosol provides evidence for a period of floodplain stability beginning in late Archaic times and ending sometime just after about 1500-1350 B.P. At that time, the floodplain soil was buried, and renewed alluviation commenced. The geoarchaeological implications of this history are clear. Sites of late Archaic and older age are buried beneath younger alluvium over a great portion of the Trinity floodplain. Relative paleosurface stability during late Archaic times will have promoted the potential for more common archaeological sites in assciation with that buried surface, holding population sizes and occupational intensities constant (the latter are major assumptions, with little if any empirical basis at this time). Though difficult to detect, most archaeological sites along the Trinity floodplain have a high probability of significant records of subsistence, intrasite patterning, and activities. Geomorphic Settings of Prehistoric Sites The geomorphic settings of prehistoric sites in the Upper Trinity Basin study area have been summarized by estimated archaeological components (Table 4.9). Not all of these sites have been visited, and the settings for many have been defined through the use of site survey forms and Taggart's (1953) map (Figure 4.3). Given the tentative nature of evidence in support of the archaeological components, the high number of unclassified prehistoric sites, and the quite difficult task of estimating geologic contexts, this summary of site settings is highly prone to error. Nonetheless, it provides a needed focus for brief discussion of the geoarchaeological record that is present in the study area. This discussion is relevant to later site prediction and cultural resources management sections of this report. The sample of 73 prehistoric sites is characterized by an inverse relationship between site frequency and site age (Table 4.9), excluding the unclassified group. Does this mean that the Upper Trinity Basin was inhabited by larger populations in Late Prehistoric times than in earlier periods? Reference to the geoarchaeological discussion makes clear that (a) this question cannot be answered without adjusting site frequency for geologic context, and (b) the potential exposures of sediments containing earlier sites are too limited to enable 'weighted' comparisons. Late Prehistoric sites are expected to occur in floodplain settings, above the West Fork paleosol, and also on any of the terraces. Their detectability should be higher in these settings, compared to older sites. While the detectability 'factor' cannot be estimated, it could conceivably be large enough to thwart population estimates via site frequencies (see Section VIr for discussion of population estimates using inter 106


---

## Page 111

TABLE 4.9 GEOMORPHIC SETTINGS OF PREHISTORIC SITES a Geomorphic Unit To To/TS TS TS/T4 T4 T4/T3 Total Lpb 14 1 1 6 22 r-l I'd U l/l .r-i +J LP-A S 3 3 1 12 tJll=: o (1) r o -i 0 I=: A 2 1 3 (1)0.. co S ..c:o EA 1 1 2 uu ~ F:t: P 7 4 7 3 l3 34 Total 26 S 12 S 24 1 73 a Geomorphic units follow terminology of Taggart [1953J See Figure 4.3. b LP- Late Prehistoric; LP-A - Late Prehistoric/Archaic; A- Archaic; EA- Early Archaic; P- unclassified prehistoric 107

## Page 112

and intrasite data). An additional factor is chronological duration of the component periods. The "Archaic" period is several millennia long, while the Late Prehistoric period in all is shorter by an order of magnitude. The issue of 'how many sites per year' (a much better population estimator) is conditioned as strongly by geologic context as is the detectability (what percentage of the sites that exist have we found?) factor. If steady-state populations are assumed, the apparent frequencies of sites meet the geologic expectations in terms of burial and exposure of sites. Young sites are more common on the floodplain, while sites of all ages are found on the terraces (Paleoindian components are notably absent from the sample). In this context, it is important to note that each of the six Late Prehistoric sites in "T4" settings occur in the area of the Irving Quadrangle map. This area, encompassing the confluence of the West Fork and Elm Fork, as well as extremely intensive development, may be subject to complicating geologic-preservation factors. In order to reconstruct population dynamics, a much more detailed chronostratigraphy must be established, and the intrasite correlates of increasing or decreasing populations must be vigorously studied (see section VII). The settings of prehistoric sites in the study area also suggest overall parameters of site condition, given predicted site formation settings associated with the geomorphic units. Well over half of the sites occur on terraces, suggesting poor contexts. Yet a fair number of these are either Late Prehistoric or Late Prehistoric-Archaic in age. Since site condition is expected to be in part proportional to site age, terrace sites may well exhibit well preserved features and spatial patterning. At the same ti~e, Late Prehistoric sites on the floodplain may have been more vulnerable to plowing and other forms of disturbance. Sites with better preservation potentials are those in floodplain settings that occur at or below the West Fork paleosol. Several examples of these kinds of sites have been found in the central portion of the Elm Fork valley in the project area. As is mentioned in Section I of this report, there are very few archaeological sites known along the West Fork of the Trinity. This pattern contrasts dramatically with site 'densities' on the Elm Fork as well as tributaries to the Elm Fork. The low frequencies of West Fork sites is of potential significance to settlement pattern stUdies. Initially our review suggested that the West Fork's recent depositional history may be quite different from that of the Elm Fork. Now, however, it is clear that the West Fork paleosol has a lateral equivalent along the Elm Fork, and that late Holocene deposition following formation of this soil characterizes both drainages. Either low site numbers on the West Fork simply represent less survey (both avocational and associated with landuse impact assessment) or there are as yet unidentified environmental-geologic factors that 108


---

## Page 113

account for the observed pattern. The common exposures of the West Fork paleosol along the West Fork channel and in gravel pit walls will serve as a marker horizon for future surveys. Paleoenvironmental Considerations The late Pleistocene and Holocene paleoenvironments of the Upper Trinity Basin are of critical importance for interpreting geologic data and for assessing possible adaptive changes by prehistoric populations. As indicated by a recent comprehensive review of paleoenvironments in Texas [Bryant and Holloway 1985], the North Texas Region has one of the poorest data bases in terms of past environments in the state and broader region. Virtually no pollen data have been recovered from this area, save for records that are poorly dated or have posed analytical problems [Raab and Woosley 1982; Bryant and Holloway 1985: 64-65]. Paleontologic data permitting climatic-environmental reconstructions are notably absent. Willimon's [1972] vertebrate and invertebrate data are the youngest Pleistocene faunas published, and they date to ca. 20,000 - 23,000 B.P., making them of diminished relevance to archaeological considerations. Using paleoenvironmental data from localities quite distant from the project area, the following brief review of late Quaternary paleoenvironments outlines apparent patterns of climatic and environmental change in the North Texas region. Such considerations highlight the need for local data and the significance of these data with respect to archaeological records in this area. Full glacial (ca.20,000-15,000 B.P.) environments are not well known in this region, but are nonetheless clearly distinct from Holocene settings. Cool if not cold climates, increased precipitation and disjunct communities of plants and animals are characteristic. Conifer parklands, including spruce and pine among deciduous elements were present from Kansas to central Texas, and extended eastwards into the western portions of the Mississippi Valley [Bryant and Holloway 1985; Gruger 1973; Hafsten 1961; King 1973]. It is virtually certain that these parklands were present in the Upper Trinity Basin. Extinct megafaunas of this period, and smaller vertebrates that today occupy allopatric territories, suggest biotic associations having no modern counterpart [Lundelius 1976]. Increased precipitation, possibly coupled with decreased evaporation rates are significant with respect to fluvial regimes; the latter must have been characterized by greater stream energies, given the evidence in the Upper Trinity Valley for incision during this interval. Detailed analysis of new borings will be necessary to document those fluvial environments. 109


---

## Page 114

The late glacial period (ca. 15,000 - 10,000 B.P.) witnessed the demise of the moist climates of the full glacial, significant changes in vegetation, and the extinction of megafaunas. Towards the end of this period, warmer grasslands with only relict members of the conifer woodlands are evident in central Texas [Bryant 1977], southwestern Oklahoma [Wilson 1966] and southeastern Oklahoma [Bryant and Holloway 1985]. The pollen record from Ferndale Bog, in southeastern Oklahoma (Figure 4.17) documents the replacement of the late glacial parkland by a grass and forb-dominated steppe shortly after 12,000 B.P.. A corresponding increase in grasses is recorded somewhat later than this in central Texas at Boriack Bog (Figure 4.17). Together, these records suggest that the Upper Trinity Valley must have undergone similar vegetational changes. The distance of pollen records from the study area nonetheless encumbers reconstruction of the timing and character of such changes. Overall, however, the shift towards warmer climates and significantly more open grasslands is important with respect to Paleoindian adaptations. Holocene environmental conditions appear to have been characterized both by successional changes in vegetation, and by responses to climatic change in the last 10,000 years by plant and animal communities. Today, older concepts and preconceptions concerning Holocene paleoenvironments are being challenged. New evidence suggests that arid mid-Holocene climates are difficult to document for the Texas region [Bryant and Holloway 1985]. Rather, this interval may be seen as one of transition from drier early Holocene environments to a late Holocene characterised by increasing moisture. Expansion of the oak-hickory forests of eastern Oklahoma after ca.6,000 B.P. may have implications with respect to the Upper Trinity Basin. A possible local response to this interval of gradual climatic change is expansion of the ·Cross Timbers at the expense of prairies. This would imply a potential increase in forest plant and animal resources. Such changes would have significant impacts on occupation potentials, which are presumed to have been relatively low during drier early Holocene times. A gradual trend of increasing moisture may also signify the potential for greater runoff and exhanced rates of sedimentation. Preceding discussions have indicated that Holocene aggradation of the Trinity Valley resulted in deposition of very thick alluvial deposits along the West and Elm Forks of the Trinity. Shifts towards moister climates are elsewhere correlated with periods of active alluviation [Knox 1983]. Late Holocene climates and environments have been studied with the benefit of better data in the southern Plains. Hall [1982] has indicated that the first millennium A.D. was characterized by moister climates than prevail today, while the last 1,000 years have been dominated by essentially modern climates. This record was clearly suggested by data from Delaware 110


---

---

## Page 116

Canyon in southwestern Oklahoma [Ferring 1982]. Locally, formation of the West Fork paleosol appears to have culminated during the middle of the first millennium A.D.. Whether this soil formed in response to climatic change, or was also part of a gradual attainment of graded streams remains to be documented. Pollen data from Ferndale Bog suggest that the trend towards increasing forest cover in southeastern Oklahoma was long-term. Better records from other portions of the southern Plains, in particular for the interval between 5,000 and 2,000 B.P. are needed to evaluate the relative change in climates during the late Holocene. Overall, there is regional evidence for major, long-term changes in climates and environments during the late Quaternary in this region. For the last 12,000 years, these have had important implications with respect to possible adaptive changes by prehistoric populations inhabiting the Upper Trinity Basin. Following introduction of Paleoindian populations into the area, resource bases changed, perhaps quantitatively as well as qualitatively. Extinction of Pleistocene megafaunas, coupled with expansion of grasslands under warmer and drier climates undoubtedly forced new adaptive strategies on the part of Early and Middle Archaic populations. Unfortunately, the alluvial history of the Trinity proposed earlier suggests that sites of this age will be extremely difficult to detect, encumbering archaeological studies of this period. Long-term increases in occupation potentials are suggested for the late Holocene, as a result of gradually increasing moisture regimes, which would have increased vegetation cover, in particular the forest curnrnunities of the Cross Timbers. It is tempting to suggest that these conditions were even more ideal during the period of ca. 2500 to 1200 years B.P. That climates were moister then is evident in contrast to modern climates; how they compare with climates during the preceding two millenia are less well known. Overall, however, the study of past environments and associated records of human adaptations in the study area remains a challenging and significant area for continued research. The paucity of well-documented records pertaining to both environments and cultures in this region signify the need to manage these resources in a manner that will ensure advances in our ability to reconstruct the events and their implications for both regional and theoretical issues. 112


---

## Page 117

SECTION V PREHISTORIC ARCHAEOLOGY by Cheri L. Douglass, Daniel J. Prikryl, C. Reid Ferring Introduction One of the principal aims of this investigation was a documentation and review of the prehistoric archaeological sites in the Upper Trinity Basin. Prior work suggested that prehistoric sites were far more numerous in the documented literature than were historic sites in the project area. Our investigations have confirmed this general observation. As described below, the long-term avocational interest in prehistoric sites, to a large degree, explains their more common documentation in the project area in deference to the historical sites. Nonetheless, our objectives here included documentation and evaluation of the prehistoric sites in the study area. Some 90 sites are known to occur in the Upper Trinity Basin project area. Given the status of prehistoric archaeological investigations in the Northcentral Texas region, this must be considered a substantial resource base for the reconstruction of past lifeways, the definition of adaptive strategies, and the multidisciplinary study of culture change throughout the terminal Pleistocene and Holocene Periods. This section of the report describes the archaeological sites, indicates the nature of previous investigations of these sites, and provides a theoretical and methodological rationale for evaluating the sites individually and collectively with respect to their research significance. These criteria are necessary for evaluation of archaeological sites and implementation of a cultural resources management program. Methods of Investigation As defined in the Scope of Work, a variety of methods have been used in order to document, describe, and evaluate the archaeological sites in the project area and to develop a framework from which their significance may be evaluated. First, an information search was conducted th~~ included investigation of site files at the Texas Archaeological Research Laboratory, the Texas Historical Commission, and local universities. Published reports concerning prehistoric archaeological investigations were reviewed, and local avocational and professional archaeologists who have worked in or near the project area were interviewed. 113


---

## Page 118

An additional aspect of our investigations included site visits. Numerous sites in the project area were visited in the field both as strictly archaeological visits and also when possible in order to accommodate archaeological and geologic objectives. The results of these site visits are included in the updated site files as well as the recommendations concerning possible future work at specific sites. One dimension of site evaluation is the aerial photograph analysis described in Appendix A. This analysis provided assistance in identifying the condition of archaeological sites that were not visited during the course. of this project, as well as the assessment of potential site preservation for areas that have not been previously inspected for site presence. Summary of Previous Investigations The organized study of archaeology in the Upper Trinity Basin began in the 1940's with the founding of the Dallas Archaeological Society (DAS). Much of our understanding of archaeology in this area stems from the efforts of this group. Over half of the sites in the project area were recorded in the early 1940s by the members of the DAS including Harris, Kirkland, Hatzenbuehler, McElroy and others. Reports on their archaeological investigations were published regularly in the DAS newsletter, The Record [for the list of publications concerning previous investigations, see Table 5.1J. During the late 1940s and early 1950s, the DAS participated in salvage work being conducted in area reservoirs, such as Lake Lavon and Lake Dallas, One result of avocational work along the East Fork was the discovery of large circular depressions in certaip sites [Wilson 1946J, which later became a diagnostic feature of the Wylie Focus [Stephenson 1952J. Also well-known in the 1950s was Crook and Harris' [1957, 1958, 1968] work at the Lewisville Site, 41DN72. Although members of the DAS continued to be active in the region, investigations of sites in the project area declined during the 1950s. No new sites were reportted in the project area during this time; however, Crook and Harris were publishing the results of their investigations of project area sites, particularly the Wheeler site (41DL30), the Wood Site (41DL76), and the Milton site (41DL259). The Carrollton and Elam foci were defined as a result of their work at these and other sites [Crook and Harris 1952, 1954a]. 114

## Page 125

The DAS continues to be active in our area, with several new sites reported by members beginning in the 1960s and continuing on into the 1980s. In all, over half the sites in the project area have been recorded by this group. Professional investigations began regionally in the late 1940s with surveys sponsored by the Smithsonian Institution at Grapevine [Stephenson 1948J, and Lavon and Garza-Little Elm [Stephenson 1949aJ and Benbrook [Stephenson 1949cJ reservoirs. No professional investigations on the Trinity and its tributaries in the project area occurred until the 19705. Since 1970, several surveys have been conducted by professional archaeologists as contract work. Some of these surveys have led to the discovery of new sites and allowed for continuing investigations of previously reported sites. other surveys have covered only small areas, and often no sites were discovered. A listing of surveys conducted within or bordering our project area is available in Table 5.2. The results of these surveys should be on file with the sponsoring agency, and all survey areas are plotted on topographical quadrangles on file with the Ft. worth Corps of Engineers. Despite the combined efforts of professionals and amateurs, much work remains to be done on this area of the Trinity. Almost all our information is from scattered surface finds or from artifacts found eroding from the walls of gravel pits. Very few sites have even been tested, and almost all testing that has been done was performed by members of the DAS. Only one site within the project area has been professionally excavated, Northlake Site (41DL234) [Morgan 1975J. One other site in this immediate region was professionally excavated, 41DN260 [McCormick et al. 1975J, but this site was not included in the project area. The need for more controlled investigations with defined research goals becomes clear when the nature of the previous investigations are considered. Culture History Four stages which have both developmental and temporal connotations have been defined for the North Central area of Texas [Suhm et al. 1954, Lynott 1977J. These stages are: Paleoindian ? to 8GOO B.P. Archaic 8000 B.P. to A.D. 500 Late Prehistoric A.D. 500 to 1600 Historic A.D. 1600 to present 121

## Page 128

The associated dates follow the generally accepted chronological framework; however, some overlapping of stages probably occurred. This section of the report deals only with the prehistoric stages. Paleoindian The Paleoindian period is characterized by small nomadic bands adapted to conditions that existed at the end of the Pleistocene. Although the Paleoindian economy has traditionally been characterized as primarily based on the exploitation of now extinct Pleistocene megafauna, solid evidence of a reliance on big game hunting is lacking in many areas of North America including North central Texas [Bryant and Shafer 1977, Johnson 1977]. The diagnostic artifacts for this period are the distinctive, finely worked lanceolate projectile points which are sometimes fluted (Figure 5.1). Within the immediate project area, our knowledge of Paleoindian occupations is based entirely on occasional surface finds of projectile points. Pemberton Hill Site is a possible Paleoindian site, located east of White Rock Creek near its confluence with the Trinity it was reported to contain biface fragments, a scraper and flakes in association with the bones of Pleistocene age bison, mammoth, and camel [Crook and Harris 1957; Archaeology Resource Consultants 1978]. The Lagow Sand Pit Site, west of White Rock Creek, contained a partially fossilized human skeleton associated with extinct Pleistocene fauna [Shuler 1923]. This was originally believed to provide evidence for a Paleoindian occupation, but later analysis showed that the faunal remains were older than the human skeleton [Oakley and Howells 1961]. Although the impression has been given that Crook considered this to be a Paleoindian site, his 1961 article actually stated that this skeleton was probably Early Archaic in age [cf. Archaeology Resource Consultants 1978; Bennett et al. 1981]. The Lewisville Site (41DN72), immediately north of our project area, is one of the more famous of the Paleoindian sites. At this site, a clovis point was found within one of twenty-two burned clay features that were interpreted as hearths [Crook and Harris 1957, 1958, 1968]. Pleistocene faunal materials occurred within and adjacent to these features. The site was originally dated at greater than 37,000 B.P., but more recent investigations have demonstrated that the presence of lignite in the features is responsible for these dates [Stanford 1981; Schiley et al. 1985]. 124


---

## Page 129

o 2 3cm I ! I • • a b c • • d e Figure 5.1 Characteristic Paleoindian Projectile Points a. Golondrina; b.,e. Plainview; c. Scottsbluff; d.,f. Clovis [Turner and Hester 1985; K. Roemer] 125


---

## Page 130

The Field Ranch site in Cooke County provides other evidence of the presence of Paleoindian hunters along the Elm Fork. This site is located north of the present project area. Seven projectile points, including Clovis, Folsom, Midland, Plainview, and Hell Gap types, were found by avocational archaeologists. Later, archaeologists from SMU exposed two hearths but did not find any associated diagnostic artifacts. Also recovered at the site were hammerstones, denticulates, a graver, a side-scraper, a gouge, and flakes [Jensen 1968]. Overall, we have very limited data on the presence of Paleoindian groups in our area. These few isolated finds do not provide evidence of how these people utilized their environment or what their patterns of movement were. The discovery and controlled excavation of good in situ sites~ would greatly increase our understanding of this period. Archaic Small bands of hunters and gatherers are also characteristic of the Archaic, but the movement of these bands is considered more restricted than in the Paleoindian period. Rather than following the migrations of large herd animals, movement followed a seasonal pattern so that locally available food could be harvested. A wider variety of plant and animal resources were exploited. Deer and smaller mammals were hunted, riverine resources were utilized, and the collections of plants was intensified. Stemmed dart points dominate the artifact assemblages, and the appearance of other artifacts, such as grinding stones, reflected changes in subsistence strategies. Other diagnostic artifacts for this period include pitted stones, Waco "Sinkers," and gouges (Figures 5.2 - 5.5). In his paper, The Archaic of Northcentral Texas, Olin McCormick [1976a] proposed that the-Archaic people of this area were migratory hunters and gatherers, moving along the Prairie/Cross Timbers ecotone. From their seasonal base camps, the resources of the prairies and the Woodlands could be exploited. Smaller activity-specific camps, such as hunting/fishing sites, lithic manufacturing stations, and transitory camps, were located well into either major ecosystem so that those resources could also be utilized. He speculated that these people were moving from the southern Plains area in the fall and then back north again in the early spring. Our knowledge of Archaic occupations in this area is somewhat more complete than for the Paleoindian period, but, as previously noted, it is based almost entirely on traits defined by lithic surface finds and artifacts eroded out of gravel and sand pits. 126

## Page 135

Only a few isolated lithic artifacts indicate the presence of Early Archaic groups in this area. The Middle and Late Archaic is somewhat better known due to the efforts of Crook and Harris [1952, 1954a,bJ who published several articles on the Carrollton and Elam foci. Based on stratigraphic relationships and comparative data from better known adjacent geographical areas, the Carrollton Focus is considered to the Middle Archaic, while the Elam Focus is considered to be Late Archaic [Crook and Harris 1952; Lynott 1977J. Middle Archaic assemblages include large triangular-bladed dart points with square-based stems, usually ground, such as Carrollton and Trinity type points. Also included are Carrollton axes, Waco Sinkers, Clear Fork-like gouges and burned clay blobs [Crook and Harris 1952J. Late Archaic sites were characterized by an increased use of local quartzites and smaller points, with the Elam, Ellis, Darl, Gary and Yarbrough points common [Crook and Harris 1952; McCormick 1976a; Lynott 1977J. The only date for the Archaic in the project area was 5,945 + 200 B.P. at the base of the Carrollton component at the Wood site (41DL76) [Crook and Harris 1959J. The date is not considered reliable because it is based on mussel shell rather than charcoal. Late Prehistoric Technologically, the Late Prehistoric is marked by the appearance of ceramics and the introduction of the bow and arrow. However, use of dart points may continue into the Late Prehistoric (Figure 5.6). A shift in settlement patterns from seasonally occupied camps to more permanently settled villages associated with temporary hunting camps is expected. In many areas this period is associated with agriculture; however, no evidence of agriculture has been found within the project area. The Late Prehistoric of the Upper Trinity is best known on the East Fork through the investigation of Wylie Focus sites [Wilson 1946; Stephenson 1952; Ross 1966; Lorrain and Hoffrichter 1968; Lynott 1975aJ. Sites which may be permanent camps or villages with the diagnostic feature of large circular pits have been recorded. The function of these pits is unknown, but hypotheses include ritual or dance areas or large house sites. Burials, sometimes with multiple interments and occasionally with grave goods, numerous hearths, a few 'animal burials, trash pits and shell concentrations have been associated with large pits. Radiocarbon dates indicate these pits were in use from 900 to 1600 A.D. A few houses, trash pits, hearths, and burials have been found in the habitation areas of these sites. Ceramics have been recovered and appear to be a mix of Caddoan types and Nocona Plain of the Henrietta Focus. . 131


---

## Page 136

a b c o 2 3 em t:1==:±:==±=:::::::ll d e f g h i Figure 5.6 Characteristic Late Prehistoric Projectile Points a.,c. Fresno; b. Harrell; d.,e. Alba; f. Washita; g.,i. Scallorn; h. Perdiz [Turner and Hester 1985, Illustrations by Kathy Roemer] 132


---

## Page 137

Small amounts of charred corn were recovered from the Butler Hole, Campbell's Hole, and Hogge Bridge sites [Stephenson 1952], but pollen analysis from the sister Grove Site does not yield any evidence of prehistoric agriculture [Lynott 1974]. As with earlier stages, our knowledge of Late Prehistoric occupations within the project area is insufficient. Two phases, early and late, have been noted, but these often occur together in mixed context [Lynott 1977]. The early phase is characterized by sand or grog-tempered ceramics associated with the Caddoan Gibson Aspect of East Texas. Diagnostic arrow point types include Alba, Scallorn and Rockwall and show resemblances to Gibson Aspect and the central Texas Austin Phase. An early phase Late Prehistoric date of AD 950 100 was obtained at Northlake Site (41DL234) [Morgan 1975; Lynott 1977]. The late phase partially correlates with the Henrietta Focus which was originally defined by Krieger [1946] based on excavations at the M.D. Harrell Site, located in Young County at the confluence of the Clear Fork and the Brazos River. Diagnostic artifacts include plain, shell-tempered Nocona Plain pottery and Fresno, Harrell, Washita, and Perdiz type arrow points. Dates for the late phase of the Late Prehistoric are 1575 + 145 from the West Wheeler Site (41DL30) [Harris 1959] and 1310-+ 120 from the Golf Course Site (4lDL35) [Harris, personal corom. in Lynott 1977]. The Northlake site provides the best evidence of a Late Prehistoric occupation in the project area. The lower levels appear to be a Late Archaic occupation, and an early phase Late Prehistoric occupation was present in the upper levels [Lynott 1977]. A total of twelve pottery sherds were recovered from levels two and three; six were decorated with incised single or parallel lines, and the rest were of a punctate design. The radiocarbon date 950 + 100 A.D. was obtained from charcoal in level three where the first appearance of pottery and worked bone artifacts were found and where an increase in ground stone was noted [Morgan 1975]. No pollen or artifactual evidence was found that would indicate horticulture was practiced. The ground stone artifacts were probably being used to process non-domesticated vegetal matter. Hunting and the gathering of aquatic fauna appeared to be the major subsistence activities throughout the occupation of this site [Morgan 1975]. Balsam Gourd Site (4lDL260) near Lewisville also seems to represent a late phase occupation [Lynott 1977]. One Harrell type point and 116 pottery sherds were recovered. It appeared to have been revisited periodically by small groups [McCormick et al. 1975] 133 •


---

## Page 138

While it is difficult to classify many Late Prehistoric sites into types due to lack of data, bison kill sites are more common in the Late Prehistoric. The presence of these sites has led Lynott's [1979] to theorize that bison may have been more plentiful in Northcentral Texas during the late phase of the Late Prehistoric period (1200-1600 A.D.). These sites are usually found along secondary drainages of the Trinity, perhaps since in these locations bison could be driven off bluffs into the creek valley [Skinner et al. 1978]. An example of this type site occurs in our project area near Denton Creek. Morris and Morris [1970] excavated a bison kill there that contained a small triangular point embedded between two of the ribs of the bison (Figure 5.7). Two vertebrae of the bison were deformed, and these were examined by Dr. George Race of Baylor Hospital. He concluded that some time before the animal's death it had been pierced, probably by the shaft of a spear, which had broken off, leaving part embedded between the two bones. Another kill site was reported on Dixon's Branch of White Rock Creek. Three small triangular points were found inside the rib cage of a bison [Harris and Harris 1970]. All of the information discussed so far has been for the Elm Fork and main Trinity Channel. The lack of data for the West Fork is overwhelming. Only six of our sites for the entire project area were from the West Fork, and five of these were of undetermined prehistoric age. Obviously, this area needs much more work. For all stages of the prehistoric period within our project area more research is needed to clear up the chronology. Most important, systematic excavations need to be performed, and more ·radiocarbon dates will have to be obtained before the chronology will become clear. Summary Although many sites are known in the Upper Trinity Basin, interpretations have been limited by the nature of the previous investigations. A comment in Skinner et al. 1978:23a that in this region Archaic culture groups have been defined primarily by artifact traits and not on the adaptive strategies utilized in their time period, applies to the whole of prehistory in this area. For this reason, as stated, a great deal of research will be needed before the chronology of this area will be understood, and only a few conclusions can be made generally about subsistence strategies, settlement patterns, and intrasite activities. 134


---

## Page 139

Limits of Excavation~ <]"------- \ '\1 ~ I-' W U1 To Creek )rili ~ ~1xJO~~ ~eJs"s'"es C~-:~_~ ~inous spirnus p I Q.A " process ts ----I \)'"" 't:t ~I m J '~ _ E _ dge of Bank projectile Figure 5.7 Plan of Bison Kill Site [Morris and Morris 1970J


---

## Page 140

One aspect of the subsistence strategy is clear, during both the Archaic and Late Prehistoric stages, riverine resources were being exploited. Over half the site reports noted the presence of mussel shell; however, Parmalee and Klippel [1974] have shown that these have a low caloric and protein value and should be viewed only as a dietary supplement. Very few sites contain other faunal materials, and in most cases the bone is too fragmentary to be identified. Species that have been identified include deer and small game, turtle and fish. Bison kills occur in Late Prehistoric stages and may indicate either a change in exploitative strategies or a more plentiful supply of bison. 41DL252 does contain bison size bone, but no analysis has been performed to determine the species. No direct evidence for horticulture or agriculture has been found within the project area. The pollen analysis of samples from Northlake Site did not indicate the presence of corn. As stated, Morgan[1975] concluded that the hunting of small game and the gathering of aquatic resources constituted the major subsistence activities throughout the site's occupation. Among the native floral resources that could have been exploited in the project area are the acorn, pecan, and hickory nut crops of the Eastern Cross Timbers and river bottomland forests. McCormick et al. [1975] presented a list of other edible plants in the vicinity of the Balsam Gourd Site and which would be found within or near the project area. Research is also still needed to understand the settlement patterns within this area. A few specific activities sites have been recognized. These include quarry sites such as 41DL25 and the twelve sites recorded between Prairie Creek and White Rock Creek [Hatzenbuehler 1942a]. Broken cobbles and cqres are reported from these sites, but no finished tools were found. Bison kill sites, such as excavated by Morris and Morris [1970], have alre,ady been discussed. Many sites appear to have been large camps that were occasionally reoccupied throughout much of the prehistoric period. These include Wood site (41DL76), 41DL69, and 41TR70. Small camp sites, generally located on the floodplain near the Trinity or Elm Fork, have also been recorded. These are primarily composed of mussel shell, burned rock, and few tools and seem to represent single occupations. Procurement and processing of freshwater mussel may have been a cornmon subsistence activity at these sites [Hays et al. 1972]. X41DL01 is an example of this type of site. 136


---

## Page 141

Although more permanent camps or villages may be expected during the Late Prehistoric stages, no direct evidence for villages has been found within our project area. However, the Wylie Focus sites on the nearby East Fork could be interpreted as villages. Evidence of burials has been reported from several sites within and near the project area. Upstream from the project area, at least seven burials have been reported from site 41DL83, which is located below the White Rock Lake Spillway [Kirkland and Harris 1941; Hatzenbuehler and Harris 1949; Harris 1949b]. Grave goods associated with the burials at 41DL83 include several turtle shell beads and stone beads, which were found arranged in a way that suggested they had been used as a necklace. Within the project area burials have been reported at 41DL35 which is situated on the Elm Fork Golf Course [Hays et al. 1972; skinner et al. 1978]. Additionally, a human skull was recovered at the Wheeler Site [Crook 1952] and an isolated human skeleton was discovered by Albritton and Patillo [1940] at 41DL27 about 0.3 miles south of the Wheeler site. Fragments of human bone that showed indications of cremation were recovered at 41DL234. Considering the nature of the data, it is not surprising that little is known about intrasite activities within our project area. With few sites tested and only one excavated, hearths and burials are almost the only features even recorded. In conclusion, the need for more research for all stages of prehistory in the Upper Trinity Basin is apparent. Due to the nature of the previous investigations, our understanding of the region is based almost entirely on lithic and ceramic artifacts. "The occurrence of out-of-context artifacts or the size of archaeological sites is not a useful measure of archaeological significance" [Skinner et al. 1978:29]. Systematic investigations, accurate recording of finds, and defined research goals are needed. Although without doubt more sites will be found along the Trinity and its tributaries, sites are rapidly being destroyed. It is disturbing to note the number of known sites that are either destroyed or highly disturbed by construction or quarrying. So much remains to be learned that we must try to recover what data we can using controlled methods before these resources are lost forever. Descriptions of all the known prehistoric sites in our project area are available in Appendix B. Historic sites and historic components of sites will be dealt with in a later section. Locations of these sites are shown in Figure 5.8a,b,c. Table 5.3 summarizes site information. 137

## Page 149

cultural Ecology of the Upper Trinity Basin Perspectives for cultural Resources Management Archaeological research in the Upper Trinity Basin will undoubtedly take a different course than that associated with most large-scale projects. The permit area-by-permit area consideration of known and predicted archaeological sites will challenge cultural resources managers to implement consistent methodologies for site evaluation and if necessary site study. These must be linked by methods and theoretical perspectives that will in the long run ensure that the collective efforts in the project area can make long-term contributions to regional history. The long-term case-by-case nature of anticipated efforts in the area highlights the importance of assessing intrasite characteristics as they relate to intersite and regional characteristics of theoretical and regional concern. A perspective that defines research objectives suitable for determining archaeological site significance is provided by cultural ecology and the study of culture change. The fundamentally diachronic aspects of archaeology [Plog 1977] highlight the opportunities that prehistoric archaeology may contribute to an explanation of long-term culture change, and in particular culture change in respon"se to environmental change. Additionally, perspectives provided by geoarchaeology [Butzer 1982] indicate the importance of ascertaining individual site histories within a project area as they relate to the fundamental character of archaeological variability. Therefore, site formation processes, including depositional and post-depositional geologic histories are integral in the determination of site significance and in the subsequent analysis of archaeological variability within and between sites. cultural ecology is the broad sub-discipline within anthropology that investigates the relationships between cultures and their biotic-cultural-physical environment. . Key objectives of cultural ecology include the definition of rates and kinds of change over time under conditions ranging from stable resource availabilities to dramatically changing resource availabilities. certain kinds of change must be addressed in the course of cultural ecologic studies. These include changing populations sizes and structures, changing demographic arrangements, differences in the division of labor or the scheduling of resource procurement activities, changing procurement and processing technologies, etc. All of these are areas of potential archaeological investigation, and all must be addressed in terms of "culture change" as a generic umbrella to these specific inquiries. An evolutionary perspective is necessary in 147


---

## Page 150

such studies because diachronic patterns of variability in cultures are fundamental to synchronic adaptive strategies. The appeal to evolutionary principles for investigating cultural ecological change is therefore a natural theoretical direction for archaeology to take. It is important to point out that much culture history remains to be accomplished in the North Texas region. Much previous work has focused on culture history with efforts directed towards identifying the "cultures" that are represented by the assemblages at specific sites. The methods of cultural ecology do not avoid the goals of culture history. On the contrary, by studying culture change it is virtually essential that detailed descriptions of technological and stylistic variation within and between assemblages be accumulated in the course of investigations. Culture ecology therefore provides a framework that places culture history in perspective and within a much broader theoretical vantage point. The analysis of culture change under stable environmental conditions provides one focus for cultural ecologic studies. Another is the study of systems under changing environmental conditions. The analysis of change under either case entails consideration of key dimensions of cultural systems, which should be investigated with respect to environmental conditions (Table 5.4). Population size, differentiation and integration within and between population units, and energy are the key dimensions defined by Plog [1977]. These have distinct behavioral correlates [Ferring and Peter 1982] and distinct archaeological . correlates with respect to intersite and intrasite variability. These archaeological correlates, then, are used to ultimately comment on the target analytical dimensions that link the cultural ecologic perspectives to cultural resources management, because these correlates define characteristics. For example, any cultural evolutionary study must consider population size and demographic arrangements as fundamental variables in the system. Both intersite and intrasite archaeological correlates have been defined. On an intersite level, the number of sites is the primary correlate. Archaeologists have realized for years that ascertaining the "number of sites" pertaining to any given period or to any given cultural unit is among our most elusive objectives. On the other hand, intrasite correlates for population are more manageable. Site size, artifact densities, and spatial patterning all comment on the patterns with which the archaeological site was used. In a diachronic setting, changing sizes of sites, changing artifact densities, changing spatial relationships among the clusters comment not only on the estimated population size of the group usi~~ the sites, but also the apparent intensity and periodicity with which the site was occupied. 148


---

## Page 151

Table 5.4 SYSTEMS DIMENSIONS AND ARCHAEOLOGICAL CORRELATES Analytical Behavioral Correlate Archaeological Correlate Dimension Intersite Intrasite Population -None- Number of Sites Site Size; Artifact Densities Spatial Patterning Functional DemographYiSched Functional Var Activity Segregation ...... ## Page 152

When complemented with evidence of functional differentiation and social integration, intrasite analysis, which comments on population size and demographic arrangements, may be realized. These archaeological correlates, therefore, define criteria that may be used in the ascertainment of individual site significance. Later, the link between the systems analysis and geoarchaeology will be well illustrated by using artifact densities and spatial patterning as characteristics of prehistoric sites, which are created within the archaeological record, only through the interplay between cultural and natural (geologic) factors. other analytical dimensions such as functional differentiation and social integration have archaeological correlates at the intersite and intrasite level. Functional differentiation and energy use, for instance, have as intrasite correlates evidence for spatial associations among tools and features and the floral and faunal remains for occupation units. In a cultural resources management scenario, the significance of a specific site may then be better evaluated in terms of the degree to which evidence for functional differentiation and .energy are preserved within a site. It should be clear that archaeologists have assessed "significance" with many of these archaeological correlates in mind. .The presentation here simply puts these correlates in a structure that is formally linked to behavioral correlates and the primary analytical dimensions indicated (Table 5.4). 150
