## Page 153

SECTION VI HISTORICAL ARCHAEOLOGY by Nancy G. Reese, Cecily A. Pegues and Bonnie C. Yates Introduction This research has met several objectives. First, it provides a list of previously documented historical cultural resources sites in the study area. It further assesses the kind and number of such sites, as well as the potential for locating them via archaeological survey in the future. Lastly, substantive recommendations to the study of history and historical archaeology in the project area are given. The project area is characterized by minimal research in historical archaeology. Most cultural resources sites in the study area have been identified during sponsored (academic or governmental) surveys, and their condition has not been verified by physical inspection in recent years. Others surmised locations derived from archival sources (e.g., Sam Street's maps). Exceptions to this situation include a survey of the Dallas Floodway Extension [Bennett et al. 1981] and ongoing archaeological investigations at Ray Roberts and Joe Pool reservoirs, each of which has added substantially to our knowledge of historic archaeological sites. Numerous sites at Ray Roberts show promise of providing important data concerning nineteenth and early twentieth century patterns of land use, economic-trade development, and social patterns in a largely rural setting [Skinner et al. 1982b]. Archaeological survey [Skinner and Conners 1979] and test excavations [Raab et al. 1982b] at Joe Pool Reservoir documented a series of sites ranging in age from the 1850s to the first quarter of this century. These sites exhibit a range of ethnic sources of occupants, functional diversity based on site setting as well as economic orientation of the owners-tenants; evidence of internal structural-artifactual variation exemplifying differential patterns of land tenureshir economir change and response to local socio-econOML~ eVolution has also been documented. These studies offer guides for developing longer-range plans concerning the significance of as yet undefined cultural resources sites in the project area and methods for locatinq ~nd evaluating potential resource sites. 152


---

## Page 154

Methods During the course of collecting data for the Trinity River Project, research was conducted at the following institutions: the Texas state Archives, Austin; the Barker Texas History Center at The University of Texas at Austin; the General Land Office in Austin; the Austin Public Library; the collections of the Texas Archaeological Research Laboratory (TARL) in Austin; the Willis Library at North Texas state University in Denton; the Texas History Collection at the Dallas Public Library; the Texas Collection at southern Methodist University; the Archives of the Dallas County Historical Society at the Hall of State, Fair Park in Dallas; and the library of Texas Womens University in Denton. The collections of these institutions were scanned for relevant data pertaining to the three-county region (Dallas, Denton, and Tarrant), and more particularly for information relating to the Trinity River Floodplain in these counties. This information was recorded and is presented in various forms throughout this report. In addition to extensive library search, informal interviews were conducted with the following individuals: Marty Craddock of the Tarrant County Historic Preservation Council; Duane Gage of Tarrant County Historical Commission; John Crain of the Dallas County Historical Society; A.C. Greene, Dallas historian; Yvonne Jenkins, Director of the Museum on the Square, Denton County Historical Society; and Bullitt Lowry and Dale Odom, History Faculty, North Texas State University, Denton. All these individuals shared generously of their time and expertise. After these data were compiled, historic archaeological sites (identified by the trinomial system of TARL, e.g., 41DL234) and potential historic localities were plotted on maps, and a survey crew attempted to ground check a number of locations. Although a great many of the potential localities are currently under Interstate-35, downtown Dallas, or commercial parks, the crew managed to locate a few, and where possible, notes were made and photographs were taken. The crew also made note of those locations that appeared to be relatively undisturbed, where the potential integrity of sites might be intact. Historic Overview "Called Daycoa by some Indians, Arkikosa by others, in 1690 Alonso de Leon, a Mexican-born officer of the Spanish crown, bestowed its modern name: La Santisima Trinidad. The Most Holy Trinity." [Greene 1973:3] 153


---

## Page 155

The region around the three forks of the Trinity River can be considered historically to be a relative newcomer as it does not have the long historical record that some portions of Texas do. The first European to visit the area might have been Luis de Moscoso, leading the survivors of Hernando De Soto's expedition. They were attempting to reach Mexico after De Soto's death on the Mississippi River in 1541 and may have crossed the Upper Trinity [Richardson 1963]. There is some indication that trading activities took place between the French, Spanish, Americans, and the Indian groups in the area. But other than this, scant notice was taken of the area, and little documentary material is available. Indian groups in the area around 1800 consisted of the Caddo, Kickapoo, Kichai, Shawnee, and Wichita. Several groups including the Wichitas had entered the region from other parts of the United States in the l700s [Newcomb 1961]. Around the l830s, skirmishes began to take place between the Indian groups in the region and Anglo settlers from other parts of the state. One of these incidents took place at the Indian village on Village Creek in present day Tarrant County. In 1838, the village, then occupied by members of the Caddo tribe, was attacked by Thomas J. Rusk leading a troop of volunteer rangers. The village was destroyed, but the same site was later reoccupied by a group of Cherokee Indians who had moved into the area from present day Cherokee County. After a period of quiet, the Indians began raiding, and a force led by General Tarrant attacked the village in 1841. At the time of the attack, the village was reported as being large, with about 225 lodges with extensive areas under cultivation [Strickland 1937]. Shortly after the raid on Village Creek, efforts were made to disband all the Indians in the Upper Trinity at which time they were forced to leave permanently. The area of the three forks, variously under the dominion of France or Spain became part of the Republic of Mexico in 1821. Americans were encouraged to settle in Texas because of both Stephen F. Austin's success at colonization, and the relaxing of immigration and land policies by the new Mexican government. The first American exploration of the Upper Trinity took place after the Republic of Texas was born, when the trader Dr. Henry Connelly went through the area on his way to present day Clarksville in 1839. Shortly thereafter, Bird's Fort was established and is commonly considered the first settlement in the area. Bird's Fort was established in 1840 by Colonel Jonathan Bird and a company of volunteer rangers. According to Webb [1952], the outpost consisted of a "blockhouse fort and several houses, three of which were within a stockade." While Colonel Bird and the rangers were relieved of their duties soon after the outpost 154


---

## Page 156

was completed, others moved in to replace them and the fort was not abandoned for about 10 years. other settlements were beginning to take place in what was to become Dallas County. Sometime around 1839, John Neely Bryan began exploring the region around the three forks. Bryan, gifted with a kind of insight, was the first to realize the potential of the location he had selected. He returned to Fort Smith, Arkansas and came back 2 years later prepared to stay [Greene 1973]. Bryan was helped in his endeavor by the Texas Land and Emigration Company, who initiated what was to become known as the Peters Colony. The young Republic of Texas was at that time eager to encourage immigration into its territories. The Texas government entered into a number of contracts with companies who were pledged to settle a certain number of families in specific areas. The companies would in turn receive compensation, often in the form of a percentage of land, for their troubles. Peters Company was one such organization and in four separate contracts, between 1841 and 1843, agreed to colonize a very large portion of north central Texas (including the entire project area) [Connor 1959]. The agreement specified that all heads of colony families would receive 640 acres and all single men would receive 320. Many problems arose between the company agents and the colonists, but the Peters Colony had an undeniably profound effect on the Upper Trinity. By 1850, when the Company had discharged its contractual obligations it had settled a total of 1388 families [Connor 1959]. Although John Neely Bryan had come to the area independently, he did receive land under the colony contract. And it was from this land that the city of Dallas was born. It is generally accepted that the county, when created in 1846, was named after the then vice-president of the United State George M. Dallas [Webb 1952]. Bryan was joined in his settlement by the Beemans and other families. Additional settlements began to grow up around Cedar springs, Farmers Branch, and Hord's Ridge (which would become oak Cliff), and in 1850 Dallas was elected the county seat. Tarrant County, named for General E. H. Tarrant who led the battle against the Cherokees at Village Creek, was created in 1849. Up to that time, very little settlement had taken place in the area. Birdville, named in honor of Captain Bird, was the largest settlement and was made county seat in 1850. Fort worth was established in 1849 as a military post. It was, however, abandoned by the military in 1853 because of the decrease in hostilities with the local Indians. A settlement had grown up around it, and by 1860 the county seat had been transferred 155


---

## Page 157

there. Denton County was also created around this same period. Named in honor of John B. Denton, the county was initially surveyed in 1849 [Webb 1952]. Pickneyville, southeast of present day Denton, was named the county seat. The county seat changed several times before Denton was decided upon in 1856. Lewisville, the closest town in the county to the project area, is located on the headright grants of John and Augustus King who came to the area in 1844. The settlement was first called Holfords Prairie. Sold in 1855 to Basdeal Lewis, the town was laid out and called Lewisville that same year. The 1850s were a time of great change throughout the entire Upper Trinity region. What had recently been wilderness was quickly becoming civilization. Transportation networks were improved, rough trails were being shaped into roads. Many of the ferrys listed as historic localities date to this period, including Keenan Crossing, California Crossing, Record and Cedar Springs crossings [Cochran 1928]. In 1854, Alexander Cockrell built the first bridge spanning the Trinity and connecting east and west Dallas. The Ft. Worth to Yuma stageline began operations in 1856, and by 1858 several more were in business. Trinity City (41DLI05) reportedly dates from the 1850s; however, no record of a plat for the town exists. Some have speculated that this was a city in name only and that it was created as a promotional gimmick by the Peters Colony representative to encourage settlement in the area [Bennett et al. 1981]. "In 1856, the city was reported as having six stores, one hotel, a wagon yard, two blacksmiths, several houses, and the Tellico Manufacturing Company which consisted of a cotton gin, wool works, lumber and grist mills" [Bennett et al. 1981:41]. It is indeed surprising, if all this did exist, to find no trace of it. Commercial enterprise improved. Mills and gins were installed up and down the Trinity at places such as Eagle Ford, Trinity Mills, Record Crossing, and Randol Mill. of all these, Randol Mill is the only one for which archaeological traces have been confirmed. Randol Mill was initially known as Leonard's Mill when it was erected in 1856. It soon became the center of a small community. Burned in 1860, the mill was rebuilt by 1862 and became the commercial center of Tarrant County during the Civil War [Dunnett 1979]. After changing hands several times, the mill came into the possession of R. A. "Bob" Randol in 1872. The mill was in operation from that time until the death of Bob Randol in 1922. The mill structure itself burned in 1933, and the community 156


---

## Page 158

disappeared [Dunnett 1979].' All that is visible today are the foundation walls (Figure 6.1) of the mill and some pieces of abandoned equipment (Figure 6.2). Another venture that was initiated during the l850s was the French socialist community of La Reunion, founded by victor Considerant and Francois Cantagrel. Considerant purchased land along the bluffs of the Trinity in the vicinity of present day Oak Cliff for the colony (Figure 6.3). Shortly afterward, colonists began to arrive; in all about 300 would come [Webb 1952]. Almost from the beginning, they started having problems. The soil was poor and unsuited for farming. Very few of the colonists were farmers and disputes erupted about labor allocations. Considerant, enthralled with the idea of making La Reunion the center of a large network of colonies, purchased property in other locations throughout the state. This put a financial strain on the mother colony that was already floundering [Hammond 1936]. By 1857, the colony was essentially defunct. Some of the colonists returned to Europe, some went to New Orleans, and some went to Dallas, where "the French people of La Reunion, urbane and cosmopolitan, were influential in neutralizing the rough edges of the rugged frontier town" [Urbanovsky 1972:22]. This might in part account for the great change in occupations as listed in the 1850 and 1860 Census records (Tables 6.1 and 6.2). The l850s also saw the first large-scale attempt to navigate the Trinity. Many thought the Trinity the most navigable stream in Texas. There had been some success with flatboats on the river, but in 1852 the "Dallas" became the first of a long line of ships to sink in the Trinity. The "Dallas," enroute to the coast, took 3 months to reach Porter's Bluff near present day Corsicana, where it was forced to turn around due to low water. It hit a snag and sank on the return trip [Greene 1973]. By studying population statistics, the steady growth of the project area becomes apparent (Table 6.3). Many of the incoming immigrants were from southern states, and it is not surprising that there was much support for the Confederacy during the Civil War. Dallas County became a supply center for southern armies, supplying flour and clothing to the Confederate cause. In general, however, there was much less impact from both the War and reconstruction than might have been expected. After the war, interest was once again revived in the Navigation issue. Part of the reason was that there was no competition for wagon freight rates [Bennett et al. 1981], still the chief means of transporting goods to market. Both the "Job 157

## Page 165

Boat #1" and the "Sallie Haynes," a wide sternwheeler, attempted the river in the late 1860s and achieved moderate success. The "Sallie Haynes" later sank somewhere just south of Dallas. The 1870s brought a new era of increased prosperity into the Upper Trinity region. A steady stream of settlers entered the area. Both Dallas and Ft. Worth began taking on something of the character they have today. Dallas was quickly becoming a mercantile center. Buffalo were being rapidly destroyed for their hides, and Dallas became the chief distributing center for this product [Bennett et al. 1981]. Cotton production was also on the increase, with Dallas acting as a marketing center. In Ft. Worth and Tarrant County, however, things were heading in a different direction. The Chisholm Trail, famous for its cattle drives since 1867, came through Ft. Worth heading north to Kansas slaughterhouses. Ft. Worth quickly became an outfitting point for the drovers. Merchants located there provided the cowboys with both supplies and entertainment. "At Ft. Worth, where hogs still roamed the streets, cowboys primed with firewater could shoot up the town on Saturday night without much fear of penalty" [Gard 1956]. This was a situation the townspeople soon found intolerable. They petitioned for and passed new laws prohibiting this kind of activity. These early laws were the start of civic groups and clubs that would eventually come to turn Ft. worth into one of the cultural centers of the region. Ft. Worth, however, would never lose it label of "Cowtown." One factor that was destined to have a tremendous impact on the region was the advent of the railroads. The state needed more people, and a source of better communication, transportation, and product conveyances. There was not enough private enterprise to finance the building of railroads, and the state could not afford to provide monetary aid. Texas had an abundance of land that was difficult to sell. Land grants to railroad companies, seemed to be the answer. In 1852, the legislature offered eight sections of land per mile of track laid [Branda 1976]. No great results came from this policy. A general law was enacted in 1854 to double the land grant and to lower the requirements. The law was changed many times until it ~as finally repealed in 1882 [Reed 1946]. Railroad lines in East Texas tripled between 1870 and 1880 (Figure 6.4). The Houston and Texas Central came through Dallas in 1872 [Acheson 1977] and by 1877 was part of a completed track from Galveston to Chicago. To ensure the east-west line of the Texas and Pacific, Dallas secured state legislation and also offered land and bonds. "The effect would jump the population from 1500 to more than 7000 in a matter of months" [Acheson 1977]. 164


---

## Page 166

## Page 167

Fort Worth grew in anticipation of the arrival of the Texas and Pacific but was to have an unexpectedly long wait. The Texas and Pacific had worked its way west from Dallas to Eagle Ford when the New York stock Exchange closed for 10 days in 1873. Railroad construction was halted. Eagle Ford became a thriving terminus town, but the ambitions of Fort Worth pressed for a different outcome. "A plan was devised and the town was plunged into the most dramatic concerted effort of its history" [Sanders 1973]. citizens literally built the last few miles of track as the state's land grant agreement with the railroad was about to be voided in 1876. Towns in the project area which developed between Dallas and Denton along the Dallas Wichita Railroad are Letot, Farmers Branch, Carrollton, Trinity Mills, and Lewisville. Hutchins lies on the Houston and Texas Central line south from Dallas. Towns between Dallas and Fort Worth along the Texas and Pacific line are Eagle Ford and Grand Prairie. The development of this area is graphically depicted on a series of archival maps annotated in Appendix C. During the 1880s, tenant farming became a common practice throughout the project area. The principle cash crop was still cotton, with corn and wheat being secondary crops. The tenant usually provided the landowner with one-third of the grain and one-fourth of the cotton grown during the season. The frequency of tenant farms can be graphically seen on the Sam Street maps for Dallas and Tarrant counties (Figures 6.5 and 6.6). This situation was amplified during the l890s by a depression. Many former landowners faced ruin and were themselves forced into tenancy. This situation also forced many into the cities, and Dallas soon became the largest one in Texas. Interest in navigation, which had taken a backseat after the arrival of the railroads, increased. The Steamboat "H.A. Harvey" arrived in Dallas in 1893. The ship shuttled freight and became an excursion boat. The populace soon became disenchanted, however, and the "Harvey" was sold in 1898 [Bennett et al. 1981]. The last all out effort for Trinity River navigation came after the turn of the century, when the Corps of Engineers began building the system of locks and dams on the river. One of these, Lock and Dam Number 1, is in the project area. Construction began on Lock and Dam Number 1 in 1905. The Lock and Dam became a popular gathering point for Dallas residents [Bennett et al. 1981]. According to photo records (Figure 6.7), a number of buildings were constructed at the site to accommodate the workers. A major flood, however, occured in 166

This area is located in the Las Colinas corporate center and was not examined by the survey crew because it is currently fenced off. 7. California Crossing. This crossing was given its name because of the many people who crossed the Trinity here on the way to California during the gold rush of 1849. It was not visited by the survey crew, but according to Greene [1985] is located where Lombardy Lane crosses the Trinity. 8. possible Ferry Location. The survey crew attempted to locate this site; but because of the massive amount of build-up in the area, the location on the project map must be considered tentative. 9. possible Ferry Location. This is the tentative location of Record's Crossing [Cochran 1928]. According to Greene [1985], Col. Record also owned a water mill that was located at the same ford. The crew conducted a "wind shield survey" through the area of this early settlement and noted numerous shot gun-type houses from a later period, but primarily light and heavy industries now dominate the area. See discussion in Historic Overview. 11. 41DL220. This is an area of packed clay with what appears to be a well dug into it. There were no artifacts associated with this location. This site was not visited by the survey crew. 12. Miller's Bridge. Sources indicated that there was originally a ferry crossing in this location, established by Wm. B. This site located on the first terrace above the Trinity river consists of a mixed scatter of prehistoric and historic material. The historic artifacts appear to date from the mid to late nineteenth century. There was no structure associated with this debris. The survey crew did not look for this site. 14. 41DL206. This site located on the edge of a gravel pit consisted of a very light scatter of late nineteenth century 173

artifacts. The site was originally located in 1981; when an attempt was made to relocate it sometime later, artifacts were no longer visible. The Trinity Project survey crew did not attempt to locate this site. 15. 41DL208. This site is a board and batten house that was converted into a barn, the foundation is made of logs. This structure was listed as being very deteriorated in 1981 when it was originally located. A light scatter of clear glass was associated with the building. According to the survey form, the structure has been placed on concrete blocks. There were no artifacts associated with this structure. No attempt was made by the survey crew to visit this site. 17. Lock and Dam Number 1. This is the location of Lock and Dam Number 1 constructed in 1905. It was greatly damaged by the flood of 1908, and rebuilt in 1909. Archival photographs of the site were examined by the crew at the Hall of state. A number of structures in the immediate vicinity were apparent from the photographs (Figure 6.9). 18. 41DLI05. This is the assumed location of Trinity City. There is some speculation on the part of scholars that this is a city in name only, that never actually existed (see discussion of Trinity City in Historic Overview). The site, however, if it ever existed, was reported as being destroyed when revisited by a crew from SMU in 1978. 19. 41DL92. This site has both historic and prehistoric components. The historic component includes the structural remains of a government checking station for boats leaving Ennis and traveling upstream. If this information is correct, Lock and Dam Number 1, located to the north, is undoubtedly connected with 41DL92 in some respect. The prehistoric crew relocated this expansive site area; there appears to be some potential for further investigation since the large area is relatively undeveloped private property. 20. 41DL216. He also indicated that both of the houses were sheathed in stone about 1930. These houses were built by Mr. Bradley, who according to the informant was one of the first boat wrights in the county. The sites were photographed by the survey crew (Figure 6.10). Mr. Alexander, born in 1898, is a long-time resident of the area 174

All that remains of the mill are the retaining wall, iron shaft, and the circular turbine wheel. A state historic marker is located at this site. Unfortunately, landfill operations have been taking place there for some time, and any remnants of the community surrounding the mill are likely destroyed. The debris from the land fill comes up to the base of the Historical Marker. The bibliography provides some excellent sources that are more complete and scholarly. It is strongly recommended that these sources be studied for detailed information on specific areas when the need arises. The nature of this project did not allow time for a complete study of each of these properties and so guidelines for further work are herein included. ## Page 181

·SECTION VII CULTURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT by C. Reid Ferring Objectives The overall purpose of this project was to assist in the preparation of an environmental impact statement for the archaeological and historical resources within the project area. Towards this end, we have identified known sites and have provided information in several sections of this report that may be used in order to predict the potential for archaeological site occurrence in areas for which no sites are known at present. An additional charge in this task was to provide the rationale for assessing the significance of individual sites with respect to the status of regional investigations and with respect to contemporary methods and theories in archaeology. This section of the report will describe the known prehistoric and historic sites in terms of their estimated age and character. Included are recommendations for specific sites with regards to future work, given the potential for adverse impacts to the sites in the course of project implementations. Prehistoric Sites in the Project Area In the previous section of this report some 90 prehistoric sites were described as known in the project area. These exhibit a remarkable diversity of character, probable ages and cultural affiliation and degree of preservation (Table 5.2). A number of these sites are known to have been destroyed by landuse activities or natural causes and are therefore not amenable to future archaeological investigations. This only enhances the potential significance of the remaining sites for which further work may be entertained in the future. Overall, our appraisal of the character and condition of these archaeological sites has been remarkably conditioned by the near absence of systematic archaeological investigations at these sites. Only one site has been "excavated," and that study was of quite limited character. A small number of sites have received minimal test excavations, the results of which are only scantily evident in publications. The condition of many of the archaeological sites may be partly inferred by the methods and conditions under which the sites were discovered (Table 5.2). A large number of sites were found in the 1940s and 1950s by avocational archaeologists in situations that describe relatively minimal disturbance to these 183


---

## Page 182

sites. These include what appear to be quite natural contexts with no evidence of landuse activities or minimal impacts as a result of agricultural activities. A significant number of sites however have been found as a result of sand and gravel quarrying operations or other excavation activities resulting in deep disturbance to the geologic deposits containing the sites. Our investigations at a number of these localities have made it quite clear that while the deep subsurface disturbances, which revealed the archaeological site, have certainly removed portions of that site, the remaining intact areas of archaeological occurrence are in many cases amenable to controlled investigation. We wish to emphasize that even though a very small portion of an archaeological site may be preserved at the edge of a sand or gravel pit, this small but nonetheless intact portion of the archaeological site in many cases appears to be of greatly enhanced potential significance. At stratified sites or very well preserved single component sites, even a small excavation block or series of controlled test pit units can provide invaluable information on the content and character of the archaeological site. Therefore, the fact that what appears to have been a near complete "destruction" of a site by past landuse activities cannot be taken as evidence that no further work at a site would be productive and that the information recovered could not make significant contributions to regional archaeological investigations. An essential criterion, which must be sought in evaluating sites that have been partially impacted by landuse activities, is whether or not the context of the site may be established. A vertical section in the wall of a sand pit exposing stratigraphy and preserving an intact portion of the archaeological site offers the opportunity to place the site into a stratigraphic, paleoenvironmental, and chronologie framework. Even a small excavation block in some situations can result in the recovery of adequate samples of artifacts and faunas, permitting the assessment of on site activities and subsistence practices. Therefore, careful attention must be given to such small, but nonetheless intact, portions of sites in the project area. The presence of a sand or gravel pit therefore cannot be taken as evidence that all archaeological potentials in that area are lost. Quite the contrary, these may signify some of the more potentially significant archaeological localities in the project area. This perspective is implicit in the recommendations made on a site by site basis (Table 5.2). 184


---

## Page 183

Site Prediction The prediction of archaeological sites within the project area is a necessary component of the cultural resources management strategy proposed herein. In the future, cultural resources managers will be required to make decisions concerning necessary actions in areas of permit applications. In many cases, no known archaeological sites will be recorded in these areas, and decisions will have to be made concerning whether or not archaeological surveys for sites are necessary. As described later, this decision making process will involve consideration not only of the potential for archaeological site occurrence, but also for the potential for archaeological site preservation and detectability. Towards this end, the considerations surrounding the prediction of prehistoric and historic sites are considered here. Prehistoric Site Prediction The management of cultural resources within the Trinity Basin will require prediction of hitherto undiscovered archaeological sites. This task is a multifaceted one and perhaps one of the most difficult within cultural resources management. For our purposes, a multidimensional approach is proposed in order to assist in site prediction. This approach is guided by the recognition of three basic processes involved in the genesis of an archaeological record. These include site formation, site preservation, and site detectability. These three processes are in turn controlled by three sets of factors: natural, cultural, and landuse factors. A matrix of these processes and factors (Table 7.1) helps illustrate the relationships among these dimensions. Before discussing this approach, it should be stated that our site prediction methods are not overly quantitative. This represents a hopefully realistic appraisal of the complexities involved in predicting sites that have not been discovered. It may be tempting and perhaps self-fulfilling to apply overly quantitative methods to archaeological data. Yet the interactions of geologic factors in the construction and modification of sites and the importance of both natural factors and present landuse variables in terms of differential site preservation/detectability are such that quantification of site prediction is perhaps attractive but in our opinion not necessarily realistic. The approach that we embrace, therefore, represents our opinion concerning how the potential for the presence and condition of an archaeological site may be best evaluated using available information concerning regional patterns of archaeological site occurrence, the geologic 185


---

## Page 184

Table 7.1 PRCCESSES AND FACTORS IN SITE PREDICTION FACTORS NATURAL CULTURAL LANDUSE SITE FORrJ'JATION Construction Sedi.mentary environment, OCcupation intensity, sedimentation rate on-site activities Ivlodification Bioturbation, weathering, Occupation periodicity, I-' erosion, scavenging reoccupation frequency- 00 '" SITE PRESERVATION Burial depth, quarrying, charmel Cutbank. erosion, slope ITOdification, erosion, intense constructions, roads, weathering pipelines, agriculture, etc. SITE DETECTABILITY Burial depth, cutbank. Artifact, feature density quarrying, construction, exposures, rodent etc. disturbance


---

## Page 185

variables involved in site formation and site preservation, and the different factors involved in our ability to detect an archaeological site, if indeed it were present. For this project, our method is oriented towards the evaluation of a specific piece of land, in this case, a piece of land for which permit application has been made. By consulting the available information concerning known sites and then by consulting the available information concerning the geologic history of the permit area, and the present and past landuse of the permit area, a reasonable decision may be made at that point concerning the potential for an archaeological site being preserved there and the potential for the archaeological site being detectable by surface or subsurface methods. Implicit in this approach is encouragement for decision-making in this process of predicting archaeological sites. To have generated a map showing differential probabilities for archaeological site occurrences would, in our opinion, have resulted in a high potential for misguided efforts as cultural resources managers make determinations concerning the necessity of archaeological investigations in a permit area. On a case-by-case basis, we feel that the general approach and the specific methodology outlined in this report will prove to be the most economical and the most accurate means to plan and implement archaeological investigations in permit areas. site Formation The first and most obvious event that must take place for an archaeological site to be created is the concentration of past human activities in a specific location. These activities must result in the deposition of artifacts and/or features that may later be detectable by archaeologists. The issue of site formations processes has become increasingly evident in the archaeological literature [Butzer 1982; Schiffer 1978, 1983; Wood and Johnson 1978]. Much of this literature focuses on the geologic environments in which archaeological sites form and the ways in which specific sedimentary environments are related to the character and preservation potential of archaeological sites [Stein and Farrand 1985]. other efforts in site formation studies focus on the impacts of humans themselves during site formation [Schiffer 1978, 1983]. These studies have identified cultural processes that in effect modify archaeological sites as they are being constructed. These include trampling and reuse of artifacts, cleaning up of site refuse with redeposition of artifacts and debris in certain portions of sites and the introduction of sediment into archaeological sites by its inhabitants. Additional perspectives are provided by considering the natural agents of site disturbance and modification [Wood and 187


---

## Page 186

Johnson 1978; stein 1983J. Together these investigations identify the importance of recognizing archaeological materials in primary context within a site as opposed to those that have suffered differential modification within and between archaeological sites [Ferring in prepJ. From a cultural resources management point of view, the factor that is initially important is whether or not a site was indeed formed at sometime in the past in a particular location. However, in terms of ascertaining the significance of an archaeological site, concerns quickly shift to the character and relative detail of behavioral records contained within an archaeological site. In this sense, site formation processes are clearly germane to cultural resource management. Archaeological site formation has been divided into two separate domains: site construction and site modification [Ferring in prep]. Site construction involves the primary events that shaped the deposition of artifacts and features in a natural sedimentary matrix. While in certain cases human alteration of the landscape is the dominant geological factor during site construction (for example, within a Pueblo occupation), most prehistoric sites form in settings in which the addition of noncultural matrix to the site is accomplished by natural depositional factors. These commonly include alluviation from streams, deposition of sediments by slopewash (colluvium,) or eolian deposition. The importance of the noncultural site matrix may be appreciated by considering stratigraphic principles in relation to archaeological formation. Given a series of repeated occupations of the same locality, the density and modification potentials of the serially deposited artifacts and features would be conditioned almost entirely by the rate at which the naturally deposited site matrix accumulated (Figure 7.1). The more rapid the sedimentation rate, the more quickly artifacts and features are buried. With rapid sedimentation, therefore, the same sequence of occupations will result in sites with lower artifacts densities, better preservation of artifacts, better preservation of spatial patterning, and more stratigraphic superpositioning of the serial occupation debris. Rapid burial enhances the removal of artifacts and features from the zone of intense weathering, which in most environments is at the surface or shallow subsurface. Therefore, sites which formed in environments with rapid sedimentation rates not only exhibit more important archaeological records related to their con$tructional histories, but they also suffer less modifications effects from weathering, bioturbation, and erosion. 188


---

## Page 187

SEDIMENTATION RATE Rapid Slow I-' 00 \.D Q) Stone c::> Bone &- Deteriorated Bone Figure 7.1 Sedimentation Rates and Site Formation. Hypothetical sequence of occupations is contrasted between depositional environments with rapid and slow rates of sedimentation. Note differential stratification and differential preservation. With slow sedimentation, serial occupation clusters and features are juxtaposed on same surface and are prone to be interpreted as 'activity areas' within single occupation unit. With same occupation events and rapid sedimentation, these records are stratigraphically superimposed; this record is clearer, albeit with lower artifact densities. (from Ferring in prep)


---

## Page 188

To the degree that it is possible to geologically identify areas that have relatively rapid sedimentation rates as opposed to areas that were characterized by paleosurface stability, the archaeologist's ability to predict the characteristics of archaeological sites is enhanced [Ferring in prep]. For example, the sedimentary record of relatively rapid accumulation of alluvium in a levee or crevasse splay environment [Allen 1965] may be significantly different than for sites that formed contemporaneously in different parts of the same river basin that were characterized by slow alluviation rates (for example, floodbasin settings). In this regard, it is important to note that fluvial sedimentary environments are characterized by both spatial and temporal variations in sedimentary environments [Bridge and Leeder 1978; Allen 1965]. Within a fluvial basin therefore, the different sedimentary environments that are significant with respect to archaeolgoical site formation are expected to show spatial distributions and temporal variations such that any given location within that basin must be evaluated independently with respect to its potential site formation histories. From a cultural resources management point of view, the entire issue of site formation is related to the presence and character of specific archaeological sites. Only with reference to differential site modification and differential site construction histories, resulting in qualitatively different archaeological information, can the difficult task of ascertaining site significance be accomplished. In other words, an explicit geoarchaeological approach to the study of site formation processes is complementary to the goals of determining site significance and therefore to the goals of cultural resources management. The above discussion indicates the rationale for identifying natural factors and cultural factors in site formation (Table 7.1). The natural factors include the specific sedimentary environment, the sedimentation rate, as well as modification factors including bioturbation and weathering. On a project-wide scale, long-term change in these factors should be carefully considered. For instance, during particular climatic regimes, weathering rates may be enhanced regardless of sedimentation rate. Also quite distinct changes in fluvial sedimentary environments may characterize a region. For example, a shift from braided to meandering streams is documented for the Mississippi and Ouachita Rivers at the close of the Pleistocene [Fisk 1944; Saucier 1974]. Such changes in broad sedimentary regimes have clear implications for archaeological site construction and modification [Butzer 1977, 1982]. 190


---

## Page 189

cultural factors in site formation must also be considered in such an approach. The occupational intensity (the period of time during which a site was continuously occupied by a group), as well as the periodicity of site use (the interval of time between occupation episodes), and of course the total number of occupation episodes at a site, all will impact the archaeological record. Their occupation parameters condition the resultant density of artifacts and features, the potential for subsequent occupations to disturb the remains of prior occupations, and so on. By explicitly recognizing natural factors and cultural factors in site formation with respect to the construction and modification of archaeological sites, a better appraisal of variability within sites and between sites may be acquired. A better understanding of archaeological variability within a project area is certainly complimentary to the process of establishing site significance; for the latter is certainly a relative dimension of the archaeological record. site Preservation site preservation is in some ways related to site modification. For the present purposes, however, site preservation is taken as modification ranging from minimal disturbance/weathering to the virtual destruction of archaeological sites. Both natural factors and modern landuse factors are involved in site preservation (Table 7.1). The natural factors include such processes as meandering of streams with total removal of the archaeological matrix or slope erosion which may redeposit artifacts into entirely secondary spatial and geologic contexts. Landuse factors are well recognized as potential agents of site preservation. Some that are especially germane to the Trinity Basin study include sand and gravel quarrying, road construction, river channel modification, or excavations for building foundations. Incidentally, it should be pointed out that certain landuse activities may actually enhance site preservation. For example, filling activities that raise the floodplain 8 to 10 feet may actually provide a deeper burial position for an archaeological site that was shallowly buried prior to the landuse activity. Several cases where this may have happened are identified in the description of known sites in the project area. 191


---

## Page 190

site Detectability It is evident that the third and last component in an archaeological investigation involves the detectability of an archaeological site. Site formation and site preservation may result in well preserved important archaeological sites. These must be detectable for them to become part of the archaeological record. In the process of defining potential site detectability, it is necessary to consider natural, cultural, and landuse factors. Natural factors include the burial depth or the availability of cutbank exposures, or the nature of vegetation in an area of concern. cultural factors link detectability to site formation processes. It is clear that sites with higher artifact densities are more likely to be found in alluvial environments. Therefore, the nature of occupations and the nature of post-occupational modification of sites are related to their detectability in the archaeological record. Lastly, landuse factors play an important role in site detectability. As for natural factors, landuse may in fact provide the means by which archaeological sites are found. Many sites in the Trinity Basin have been found only after they were partially destroyed by natural factors such as cutbank erosion or landuse factors including sand and gravel quarrying, road construction, or agricultural activities. The basic fact that sites must be partially destroyed before they may be discovered is evident when site detectability is considered. Therefore, certain landuse activities and certain potentially destructive natural factors are in other cases the very mechanisms which bring an archaeological site to our attention. Overall, consideration of site formation, preservation, and detectability and allusion to the natural, cultural, and landuse factors that control these processes can assist in the identification of archaeolgical site potentials in a specified permit area. These perspectives are used later in terms of a recommended program for evaluating the potential for archaeological site presence. This in effect is a "site prediction model." It is not overly quantified nor does it rank all of the land in the project area in terms of relative site potentials. Rather, it identifies and simplifies the factors that must be considered on a case-by-case basis to assess the need for archaeological investigations in a permit area. Historic Site Prediction The factors surrounding historic site prediction are radically different than those pertinent to prediction of prehistoric sites. The obvious differences between these periods 192


---

## Page 191

in terms of economic systems, land ownership, communications, and transportation networks, as well as age of sites, signify that site presence, site character, and site detectability should be treated in wholly different ways for historic period sites. Results of research at Joe Pool and Ray Roberts reservoirs suggest certain patterns of site presence and methods of site detection that can assist in evaluating (i.e., predicting) potential sites within a defined permit request area. As for prehistoric sites, there is a general relationship between site age and site preservation potentials. Older sites at Joe Pool and Ray Roberts were difficult to find even when tract histories or other archival data indicated their presence. In particular, Antebellum sites are rare in this area, despite the knowledge that settlements became increasingly common after the Peters Colony was established. Both site characteristics (common log structures with poor preservation potential) as well as site histories (i.e., adaptive reuse of sites settled early in the Antebellum period over subsequent periods) are involved in this pattern. It is clear that older historic sites will be more difficult to locate, yet they exhibit higher potential significance than the more common sites of later periods. For younger sites, dating to the post-Civil War through early twentieth century, Sam Street's maps are invaluable aids in Dallas and Tarrant Counties. At Joe Pool, the Dallas map proved to be extremely reliable, even in locating sites that had not been detected by initial ground survey. These maps clearly indicate a large number of primary residences, tenant houses as well as commercial- and transportation- related localities within the project area. Tract searching for areas on Street's maps showing sites can assist in determining the potential age and character of sites. In particular, tract records for Tarrant County carry detailed descriptions of the number and kind of structures present on a given tract, and when additions of structures were made. Ground checking should follow these inquiries when site potential is demonstrated. Landuse and Potential Impacts Site prediction and the distribution of known archaeological sites, which have not been visited as a part of this project, require that landuse activities be considered in terms of potential impacts to known and predicted archaeological sites. In Appendix A, landuse activities in the project area are described following the results of air photo analysis and ground truthing. The character of these impacts is diverse. They range from activities that would almost inevitably result in total site 193


---

## Page 192

destruction, such as deep lake excavation or deep foundation excavations. They also include activities that may cause surficial damage to sites, including paving or landscaping activities associated with development. Another type of landuse entails fill activities, such as levee construction or the raising of the floodplain, as seen in many parts of the project area. These latter activities may do no physical damage to archaeological sites, rather they would result in a change in sites accessibility. This must be considered an impact on the archaeological resources, insomuch as the deep burial of sites by land fill activities effectively will remove them from the area of possible archaeological investigation. On a case-by-case basis, the engineers' plans for project implementation will have to be evaluated with respect to potential impacts to the archaeological sites that are known or are predicted in the project area. All available evidence suggests that the project area will be the location of increasingly intensive development in the coming years. A major challenge to cultural resources managers will be to find the balance point between the impacts of an individual development in a context of cumulative impacts to archaeological resources in the project area. Assessment of Necessary Action in Permit Areas The following steps are recommended in order to make initial determinations with respect to archaeological actions in a specified permit area. This general methodology incorporates information from the documentations of known sites, the assessment of archaeological potentials for site presence, and the landuse analyses conducted as part of this project. The steps recommended in terms of initial decision making upon receipt of a permit application and definition of the nature of landuse activities in the permit area are as follows: 1. Define permit area on project base maps 2. Define the nature and extent of landuse activities in the permit area with respect to subsurface disturbance, depth of disturbance, fill activities 3. Consult list of known archaeological sites and make determinations following recommendations provided herein. 4. Consult geologic map of project area and define the geomorphic units present in the permit area. Consult geologic synthesis in terms of site potential . 194


---

## Page 193

SECTION VIII SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS The Trinity Basin Project has resulted in a comprehensive review of known archaeological sites within the study area. This has been accomplished as a result of literature reviews, informant interviews, searching of site files at state and local institutions, and field inspection. In addition, the geologic context for these sites have been appraised by a reconnaissance study of the Late Quaternary deposits of the Upper Trinity Basin. Together these provide a framework for interpreting the potential significance of these archaeological resources, as well as those resources that are at present undocumented but are predicted to occur in the study area. While many of the archaeological sites described in this report have already been so severely impacted by landuse activities or in fact have been completely destroyed by those activities, a great number of sites both known and predicted occur in the study area. The significance of these sites must be evaluated on a case-by-case basis, yet collectively, they comprise an extremely important aspect of the archaeological record of the North Texas area. It has only been in recent times that major archaeological efforts in this region have been made part of large-scale federal landuse activities. As a result, the archaeological record from this region is comprised mainly from limited investigations at a dispersed array of archaeological sites. A review of the past work in this region similarly indicates that from a point of view of culture history, and past environments, our framework for interpreting these sites is feeble. Therefore, the archaeological data and the contextual data contained at sites in the project area must be regarded as potentially significant for regional investigations. Recommendations Site-specific recommendations are contained in a previous section of this report. These identify sites for which no further work is deemed necessary owing to site destruction, and recommendations concerning necessary site visits in cases where no recent information for sites is available. On the basis of the present investigation, other sites have been recommended for testing. These test excavations are deemed necessary for evaluation of the sites with respect to state and national designations as landmarks or National Register sites, respectively. A small number of sites have been identified as 196


---

## Page 194

those which should be avoided or intensively investigated. Implicit in this category of recommended sites is the conclusion that adequate information is present to warrant nomination for state archaeological landmark and/or National Register of Historic Places designation. A general recommendation is inherent in this report with respect to the continued search for archaeological sites within the project area. Site prediction for the floodplain and lower terrace settings in the project area must be done on a location-specific basis, yet all available information presented in this report suggests that the general probabilities for additional sites within the project area are extremely high. Site detection at the same time is expected in many cases to be very difficult owing to recent patterns of alluviation. Careful consideration of .possible subsurface site detection techniques must be given to permit areas. These efforts may be facilitated by a more comprehensive geologic investigation, particularly of the Trinity floodplain. An additional area for which further work is recommended concerns the geologic and paleoenvironmental context for the archaeological sites in the Trinity Basin. With a more sound stratigraphic and chronologie framework for the Late Quaternary and Holocene deposits along the Trinity, a much better appraisal of site preservation potentials may be gained. This framework will assist both archaeological research goals and cultural resources management activities. The geologic and paleoenvironmental data contained within the project area are viewed here as a resource in themselves. without such a contextual framework, interpretation of archaeological variability and change within the project area will by definition be deficient. At some point, therefore, geologic and paleoenvironmental data within the project must be considered as resources to which adverse impacts must be addressed and if necessary, mitigated. It is recommended, therefore, that when major portions of the project area are to be impacted by construction activities that geologic and paleoenvironment data contained in the area be considered as resources and that impacts to these data be considered with respect to strategies of avoidance or mitigation. 197
